Then she ran down the path through the other door and then\r\ninto the orchard, and when she stood and looked up there was the tree\r\non the other side of the wall, and there was the robin just finishing\r\nhis song and beginning to preen his feathers with his beak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe walked round and looked closely at that side of the orchard wall,\r\nbut she only found what she had found before\xe2\x80\x94that there was no door in\r\nit. Then she ran through the kitchen-gardens again and out into the\r\nwalk outside the long ivy-covered wall, and she walked to the end of it\r\nand looked at it, but there was no door; and then she walked to the\r\nother end, looking again, but there was no door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very queer,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBen Weatherstaff said there was no door\r\nand there is no door. But there must have been one ten years ago,\r\nbecause Mr. Craven buried the key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis gave her so much to think of that she began to be quite interested\r\nand feel that she was not sorry that she had come to Misselthwaite\r\nManor. In India she had always felt hot and too languid to care much\r\nabout anything. The fact was that the fresh wind from the moor had\r\nbegun to blow the cobwebs out of her young brain and to waken her up a\r\nlittle.\r\n\r\nShe stayed out of doors nearly all day, and when she sat down to her\r\nsupper at night she felt hungry and drowsy and comfortable. She did not\r\nfeel cross when Martha chattered away. She felt as if she rather liked\r\nto hear her, and at last she thought she would ask her a question. She\r\nasked it after she had finished her supper and had sat down on the\r\nhearth-rug before the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did Mr. Craven hate the garden?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nShe had made Martha stay with her and Martha had not objected at all.\r\nShe was very young, and used to a crowded cottage full of brothers and\r\nsisters, and she found it dull in the great servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall downstairs\r\nwhere the footman and upper-housemaids made fun of her Yorkshire speech\r\nand looked upon her as a common little thing, and sat and whispered\r\namong themselves. Martha liked to talk, and the strange child who had\r\nlived in India, and been waited upon by \xe2\x80\x9cblacks,\xe2\x80\x9d was novelty enough to\r\nattract her.\r\n\r\nShe sat down on the hearth herself without waiting to be asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt tha\xe2\x80\x99 thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 about that garden yet?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwould. That was just the way with me when I first heard about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did he hate it?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary persisted.\r\n\r\nMartha tucked her feet under her and made herself quite comfortable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen to th\xe2\x80\x99 wind wutherin\xe2\x80\x99 round the house,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou could\r\nbare stand up on the moor if you was out on it tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary did not know what \xe2\x80\x9cwutherin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d meant until she listened, and then\r\nshe understood. It must mean that hollow shuddering sort of roar which\r\nrushed round and round the house as if the giant no one could see were\r\nbuffeting it and beating at the walls and windows to try to break in.\r\nBut one knew he could not get in, and somehow it made one feel very\r\nsafe and warm inside a room with a red coal fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why did he hate it so?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, after she had listened. She\r\nintended to know if Martha did.\r\n\r\nThen Martha gave up her store of knowledge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Medlock said it\xe2\x80\x99s not to be talked about.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s lots o\xe2\x80\x99 things in this place that\xe2\x80\x99s not to be talked over.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s Mr. Craven\xe2\x80\x99s orders. His troubles are none servants\xe2\x80\x99 business,\r\nhe says. But for th\xe2\x80\x99 garden he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be like he is. It was Mrs.\r\nCraven\xe2\x80\x99s garden that she had made when first they were married an\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\njust loved it, an\xe2\x80\x99 they used to \xe2\x80\x99tend the flowers themselves. An\xe2\x80\x99 none\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 gardeners was ever let to go in. Him an\xe2\x80\x99 her used to go in an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshut th\xe2\x80\x99 door an\xe2\x80\x99 stay there hours an\xe2\x80\x99 hours, readin\xe2\x80\x99 and talkin\xe2\x80\x99. An\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshe was just a bit of a girl an\xe2\x80\x99 there was an old tree with a branch\r\nbent like a seat on it. An\xe2\x80\x99 she made roses grow over it an\xe2\x80\x99 she used to\r\nsit there. But one day when she was sittin\xe2\x80\x99 there th\xe2\x80\x99 branch broke an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshe fell on th\xe2\x80\x99 ground an\xe2\x80\x99 was hurt so bad that next day she died. Th\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndoctors thought he\xe2\x80\x99d go out o\xe2\x80\x99 his mind an\xe2\x80\x99 die, too. That\xe2\x80\x99s why he\r\nhates it. No one\xe2\x80\x99s never gone in since, an\xe2\x80\x99 he won\xe2\x80\x99t let anyone talk\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary did not ask any more questions. She looked at the red fire and\r\nlistened to the wind \xe2\x80\x9cwutherin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d It seemed to be \xe2\x80\x9cwutherin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d louder\r\nthan ever.\r\n\r\nAt that moment a very good thing was happening to her. Four good things\r\nhad happened to her, in fact, since she came to Misselthwaite Manor.\r\nShe had felt as if she had understood a robin and that he had\r\nunderstood her; she had run in the wind until her blood had grown warm;\r\nshe had been healthily hungry for the first time in her life; and she\r\nhad found out what it was to be sorry for someone.\r\n\r\nBut as she was listening to the wind she began to listen to something\r\nelse. She did not know what it was, because at first she could scarcely\r\ndistinguish it from the wind itself. It was a curious sound\xe2\x80\x94it seemed\r\nalmost as if a child were crying somewhere. Sometimes the wind sounded\r\nrather like a child crying, but presently Mistress Mary felt quite sure\r\nthis sound was inside the house, not outside it. It was far away, but\r\nit was inside. She turned round and looked at Martha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear anyone crying?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nMartha suddenly looked confused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 wind. Sometimes it sounds like as if\r\nsomeone was lost on th\xe2\x80\x99 moor an\xe2\x80\x99 wailin\xe2\x80\x99. It\xe2\x80\x99s got all sorts o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut listen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s in the house\xe2\x80\x94down one of those long\r\ncorridors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd at that very moment a door must have been opened somewhere\r\ndownstairs; for a great rushing draft blew along the passage and the\r\ndoor of the room they sat in was blown open with a crash, and as they\r\nboth jumped to their feet the light was blown out and the crying sound\r\nwas swept down the far corridor so that it was to be heard more plainly\r\nthan ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI told you so! It is someone crying\xe2\x80\x94and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\ngrown-up person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha ran and shut the door and turned the key, but before she did it\r\nthey both heard the sound of a door in some far passage shutting with a\r\nbang, and then everything was quiet, for even the wind ceased\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwutherin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d for a few moments.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was th\xe2\x80\x99 wind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha stubbornly. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t, it was\r\nlittle Betty Butterworth, th\xe2\x80\x99 scullery-maid. She\xe2\x80\x99s had th\xe2\x80\x99 toothache\r\nall day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut something troubled and awkward in her manner made Mistress Mary\r\nstare very hard at her. She did not believe she was speaking the truth.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHERE WAS SOMEONE CRYING\xe2\x80\x94THERE WAS!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next day the rain poured down in torrents again, and when Mary\r\nlooked out of her window the moor was almost hidden by gray mist and\r\ncloud. There could be no going out today.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you do in your cottage when it rains like this?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked\r\nMartha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry to keep from under each other\xe2\x80\x99s feet mostly,\xe2\x80\x9d Martha answered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! there does seem a lot of us then. Mother\xe2\x80\x99s a good-tempered woman\r\nbut she gets fair moithered. The biggest ones goes out in th\xe2\x80\x99 cow-shed\r\nand plays there. Dickon he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind th\xe2\x80\x99 wet. He goes out just th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsame as if th\xe2\x80\x99 sun was shinin\xe2\x80\x99. He says he sees things on rainy days as\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t show when it\xe2\x80\x99s fair weather. He once found a little fox cub\r\nhalf drowned in its hole and he brought it home in th\xe2\x80\x99 bosom of his\r\nshirt to keep it warm. Its mother had been killed nearby an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 hole\r\nwas swum out an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 rest o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 litter was dead. He\xe2\x80\x99s got it at home\r\nnow. He found a half-drowned young crow another time an\xe2\x80\x99 he brought it\r\nhome, too, an\xe2\x80\x99 tamed it. It\xe2\x80\x99s named Soot because it\xe2\x80\x99s so black, an\xe2\x80\x99 it\r\nhops an\xe2\x80\x99 flies about with him everywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe time had come when Mary had forgotten to resent Martha\xe2\x80\x99s familiar\r\ntalk. She had even begun to find it interesting and to be sorry when\r\nshe stopped or went away. The stories she had been told by her Ayah\r\nwhen she lived in India had been quite unlike those Martha had to tell\r\nabout the moorland cottage which held fourteen people who lived in four\r\nlittle rooms and never had quite enough to eat. The children seemed to\r\ntumble about and amuse themselves like a litter of rough, good-natured\r\ncollie puppies. Mary was most attracted by the mother and Dickon. When\r\nMartha told stories of what \xe2\x80\x9cmother\xe2\x80\x9d said or did they always sounded\r\ncomfortable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had a raven or a fox cub I could play with it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nhave nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha looked perplexed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan tha\xe2\x80\x99 knit?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan tha\xe2\x80\x99 sew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan tha\xe2\x80\x99 read?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tha read somethin\xe2\x80\x99, or learn a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 spellin\xe2\x80\x99?\r\nTha\xe2\x80\x99st old enough to be learnin\xe2\x80\x99 thy book a good bit now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t any books,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cThose I had were left in India.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cIf Mrs. Medlock\xe2\x80\x99d let thee go into th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlibrary, there\xe2\x80\x99s thousands o\xe2\x80\x99 books there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary did not ask where the library was, because she was suddenly\r\ninspired by a new idea. She made up her mind to go and find it herself.\r\nShe was not troubled about Mrs. Medlock. Mrs. Medlock seemed always to\r\nbe in her comfortable housekeeper\xe2\x80\x99s sitting-room downstairs. In this\r\nqueer place one scarcely ever saw anyone at all. In fact, there was no\r\none to see but the servants, and when their master was away they lived\r\na luxurious life below stairs, where there was a huge kitchen hung\r\nabout with shining brass and pewter, and a large servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall where\r\nthere were four or five abundant meals eaten every day, and where a\r\ngreat deal of lively romping went on when Mrs. Medlock was out of the\r\nway.\r\n\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s meals were served regularly, and Martha waited on her, but no\r\none troubled themselves about her in the least. Mrs. Medlock came and\r\nlooked at her every day or two, but no one inquired what she did or\r\ntold her what to do. She supposed that perhaps this was the English way\r\nof treating children. In India she had always been attended by her\r\nAyah, who had followed her about and waited on her, hand and foot. She\r\nhad often been tired of her company. Now she was followed by nobody and\r\nwas learning to dress herself because Martha looked as though she\r\nthought she was silly and stupid when she wanted to have things handed\r\nto her and put on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t tha\xe2\x80\x99 got good sense?\xe2\x80\x9d she said once, when Mary had stood\r\nwaiting for her to put on her gloves for her. \xe2\x80\x9cOur Susan Ann is twice\r\nas sharp as thee an\xe2\x80\x99 she\xe2\x80\x99s only four year\xe2\x80\x99 old. Sometimes tha\xe2\x80\x99 looks\r\nfair soft in th\xe2\x80\x99 head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had worn her contrary scowl for an hour after that, but it made\r\nher think several entirely new things.\r\n\r\nShe stood at the window for about ten minutes this morning after Martha\r\nhad swept up the hearth for the last time and gone downstairs. She was\r\nthinking over the new idea which had come to her when she heard of the\r\nlibrary. She did not care very much about the library itself, because\r\nshe had read very few books; but to hear of it brought back to her mind\r\nthe hundred rooms with closed doors. She wondered if they were all\r\nreally locked and what she would find if she could get into any of\r\nthem. Were there a hundred really? Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t she go and see how\r\nmany doors she could count? It would be something to do on this morning\r\nwhen she could not go out. She had never been taught to ask permission\r\nto do things, and she knew nothing at all about authority, so she would\r\nnot have thought it necessary to ask Mrs. Medlock if she might walk\r\nabout the house, even if she had seen her.\r\n\r\nShe opened the door of the room and went into the corridor, and then\r\nshe began her wanderings. It was a long corridor and it branched into\r\nother corridors and it led her up short flights of steps which mounted\r\nto others again. There were doors and doors, and there were pictures on\r\nthe walls. Sometimes they were pictures of dark, curious landscapes,\r\nbut oftenest they were portraits of men and women in queer, grand\r\ncostumes made of satin and velvet. She found herself in one long\r\ngallery whose walls were covered with these portraits. She had never\r\nthought there could be so many in any house. She walked slowly down\r\nthis place and stared at the faces which also seemed to stare at her.\r\nShe felt as if they were wondering what a little girl from India was\r\ndoing in their house. Some were pictures of children\xe2\x80\x94little girls in\r\nthick satin frocks which reached to their feet and stood out about\r\nthem, and boys with puffed sleeves and lace collars and long hair, or\r\nwith big ruffs around their necks. She always stopped to look at the\r\nchildren, and wonder what their names were, and where they had gone,\r\nand why they wore such odd clothes. There was a stiff, plain little\r\ngirl rather like herself. She wore a green brocade dress and held a\r\ngreen parrot on her finger. Her eyes had a sharp, curious look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you live now?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary aloud to her. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you were\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSurely no other little girl ever spent such a queer morning. It seemed\r\nas if there was no one in all the huge rambling house but her own small\r\nself, wandering about upstairs and down, through narrow passages and\r\nwide ones, where it seemed to her that no one but herself had ever\r\nwalked. Since so many rooms had been built, people must have lived in\r\nthem, but it all seemed so empty that she could not quite believe it\r\ntrue.\r\n\r\nIt was not until she climbed to the second floor that she thought of\r\nturning the handle of a door. All the doors were shut, as Mrs. Medlock\r\nhad said they were, but at last she put her hand on the handle of one\r\nof them and turned it. She was almost frightened for a moment when she\r\nfelt that it turned without difficulty and that when she pushed upon\r\nthe door itself it slowly and heavily opened. It was a massive door and\r\nopened into a big bedroom. There were embroidered hangings on the wall,\r\nand inlaid furniture such as she had seen in India stood about the\r\nroom. A broad window with leaded panes looked out upon the moor; and\r\nover the mantel was another portrait of the stiff, plain little girl\r\nwho seemed to stare at her more curiously than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps she slept here once,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cShe stares at me so that she\r\nmakes me feel queer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter that she opened more doors and more. She saw so many rooms that\r\nshe became quite tired and began to think that there must be a hundred,\r\nthough she had not counted them. In all of them there were old pictures\r\nor old tapestries with strange scenes worked on them. There were\r\ncurious pieces of furniture and curious ornaments in nearly all of\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nIn one room, which looked like a lady\xe2\x80\x99s sitting-room, the hangings were\r\nall embroidered velvet, and in a cabinet were about a hundred little\r\nelephants made of ivory. They were of different sizes, and some had\r\ntheir mahouts or palanquins on their backs. Some were much bigger than\r\nthe others and some were so tiny that they seemed only babies. Mary had\r\nseen carved ivory in India and she knew all about elephants. She opened\r\nthe door of the cabinet and stood on a footstool and played with these\r\nfor quite a long time. When she got tired she set the elephants in\r\norder and shut the door of the cabinet.\r\n\r\nIn all her wanderings through the long corridors and the empty rooms,\r\nshe had seen nothing alive; but in this room she saw something. Just\r\nafter she had closed the cabinet door she heard a tiny rustling sound.\r\nIt made her jump and look around at the sofa by the fireplace, from\r\nwhich it seemed to come. In the corner of the sofa there was a cushion,\r\nand in the velvet which covered it there was a hole, and out of the\r\nhole peeped a tiny head with a pair of frightened eyes in it.\r\n\r\nMary crept softly across the room to look. The bright eyes belonged to\r\na little gray mouse, and the mouse had eaten a hole into the cushion\r\nand made a comfortable nest there. Six baby mice were cuddled up asleep\r\nnear her. If there was no one else alive in the hundred rooms there\r\nwere seven mice who did not look lonely at all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so frightened I would take them back with me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mary.\r\n\r\nShe had wandered about long enough to feel too tired to wander any\r\nfarther, and she turned back. Two or three times she lost her way by\r\nturning down the wrong corridor and was obliged to ramble up and down\r\nuntil she found the right one; but at last she reached her own floor\r\nagain, though she was some distance from her own room and did not know\r\nexactly where she was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I have taken a wrong turning again,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, standing\r\nstill at what seemed the end of a short passage with tapestry on the\r\nwall. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know which way to go. How still everything is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was while she was standing here and just after she had said this\r\nthat the stillness was broken by a sound. It was another cry, but not\r\nquite like the one she had heard last night; it was only a short one, a\r\nfretful childish whine muffled by passing through walls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nearer than it was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, her heart beating rather faster.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it _is_ crying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe put her hand accidentally upon the tapestry near her, and then\r\nsprang back, feeling quite startled. The tapestry was the covering of a\r\ndoor which fell open and showed her that there was another part of the\r\ncorridor behind it, and Mrs. Medlock was coming up it with her bunch of\r\nkeys in her hand and a very cross look on her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing here?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and she took Mary by the arm and\r\npulled her away. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI turned round the wrong corner,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know which\r\nway to go and I heard someone crying.\xe2\x80\x9d She quite hated Mrs. Medlock at\r\nthe moment, but she hated her more the next.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t hear anything of the sort,\xe2\x80\x9d said the housekeeper. \xe2\x80\x9cYou come\r\nalong back to your own nursery or I\xe2\x80\x99ll box your ears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd she took her by the arm and half pushed, half pulled her up one\r\npassage and down another until she pushed her in at the door of her own\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou stay where you\xe2\x80\x99re told to stay or you\xe2\x80\x99ll find\r\nyourself locked up. The master had better get you a governess, same as\r\nhe said he would. You\xe2\x80\x99re one that needs someone to look sharp after\r\nyou. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got enough to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went out of the room and slammed the door after her, and Mary went\r\nand sat on the hearth-rug, pale with rage. She did not cry, but ground\r\nher teeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere _was_ someone crying\xe2\x80\x94there _was_\xe2\x80\x94there _was!_\xe2\x80\x9d she said to\r\nherself.\r\n\r\nShe had heard it twice now, and sometime she would find out. She had\r\nfound out a great deal this morning. She felt as if she had been on a\r\nlong journey, and at any rate she had had something to amuse her all\r\nthe time, and she had played with the ivory elephants and had seen the\r\ngray mouse and its babies in their nest in the velvet cushion.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\nTHE KEY TO THE GARDEN\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo days after this, when Mary opened her eyes she sat upright in bed\r\nimmediately, and called to Martha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at the moor! Look at the moor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe rainstorm had ended and the gray mist and clouds had been swept\r\naway in the night by the wind. The wind itself had ceased and a\r\nbrilliant, deep blue sky arched high over the moorland. Never, never\r\nhad Mary dreamed of a sky so blue. In India skies were hot and blazing;\r\nthis was of a deep cool blue which almost seemed to sparkle like the\r\nwaters of some lovely bottomless lake, and here and there, high, high\r\nin the arched blueness floated small clouds of snow-white fleece. The\r\nfar-reaching world of the moor itself looked softly blue instead of\r\ngloomy purple-black or awful dreary gray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha with a cheerful grin. \xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 storm\xe2\x80\x99s over for a bit.\r\nIt does like this at this time o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 year. It goes off in a night like\r\nit was pretendin\xe2\x80\x99 it had never been here an\xe2\x80\x99 never meant to come again.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s because th\xe2\x80\x99 springtime\xe2\x80\x99s on its way. It\xe2\x80\x99s a long way off yet,\r\nbut it\xe2\x80\x99s comin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought perhaps it always rained or looked dark in England,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha, sitting up on her heels among her black lead\r\nbrushes. \xe2\x80\x9cNowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 soart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does that mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary seriously. In India the natives spoke\r\ndifferent dialects which only a few people understood, so she was not\r\nsurprised when Martha used words she did not know.\r\n\r\nMartha laughed as she had done the first morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve talked broad Yorkshire again like Mrs.\r\nMedlock said I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t. \xe2\x80\x98Nowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 soart\xe2\x80\x99 means\r\n\xe2\x80\x98nothin\xe2\x80\x99-of-the-sort,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d slowly and carefully, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it takes so long to\r\nsay it. Yorkshire\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 sunniest place on earth when it is sunny. I\r\ntold thee tha\xe2\x80\x99d like th\xe2\x80\x99 moor after a bit. Just you wait till you see\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 gold-colored gorse blossoms an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 blossoms o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 broom, an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nheather flowerin\xe2\x80\x99, all purple bells, an\xe2\x80\x99 hundreds o\xe2\x80\x99 butterflies\r\nflutterin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 bees hummin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 skylarks soarin\xe2\x80\x99 up an\xe2\x80\x99 singin\xe2\x80\x99. You\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwant to get out on it at sunrise an\xe2\x80\x99 live out on it all day like Dickon\r\ndoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould I ever get there?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary wistfully, looking through her\r\nwindow at the far-off blue. It was so new and big and wonderful and\r\nsuch a heavenly color.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99s never used tha\xe2\x80\x99 legs since tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwas born, it seems to me. Tha\xe2\x80\x99 couldn\xe2\x80\x99t walk five mile. It\xe2\x80\x99s five mile\r\nto our cottage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see your cottage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha stared at her a moment curiously before she took up her\r\npolishing brush and began to rub the grate again. She was thinking that\r\nthe small plain face did not look quite as sour at this moment as it\r\nhad done the first morning she saw it. It looked just a trifle like\r\nlittle Susan Ann\xe2\x80\x99s when she wanted something very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll ask my mother about it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s one o\xe2\x80\x99 them that nearly\r\nalways sees a way to do things. It\xe2\x80\x99s my day out today an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhome. Eh! I am glad. Mrs. Medlock thinks a lot o\xe2\x80\x99 mother. Perhaps she\r\ncould talk to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like your mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think tha\xe2\x80\x99 did,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Martha, polishing away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, tha\xe2\x80\x99 hasn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Martha.\r\n\r\nShe sat up on her heels again and rubbed the end of her nose with the\r\nback of her hand as if puzzled for a moment, but she ended quite\r\npositively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she\xe2\x80\x99s that sensible an\xe2\x80\x99 hard workin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 good-natured an\xe2\x80\x99 clean\r\nthat no one could help likin\xe2\x80\x99 her whether they\xe2\x80\x99d seen her or not. When\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 home to her on my day out I just jump for joy when I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ncrossin\xe2\x80\x99 the moor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d added Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha stoutly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve told thee that th\xe2\x80\x99 very birds likes\r\nhim an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 rabbits an\xe2\x80\x99 wild sheep an\xe2\x80\x99 ponies, an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 foxes\r\nthemselves. I wonder,\xe2\x80\x9d staring at her reflectively, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat Dickon would\r\nthink of thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary in her stiff, cold little way. \xe2\x80\x9cNo one\r\ndoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha looked reflective again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does tha\xe2\x80\x99 like thysel\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired, really quite as if she were\r\ncurious to know.\r\n\r\nMary hesitated a moment and thought it over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all\xe2\x80\x94really,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I never thought of that\r\nbefore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha grinned a little as if at some homely recollection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother said that to me once,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was at her wash-tub an\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nwas in a bad temper an\xe2\x80\x99 talkin\xe2\x80\x99 ill of folk, an\xe2\x80\x99 she turns round on me\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 says: \xe2\x80\x98Tha\xe2\x80\x99 young vixen, tha\xe2\x80\x99! There tha\xe2\x80\x99 stands sayin\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t like this one an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like that one. How does tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlike thysel\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x99 It made me laugh an\xe2\x80\x99 it brought me to my senses in a\r\nminute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went away in high spirits as soon as she had given Mary her\r\nbreakfast. She was going to walk five miles across the moor to the\r\ncottage, and she was going to help her mother with the washing and do\r\nthe week\xe2\x80\x99s baking and enjoy herself thoroughly.\r\n\r\nMary felt lonelier than ever when she knew she was no longer in the\r\nhouse. She went out into the garden as quickly as possible, and the\r\nfirst thing she did was to run round and round the fountain flower\r\ngarden ten times. She counted the times carefully and when she had\r\nfinished she felt in better spirits. The sunshine made the whole place\r\nlook different. The high, deep, blue sky arched over Misselthwaite as\r\nwell as over the moor, and she kept lifting her face and looking up\r\ninto it, trying to imagine what it would be like to lie down on one of\r\nthe little snow-white clouds and float about. She went into the first\r\nkitchen-garden and found Ben Weatherstaff working there with two other\r\ngardeners. The change in the weather seemed to have done him good. He\r\nspoke to her of his own accord.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpringtime\xe2\x80\x99s comin,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cCannot tha\xe2\x80\x99 smell it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary sniffed and thought she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI smell something nice and fresh and damp,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 good rich earth,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, digging away. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s in a\r\ngood humor makin\xe2\x80\x99 ready to grow things. It\xe2\x80\x99s glad when plantin\xe2\x80\x99 time\r\ncomes. It\xe2\x80\x99s dull in th\xe2\x80\x99 winter when it\xe2\x80\x99s got nowt to do. In th\xe2\x80\x99 flower\r\ngardens out there things will be stirrin\xe2\x80\x99 down below in th\xe2\x80\x99 dark. Th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsun\xe2\x80\x99s warmin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em. You\xe2\x80\x99ll see bits o\xe2\x80\x99 green spikes stickin\xe2\x80\x99 out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nblack earth after a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will they be?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrocuses an\xe2\x80\x99 snowdrops an\xe2\x80\x99 daffydowndillys. Has tha\xe2\x80\x99 never seen them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Everything is hot, and wet, and green after the rains in India,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I think things grow up in a night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese won\xe2\x80\x99t grow up in a night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Weatherstaff. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99ll have to\r\nwait for \xe2\x80\x99em. They\xe2\x80\x99ll poke up a bit higher here, an\xe2\x80\x99 push out a spike\r\nmore there, an\xe2\x80\x99 uncurl a leaf this day an\xe2\x80\x99 another that. You watch\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\nVery soon she heard the soft rustling flight of wings again and she\r\nknew at once that the robin had come again. He was very pert and\r\nlively, and hopped about so close to her feet, and put his head on one\r\nside and looked at her so slyly that she asked Ben Weatherstaff a\r\nquestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he remembers me?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemembers thee!\xe2\x80\x9d said Weatherstaff indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe knows every\r\ncabbage stump in th\xe2\x80\x99 gardens, let alone th\xe2\x80\x99 people. He\xe2\x80\x99s never seen a\r\nlittle wench here before, an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99s bent on findin\xe2\x80\x99 out all about thee.\r\nTha\xe2\x80\x99s no need to try to hide anything from _him_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre things stirring down below in the dark in that garden where he\r\nlives?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat garden?\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Weatherstaff, becoming surly again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one where the old rose-trees are.\xe2\x80\x9d She could not help asking,\r\nbecause she wanted so much to know. \xe2\x80\x9cAre all the flowers dead, or do\r\nsome of them come again in the summer? Are there ever any roses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff, hunching his shoulders toward the\r\nrobin. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s the only one as knows. No one else has seen inside it for\r\nten year\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTen years was a long time, Mary thought. She had been born ten years\r\nago.\r\n\r\nShe walked away, slowly thinking. She had begun to like the garden just\r\nas she had begun to like the robin and Dickon and Martha\xe2\x80\x99s mother. She\r\nwas beginning to like Martha, too. That seemed a good many people to\r\nlike\xe2\x80\x94when you were not used to liking. She thought of the robin as one\r\nof the people. She went to her walk outside the long, ivy-covered wall\r\nover which she could see the tree-tops; and the second time she walked\r\nup and down the most interesting and exciting thing happened to her,\r\nand it was all through Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s robin.\r\n\r\nShe heard a chirp and a twitter, and when she looked at the bare\r\nflower-bed at her left side there he was hopping about and pretending\r\nto peck things out of the earth to persuade her that he had not\r\nfollowed her. But she knew he had followed her and the surprise so\r\nfilled her with delight that she almost trembled a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do remember me!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out. \xe2\x80\x9cYou do! You are prettier than\r\nanything else in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe chirped, and talked, and coaxed and he hopped, and flirted his tail\r\nand twittered. It was as if he were talking. His red waistcoat was like\r\nsatin and he puffed his tiny breast out and was so fine and so grand\r\nand so pretty that it was really as if he were showing her how\r\nimportant and like a human person a robin could be. Mistress Mary\r\nforgot that she had ever been contrary in her life when he allowed her\r\nto draw closer and closer to him, and bend down and talk and try to\r\nmake something like robin sounds.\r\n\r\nOh! to think that he should actually let her come as near to him as\r\nthat! He knew nothing in the world would make her put out her hand\r\ntoward him or startle him in the least tiniest way. He knew it because\r\nhe was a real person\xe2\x80\x94only nicer than any other person in the world. She\r\nwas so happy that she scarcely dared to breathe.\r\n\r\nThe flower-bed was not quite bare. It was bare of flowers because the\r\nperennial plants had been cut down for their winter rest, but there\r\nwere tall shrubs and low ones which grew together at the back of the\r\nbed, and as the robin hopped about under them she saw him hop over a\r\nsmall pile of freshly turned up earth. He stopped on it to look for a\r\nworm. The earth had been turned up because a dog had been trying to dig\r\nup a mole and he had scratched quite a deep hole.\r\n\r\nMary looked at it, not really knowing why the hole was there, and as\r\nshe looked she saw something almost buried in the newly-turned soil. It\r\nwas something like a ring of rusty iron or brass and when the robin\r\nflew up into a tree nearby she put out her hand and picked the ring up.\r\nIt was more than a ring, however; it was an old key which looked as if\r\nit had been buried a long time.\r\n\r\nMistress Mary stood up and looked at it with an almost frightened face\r\nas it hung from her finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it has been buried for ten years,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a whisper.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it is the key to the garden!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\nTHE ROBIN WHO SHOWED THE WAY\r\n\r\n\r\nShe looked at the key quite a long time. She turned it over and over,\r\nand thought about it. As I have said before, she was not a child who\r\nhad been trained to ask permission or consult her elders about things.\r\nAll she thought about the key was that if it was the key to the closed\r\ngarden, and she could find out where the door was, she could perhaps\r\nopen it and see what was inside the walls, and what had happened to the\r\nold rose-trees. It was because it had been shut up so long that she\r\nwanted to see it. It seemed as if it must be different from other\r\nplaces and that something strange must have happened to it during ten\r\nyears. Besides that, if she liked it she could go into it every day and\r\nshut the door behind her, and she could make up some play of her own\r\nand play it quite alone, because nobody would ever know where she was,\r\nbut would think the door was still locked and the key buried in the\r\nearth. The thought of that pleased her very much.\r\n\r\nLiving as it were, all by herself in a house with a hundred\r\nmysteriously closed rooms and having nothing whatever to do to amuse\r\nherself, had set her inactive brain to working and was actually\r\nawakening her imagination. There is no doubt that the fresh, strong,\r\npure air from the moor had a great deal to do with it. Just as it had\r\ngiven her an appetite, and fighting with the wind had stirred her\r\nblood, so the same things had stirred her mind. In India she had always\r\nbeen too hot and languid and weak to care much about anything, but in\r\nthis place she was beginning to care and to want to do new things.\r\nAlready she felt less \xe2\x80\x9ccontrary,\xe2\x80\x9d though she did not know why.\r\n\r\nShe put the key in her pocket and walked up and down her walk. No one\r\nbut herself ever seemed to come there, so she could walk slowly and\r\nlook at the wall, or, rather, at the ivy growing on it. The ivy was the\r\nbaffling thing. Howsoever carefully she looked she could see nothing\r\nbut thickly growing, glossy, dark green leaves. She was very much\r\ndisappointed. Something of her contrariness came back to her as she\r\npaced the walk and looked over it at the tree-tops inside. It seemed so\r\nsilly, she said to herself, to be near it and not be able to get in.\r\nShe took the key in her pocket when she went back to the house, and she\r\nmade up her mind that she would always carry it with her when she went\r\nout, so that if she ever should find the hidden door she would be\r\nready.\r\n\r\nMrs. Medlock had allowed Martha to sleep all night at the cottage, but\r\nshe was back at her work in the morning with cheeks redder than ever\r\nand in the best of spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI got up at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! it was pretty on th\xe2\x80\x99 moor\r\nwith th\xe2\x80\x99 birds gettin\xe2\x80\x99 up an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 rabbits scamperin\xe2\x80\x99 about an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 sun\r\nrisin\xe2\x80\x99. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t walk all th\xe2\x80\x99 way. A man gave me a ride in his cart an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI did enjoy myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was full of stories of the delights of her day out. Her mother had\r\nbeen glad to see her and they had got the baking and washing all out of\r\nthe way. She had even made each of the children a doughcake with a bit\r\nof brown sugar in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had \xe2\x80\x99em all pipin\xe2\x80\x99 hot when they came in from playin\xe2\x80\x99 on th\xe2\x80\x99 moor.\r\nAn\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 cottage all smelt o\xe2\x80\x99 nice, clean hot bakin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 there was a\r\ngood fire, an\xe2\x80\x99 they just shouted for joy. Our Dickon he said our\r\ncottage was good enough for a king to live in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the evening they had all sat round the fire, and Martha and her\r\nmother had sewed patches on torn clothes and mended stockings and\r\nMartha had told them about the little girl who had come from India and\r\nwho had been waited on all her life by what Martha called \xe2\x80\x9cblacks\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nuntil she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how to put on her own stockings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! they did like to hear about you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cThey wanted to\r\nknow all about th\xe2\x80\x99 blacks an\xe2\x80\x99 about th\xe2\x80\x99 ship you came in. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntell \xe2\x80\x99em enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary reflected a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you a great deal more before your next day out,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cso that you will have more to talk about. I dare say they would like\r\nto hear about riding on elephants and camels, and about the officers\r\ngoing to hunt tigers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word!\xe2\x80\x9d cried delighted Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would set \xe2\x80\x99em clean off their\r\nheads. Would tha\xe2\x80\x99 really do that, Miss? It would be same as a wild\r\nbeast show like we heard they had in York once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndia is quite different from Yorkshire,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said slowly, as she\r\nthought the matter over. \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of that. Did Dickon and your\r\nmother like to hear you talk about me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, our Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s eyes nearly started out o\xe2\x80\x99 his head, they got that\r\nround,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cBut mother, she was put out about your\r\nseemin\xe2\x80\x99 to be all by yourself like. She said, \xe2\x80\x98Hasn\xe2\x80\x99t Mr. Craven got no\r\ngoverness for her, nor no nurse?\xe2\x80\x99 and I said, \xe2\x80\x98No, he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t, though\r\nMrs. Medlock says he will when he thinks of it, but she says he mayn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink of it for two or three years.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want a governess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut mother says you ought to be learnin\xe2\x80\x99 your book by this time an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyou ought to have a woman to look after you, an\xe2\x80\x99 she says: \xe2\x80\x98Now,\r\nMartha, you just think how you\xe2\x80\x99d feel yourself, in a big place like\r\nthat, wanderin\xe2\x80\x99 about all alone, an\xe2\x80\x99 no mother. You do your best to\r\ncheer her up,\xe2\x80\x99 she says, an\xe2\x80\x99 I said I would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary gave her a long, steady look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do cheer me up,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI like to hear you talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently Martha went out of the room and came back with something held\r\nin her hands under her apron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does tha\xe2\x80\x99 think,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a cheerful grin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve brought\r\nthee a present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA present!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mistress Mary. How could a cottage full of\r\nfourteen hungry people give anyone a present!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man was drivin\xe2\x80\x99 across the moor peddlin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d Martha explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nstopped his cart at our door. He had pots an\xe2\x80\x99 pans an\xe2\x80\x99 odds an\xe2\x80\x99 ends,\r\nbut mother had no money to buy anythin\xe2\x80\x99. Just as he was goin\xe2\x80\x99 away our\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Lizabeth Ellen called out, \xe2\x80\x98Mother, he\xe2\x80\x99s got skippin\xe2\x80\x99-ropes with red\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 blue handles.\xe2\x80\x99 An\xe2\x80\x99 mother she calls out quite sudden, \xe2\x80\x98Here, stop,\r\nmister! How much are they?\xe2\x80\x99 An\xe2\x80\x99 he says \xe2\x80\x98Tuppence\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 mother she\r\nbegan fumblin\xe2\x80\x99 in her pocket an\xe2\x80\x99 she says to me, \xe2\x80\x98Martha, tha\xe2\x80\x99s brought\r\nme thy wages like a good lass, an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ve got four places to put every\r\npenny, but I\xe2\x80\x99m just goin\xe2\x80\x99 to take tuppence out of it to buy that child\r\na skippin\xe2\x80\x99-rope,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 she bought one an\xe2\x80\x99 here it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe brought it out from under her apron and exhibited it quite proudly.\r\nIt was a strong, slender rope with a striped red and blue handle at\r\neach end, but Mary Lennox had never seen a skipping-rope before. She\r\ngazed at it with a mystified expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it for?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor!\xe2\x80\x9d cried out Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes tha\xe2\x80\x99 mean that they\xe2\x80\x99ve not got\r\nskippin\xe2\x80\x99-ropes in India, for all they\xe2\x80\x99ve got elephants and tigers and\r\ncamels! No wonder most of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x99s black. This is what it\xe2\x80\x99s for; just\r\nwatch me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd she ran into the middle of the room and, taking a handle in each\r\nhand, began to skip, and skip, and skip, while Mary turned in her chair\r\nto stare at her, and the queer faces in the old portraits seemed to\r\nstare at her, too, and wonder what on earth this common little cottager\r\nhad the impudence to be doing under their very noses. But Martha did\r\nnot even see them. The interest and curiosity in Mistress Mary\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\ndelighted her, and she went on skipping and counted as she skipped\r\nuntil she had reached a hundred.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could skip longer than that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said when she stopped. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nskipped as much as five hundred when I was twelve, but I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t as fat\r\nthen as I am now, an\xe2\x80\x99 I was in practice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary got up from her chair beginning to feel excited herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks nice,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYour mother is a kind woman. Do you think I\r\ncould ever skip like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou just try it,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Martha, handing her the skipping-rope. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t skip a hundred at first, but if you practice you\xe2\x80\x99ll mount up.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s what mother said. She says, \xe2\x80\x98Nothin\xe2\x80\x99 will do her more good than\r\nskippin\xe2\x80\x99 rope. It\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 sensiblest toy a child can have. Let her play\r\nout in th\xe2\x80\x99 fresh air skippin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99ll stretch her legs an\xe2\x80\x99 arms an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ngive her some strength in \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was plain that there was not a great deal of strength in Mistress\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s arms and legs when she first began to skip. She was not very\r\nclever at it, but she liked it so much that she did not want to stop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut on tha\xe2\x80\x99 things and run an\xe2\x80\x99 skip out o\xe2\x80\x99 doors,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother said I must tell you to keep out o\xe2\x80\x99 doors as much as you could,\r\neven when it rains a bit, so as tha\xe2\x80\x99 wrap up warm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary put on her coat and hat and took her skipping-rope over her arm.\r\nShe opened the door to go out, and then suddenly thought of something\r\nand turned back rather slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMartha,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthey were your wages. It was your two-pence\r\nreally. Thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d She said it stiffly because she was not used to\r\nthanking people or noticing that they did things for her. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, and held out her hand because she did not know what else to\r\ndo.\r\n\r\nMartha gave her hand a clumsy little shake, as if she was not\r\naccustomed to this sort of thing either. Then she laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! th\xe2\x80\x99 art a queer, old-womanish thing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf tha\xe2\x80\x99d been our\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Lizabeth Ellen tha\xe2\x80\x99d have given me a kiss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary looked stiffer than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want me to kiss you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha laughed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, not me,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIf tha\xe2\x80\x99 was different, p\xe2\x80\x99raps tha\xe2\x80\x99d want\r\nto thysel\xe2\x80\x99. But tha\xe2\x80\x99 isn\xe2\x80\x99t. Run off outside an\xe2\x80\x99 play with thy rope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMistress Mary felt a little awkward as she went out of the room.\r\nYorkshire people seemed strange, and Martha was always rather a puzzle\r\nto her. At first she had disliked her very much, but now she did not.\r\nThe skipping-rope was a wonderful thing. She counted and skipped, and\r\nskipped and counted, until her cheeks were quite red, and she was more\r\ninterested than she had ever been since she was born. The sun was\r\nshining and a little wind was blowing\xe2\x80\x94not a rough wind, but one which\r\ncame in delightful little gusts and brought a fresh scent of newly\r\nturned earth with it. She skipped round the fountain garden, and up one\r\nwalk and down another. She skipped at last into the kitchen-garden and\r\nsaw Ben Weatherstaff digging and talking to his robin, which was\r\nhopping about him. She skipped down the walk toward him and he lifted\r\nhis head and looked at her with a curious expression. She had wondered\r\nif he would notice her. She wanted him to see her skip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word. P\xe2\x80\x99raps tha\xe2\x80\x99 art a young \xe2\x80\x99un, after\r\nall, an\xe2\x80\x99 p\xe2\x80\x99raps tha\xe2\x80\x99s got child\xe2\x80\x99s blood in thy veins instead of sour\r\nbuttermilk. Tha\xe2\x80\x99s skipped red into thy cheeks as sure as my name\xe2\x80\x99s Ben\r\nWeatherstaff. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have believed tha\xe2\x80\x99 could do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never skipped before,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m just beginning. I can only go\r\nup to twenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 keep on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 shapes well enough at it for a young\r\n\xe2\x80\x99un that\xe2\x80\x99s lived with heathen. Just see how he\xe2\x80\x99s watchin\xe2\x80\x99 thee,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\njerking his head toward the robin. \xe2\x80\x9cHe followed after thee yesterday.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be at it again today. He\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound to find out what th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nskippin\xe2\x80\x99-rope is. He\xe2\x80\x99s never seen one. Eh!\xe2\x80\x9d shaking his head at the\r\nbird, \xe2\x80\x9ctha\xe2\x80\x99 curiosity will be th\xe2\x80\x99 death of thee sometime if tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t look sharp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary skipped round all the gardens and round the orchard, resting every\r\nfew minutes. At length she went to her own special walk and made up her\r\nmind to try if she could skip the whole length of it. It was a good\r\nlong skip and she began slowly, but before she had gone half-way down\r\nthe path she was so hot and breathless that she was obliged to stop.\r\nShe did not mind much, because she had already counted up to thirty.\r\nShe stopped with a little laugh of pleasure, and there, lo and behold,\r\nwas the robin swaying on a long branch of ivy. He had followed her and\r\nhe greeted her with a chirp. As Mary had skipped toward him she felt\r\nsomething heavy in her pocket strike against her at each jump, and when\r\nshe saw the robin she laughed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou showed me where the key was yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to\r\nshow me the door today; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe robin flew from his swinging spray of ivy on to the top of the wall\r\nand he opened his beak and sang a loud, lovely trill, merely to show\r\noff. Nothing in the world is quite as adorably lovely as a robin when\r\nhe shows off\xe2\x80\x94and they are nearly always doing it.\r\n\r\nMary Lennox had heard a great deal about Magic in her Ayah\xe2\x80\x99s stories,\r\nand she always said that what happened almost at that moment was Magic.\r\n\r\nOne of the nice little gusts of wind rushed down the walk, and it was a\r\nstronger one than the rest. It was strong enough to wave the branches\r\nof the trees, and it was more than strong enough to sway the trailing\r\nsprays of untrimmed ivy hanging from the wall. Mary had stepped close\r\nto the robin, and suddenly the gust of wind swung aside some loose ivy\r\ntrails, and more suddenly still she jumped toward it and caught it in\r\nher hand. This she did because she had seen something under it\xe2\x80\x94a round\r\nknob which had been covered by the leaves hanging over it. It was the\r\nknob of a door.\r\n\r\nShe put her hands under the leaves and began to pull and push them\r\naside. Thick as the ivy hung, it nearly all was a loose and swinging\r\ncurtain, though some had crept over wood and iron. Mary\xe2\x80\x99s heart began\r\nto thump and her hands to shake a little in her delight and excitement.\r\nThe robin kept singing and twittering away and tilting his head on one\r\nside, as if he were as excited as she was. What was this under her\r\nhands which was square and made of iron and which her fingers found a\r\nhole in?\r\n\r\nIt was the lock of the door which had been closed ten years and she put\r\nher hand in her pocket, drew out the key and found it fitted the\r\nkeyhole. She put the key in and turned it. It took two hands to do it,\r\nbut it did turn.\r\n\r\nAnd then she took a long breath and looked behind her up the long walk\r\nto see if anyone was coming. No one was coming. No one ever did come,\r\nit seemed, and she took another long breath, because she could not help\r\nit, and she held back the swinging curtain of ivy and pushed back the\r\ndoor which opened slowly\xe2\x80\x94slowly.\r\n\r\nThen she slipped through it, and shut it behind her, and stood with her\r\nback against it, looking about her and breathing quite fast with\r\nexcitement, and wonder, and delight.\r\n\r\nShe was standing _inside_ the secret garden.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\nTHE STRANGEST HOUSE ANYONE EVER LIVED IN\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was the sweetest, most mysterious-looking place anyone could\r\nimagine. The high walls which shut it in were covered with the leafless\r\nstems of climbing roses which were so thick that they were matted\r\ntogether. Mary Lennox knew they were roses because she had seen a great\r\nmany roses in India. All the ground was covered with grass of a wintry\r\nbrown and out of it grew clumps of bushes which were surely rosebushes\r\nif they were alive. There were numbers of standard roses which had so\r\nspread their branches that they were like little trees. There were\r\nother trees in the garden, and one of the things which made the place\r\nlook strangest and loveliest was that climbing roses had run all over\r\nthem and swung down long tendrils which made light swaying curtains,\r\nand here and there they had caught at each other or at a far-reaching\r\nbranch and had crept from one tree to another and made lovely bridges\r\nof themselves. There were neither leaves nor roses on them now and Mary\r\ndid not know whether they were dead or alive, but their thin gray or\r\nbrown branches and sprays looked like a sort of hazy mantle spreading\r\nover everything, walls, and trees, and even brown grass, where they had\r\nfallen from their fastenings and run along the ground. It was this hazy\r\ntangle from tree to tree which made it all look so mysterious. Mary had\r\nthought it must be different from other gardens which had not been left\r\nall by themselves so long; and indeed it was different from any other\r\nplace she had ever seen in her life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow still it is!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cHow still!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she waited a moment and listened at the stillness. The robin, who\r\nhad flown to his treetop, was still as all the rest. He did not even\r\nflutter his wings; he sat without stirring, and looked at Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo wonder it is still,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered again. \xe2\x80\x9cI am the first person\r\nwho has spoken in here for ten years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe moved away from the door, stepping as softly as if she were afraid\r\nof awakening someone. She was glad that there was grass under her feet\r\nand that her steps made no sounds. She walked under one of the\r\nfairy-like gray arches between the trees and looked up at the sprays\r\nand tendrils which formed them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if they are all quite dead,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it all a quite\r\ndead garden? I wish it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf she had been Ben Weatherstaff she could have told whether the wood\r\nwas alive by looking at it, but she could only see that there were only\r\ngray or brown sprays and branches and none showed any signs of even a\r\ntiny leaf-bud anywhere.\r\n\r\nBut she was _inside_ the wonderful garden and she could come through\r\nthe door under the ivy any time and she felt as if she had found a\r\nworld all her own.\r\n\r\nThe sun was shining inside the four walls and the high arch of blue sky\r\nover this particular piece of Misselthwaite seemed even more brilliant\r\nand soft than it was over the moor. The robin flew down from his\r\ntree-top and hopped about or flew after her from one bush to another.\r\nHe chirped a good deal and had a very busy air, as if he were showing\r\nher things. Everything was strange and silent and she seemed to be\r\nhundreds of miles away from anyone, but somehow she did not feel lonely\r\nat all. All that troubled her was her wish that she knew whether all\r\nthe roses were dead, or if perhaps some of them had lived and might put\r\nout leaves and buds as the weather got warmer. She did not want it to\r\nbe a quite dead garden. If it were a quite alive garden, how wonderful\r\nit would be, and what thousands of roses would grow on every side!\r\n\r\nHer skipping-rope had hung over her arm when she came in and after she\r\nhad walked about for a while she thought she would skip round the whole\r\ngarden, stopping when she wanted to look at things. There seemed to\r\nhave been grass paths here and there, and in one or two corners there\r\nwere alcoves of evergreen with stone seats or tall moss-covered flower\r\nurns in them.\r\n\r\nAs she came near the second of these alcoves she stopped skipping.\r\nThere had once been a flowerbed in it, and she thought she saw\r\nsomething sticking out of the black earth\xe2\x80\x94some sharp little pale green\r\npoints. She remembered what Ben Weatherstaff had said and she knelt\r\ndown to look at them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they are tiny growing things and they _might_ be crocuses or\r\nsnowdrops or daffodils,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered.\r\n\r\nShe bent very close to them and sniffed the fresh scent of the damp\r\nearth. She liked it very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps there are some other ones coming up in other places,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI will go all over the garden and look.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not skip, but walked. She went slowly and kept her eyes on the\r\nground. She looked in the old border beds and among the grass, and\r\nafter she had gone round, trying to miss nothing, she had found ever so\r\nmany more sharp, pale green points, and she had become quite excited\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a quite dead garden,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out softly to herself. \xe2\x80\x9cEven\r\nif the roses are dead, there are other things alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not know anything about gardening, but the grass seemed so\r\nthick in some of the places where the green points were pushing their\r\nway through that she thought they did not seem to have room enough to\r\ngrow. She searched about until she found a rather sharp piece of wood\r\nand knelt down and dug and weeded out the weeds and grass until she\r\nmade nice little clear places around them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow they look as if they could breathe,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after she had\r\nfinished with the first ones. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to do ever so many more. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndo all I can see. If I haven\xe2\x80\x99t time today I can come tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went from place to place, and dug and weeded, and enjoyed herself\r\nso immensely that she was led on from bed to bed and into the grass\r\nunder the trees. The exercise made her so warm that she first threw her\r\ncoat off, and then her hat, and without knowing it she was smiling down\r\non to the grass and the pale green points all the time.\r\n\r\nThe robin was tremendously busy. He was very much pleased to see\r\ngardening begun on his own estate. He had often wondered at Ben\r\nWeatherstaff. Where gardening is done all sorts of delightful things to\r\neat are turned up with the soil. Now here was this new kind of creature\r\nwho was not half Ben\xe2\x80\x99s size and yet had had the sense to come into his\r\ngarden and begin at once.\r\n\r\nMistress Mary worked in her garden until it was time to go to her\r\nmidday dinner. In fact, she was rather late in remembering, and when\r\nshe put on her coat and hat, and picked up her skipping-rope, she could\r\nnot believe that she had been working two or three hours. She had been\r\nactually happy all the time; and dozens and dozens of the tiny, pale\r\ngreen points were to be seen in cleared places, looking twice as\r\ncheerful as they had looked before when the grass and weeds had been\r\nsmothering them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall come back this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking all round at her\r\nnew kingdom, and speaking to the trees and the rose-bushes as if they\r\nheard her.\r\n\r\nThen she ran lightly across the grass, pushed open the slow old door\r\nand slipped through it under the ivy. She had such red cheeks and such\r\nbright eyes and ate such a dinner that Martha was delighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo pieces o\xe2\x80\x99 meat an\xe2\x80\x99 two helps o\xe2\x80\x99 rice puddin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cEh!\r\nmother will be pleased when I tell her what th\xe2\x80\x99 skippin\xe2\x80\x99-rope\xe2\x80\x99s done\r\nfor thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the course of her digging with her pointed stick Mistress Mary had\r\nfound herself digging up a sort of white root rather like an onion. She\r\nhad put it back in its place and patted the earth carefully down on it\r\nand just now she wondered if Martha could tell her what it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMartha,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat are those white roots that look like onions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re bulbs,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cLots o\xe2\x80\x99 spring flowers grow from\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em. Th\xe2\x80\x99 very little ones are snowdrops an\xe2\x80\x99 crocuses an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 big ones\r\nare narcissuses an\xe2\x80\x99 jonquils and daffydowndillys. Th\xe2\x80\x99 biggest of all is\r\nlilies an\xe2\x80\x99 purple flags. Eh! they are nice. Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s got a whole lot of\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em planted in our bit o\xe2\x80\x99 garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Dickon know all about them?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary, a new idea taking\r\npossession of her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur Dickon can make a flower grow out of a brick walk. Mother says he\r\njust whispers things out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo bulbs live a long time? Would they live years and years if no one\r\nhelped them?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Mary anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re things as helps themselves,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s why poor\r\nfolk can afford to have \xe2\x80\x99em. If you don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble \xe2\x80\x99em, most of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwork away underground for a lifetime an\xe2\x80\x99 spread out an\xe2\x80\x99 have little\r\n\xe2\x80\x99uns. There\xe2\x80\x99s a place in th\xe2\x80\x99 park woods here where there\xe2\x80\x99s snowdrops by\r\nthousands. They\xe2\x80\x99re the prettiest sight in Yorkshire when th\xe2\x80\x99 spring\r\ncomes. No one knows when they was first planted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish the spring was here now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to see all the\r\nthings that grow in England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had finished her dinner and gone to her favorite seat on the\r\nhearth-rug.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish\xe2\x80\x94I wish I had a little spade,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever does tha\xe2\x80\x99 want a spade for?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Martha, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cArt\r\ntha\xe2\x80\x99 goin\xe2\x80\x99 to take to diggin\xe2\x80\x99? I must tell mother that, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary looked at the fire and pondered a little. She must be careful if\r\nshe meant to keep her secret kingdom. She wasn\xe2\x80\x99t doing any harm, but if\r\nMr. Craven found out about the open door he would be fearfully angry\r\nand get a new key and lock it up forevermore. She really could not bear\r\nthat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is such a big lonely place,\xe2\x80\x9d she said slowly, as if she were\r\nturning matters over in her mind. \xe2\x80\x9cThe house is lonely, and the park is\r\nlonely, and the gardens are lonely. So many places seem shut up. I\r\nnever did many things in India, but there were more people to look\r\nat\xe2\x80\x94natives and soldiers marching by\xe2\x80\x94and sometimes bands playing, and my\r\nAyah told me stories. There is no one to talk to here except you and\r\nBen Weatherstaff. And you have to do your work and Ben Weatherstaff\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t speak to me often. I thought if I had a little spade I could dig\r\nsomewhere as he does, and I might make a little garden if he would give\r\nme some seeds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha\xe2\x80\x99s face quite lighted up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere now!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cif that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t one of th\xe2\x80\x99 things mother\r\nsaid. She says, \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s such a lot o\xe2\x80\x99 room in that big place, why\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t they give her a bit for herself, even if she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t plant\r\nnothin\xe2\x80\x99 but parsley an\xe2\x80\x99 radishes? She\xe2\x80\x99d dig an\xe2\x80\x99 rake away an\xe2\x80\x99 be right\r\ndown happy over it.\xe2\x80\x99 Them was the very words she said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHow many things she knows, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s like she says: \xe2\x80\x98A woman as brings up twelve\r\nchildren learns something besides her A B C. Children\xe2\x80\x99s as good as\r\n\xe2\x80\x99rithmetic to set you findin\xe2\x80\x99 out things.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much would a spade cost\xe2\x80\x94a little one?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d was Martha\xe2\x80\x99s reflective answer, \xe2\x80\x9cat Thwaite village there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nshop or so an\xe2\x80\x99 I saw little garden sets with a spade an\xe2\x80\x99 a rake an\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\nfork all tied together for two shillings. An\xe2\x80\x99 they was stout enough to\r\nwork with, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got more than that in my purse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Morrison gave\r\nme five shillings and Mrs. Medlock gave me some money from Mr. Craven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he remember thee that much?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Martha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Medlock said I was to have a shilling a week to spend. She gives\r\nme one every Saturday. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to spend it on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word! that\xe2\x80\x99s riches,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 can buy anything in th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nworld tha\xe2\x80\x99 wants. Th\xe2\x80\x99 rent of our cottage is only one an\xe2\x80\x99 threepence\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99s like pullin\xe2\x80\x99 eye-teeth to get it. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ve just thought of\r\nsomethin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d putting her hands on her hips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the shop at Thwaite they sell packages o\xe2\x80\x99 flower-seeds for a penny\r\neach, and our Dickon he knows which is th\xe2\x80\x99 prettiest ones an\xe2\x80\x99 how to\r\nmake \xe2\x80\x99em grow. He walks over to Thwaite many a day just for th\xe2\x80\x99 fun of\r\nit. Does tha\xe2\x80\x99 know how to print letters?\xe2\x80\x9d suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know how to write,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary answered.\r\n\r\nMartha shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur Dickon can only read printin\xe2\x80\x99. If tha\xe2\x80\x99 could print we could write\r\na letter to him an\xe2\x80\x99 ask him to go an\xe2\x80\x99 buy th\xe2\x80\x99 garden tools an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nseeds at th\xe2\x80\x99 same time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! you\xe2\x80\x99re a good girl!\xe2\x80\x9d Mary cried. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are, really! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nyou were so nice. I know I can print letters if I try. Let\xe2\x80\x99s ask Mrs.\r\nMedlock for a pen and ink and some paper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got some of my own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cI bought \xe2\x80\x99em so I could print\r\na bit of a letter to mother of a Sunday. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and get it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe ran out of the room, and Mary stood by the fire and twisted her\r\nthin little hands together with sheer pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have a spade,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cI can make the earth nice and soft\r\nand dig up weeds. If I have seeds and can make flowers grow the garden\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t be dead at all\xe2\x80\x94it will come alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not go out again that afternoon because when Martha returned\r\nwith her pen and ink and paper she was obliged to clear the table and\r\ncarry the plates and dishes downstairs and when she got into the\r\nkitchen Mrs. Medlock was there and told her to do something, so Mary\r\nwaited for what seemed to her a long time before she came back. Then it\r\nwas a serious piece of work to write to Dickon. Mary had been taught\r\nvery little because her governesses had disliked her too much to stay\r\nwith her. She could not spell particularly well but she found that she\r\ncould print letters when she tried. This was the letter Martha dictated\r\nto her:\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_My Dear Dickon:_\r\n\r\nThis comes hoping to find you well as it leaves me at present. Miss\r\nMary has plenty of money and will you go to Thwaite and buy her some\r\nflower seeds and a set of garden tools to make a flower-bed. Pick the\r\nprettiest ones and easy to grow because she has never done it before\r\nand lived in India which is different. Give my love to mother and\r\neveryone of you. Miss Mary is going to tell me a lot more so that on my\r\nnext day out you can hear about elephants and camels and gentlemen\r\ngoing hunting lions and tigers.\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cYour loving sister,\r\n\r\n                \xe2\x80\x9cMartha Ph\xc5\x93be Sowerby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll put the money in th\xe2\x80\x99 envelope an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ll get th\xe2\x80\x99 butcher boy to\r\ntake it in his cart. He\xe2\x80\x99s a great friend o\xe2\x80\x99 Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow shall I get the things when Dickon buys them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll bring \xe2\x80\x99em to you himself. He\xe2\x80\x99ll like to walk over this way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cthen I shall see him! I never thought I should\r\nsee Dickon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes tha\xe2\x80\x99 want to see him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Martha suddenly, for Mary had looked\r\nso pleased.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do. I never saw a boy foxes and crows loved. I want to see him\r\nvery much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha gave a little start, as if she remembered something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow to think,\xe2\x80\x9d she broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cto think o\xe2\x80\x99 me forgettin\xe2\x80\x99 that there;\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 I thought I was goin\xe2\x80\x99 to tell you first thing this mornin\xe2\x80\x99. I asked\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x94and she said she\xe2\x80\x99d ask Mrs. Medlock her own self.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Mary began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I said Tuesday. Ask her if you might be driven over to our\r\ncottage some day and have a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 mother\xe2\x80\x99s hot oat cake, an\xe2\x80\x99 butter,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 a glass o\xe2\x80\x99 milk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed as if all the interesting things were happening in one day.\r\nTo think of going over the moor in the daylight and when the sky was\r\nblue! To think of going into the cottage which held twelve children!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes she think Mrs. Medlock would let me go?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, quite\r\nanxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, she thinks she would. She knows what a tidy woman mother is and\r\nhow clean she keeps the cottage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I went I should see your mother as well as Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary,\r\nthinking it over and liking the idea very much. \xe2\x80\x9cShe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be\r\nlike the mothers in India.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer work in the garden and the excitement of the afternoon ended by\r\nmaking her feel quiet and thoughtful. Martha stayed with her until\r\ntea-time, but they sat in comfortable quiet and talked very little. But\r\njust before Martha went downstairs for the tea-tray, Mary asked a\r\nquestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMartha,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9chas the scullery-maid had the toothache again\r\ntoday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha certainly started slightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes thee ask that?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause when I waited so long for you to come back I opened the door\r\nand walked down the corridor to see if you were coming. And I heard\r\nthat far-off crying again, just as we heard it the other night. There\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t a wind today, so you see it couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been the wind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha restlessly. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 mustn\xe2\x80\x99t go walkin\xe2\x80\x99 about in\r\ncorridors an\xe2\x80\x99 listenin\xe2\x80\x99. Mr. Craven would be that there angry there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno knowin\xe2\x80\x99 what he\xe2\x80\x99d do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t listening,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI was just waiting for you\xe2\x80\x94and I\r\nheard it. That\xe2\x80\x99s three times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word! There\xe2\x80\x99s Mrs. Medlock\xe2\x80\x99s bell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha, and she almost ran\r\nout of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the strangest house anyone ever lived in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary drowsily, as\r\nshe dropped her head on the cushioned seat of the armchair near her.\r\nFresh air, and digging, and skipping-rope had made her feel so\r\ncomfortably tired that she fell asleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\nDICKON\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sun shone down for nearly a week on the secret garden. The Secret\r\nGarden was what Mary called it when she was thinking of it. She liked\r\nthe name, and she liked still more the feeling that when its beautiful\r\nold walls shut her in no one knew where she was. It seemed almost like\r\nbeing shut out of the world in some fairy place. The few books she had\r\nread and liked had been fairy-story books, and she had read of secret\r\ngardens in some of the stories. Sometimes people went to sleep in them\r\nfor a hundred years, which she had thought must be rather stupid. She\r\nhad no intention of going to sleep, and, in fact, she was becoming\r\nwider awake every day which passed at Misselthwaite. She was beginning\r\nto like to be out of doors; she no longer hated the wind, but enjoyed\r\nit. She could run faster, and longer, and she could skip up to a\r\nhundred. The bulbs in the secret garden must have been much astonished.\r\nSuch nice clear places were made round them that they had all the\r\nbreathing space they wanted, and really, if Mistress Mary had known it,\r\nthey began to cheer up under the dark earth and work tremendously. The\r\nsun could get at them and warm them, and when the rain came down it\r\ncould reach them at once, so they began to feel very much alive.\r\n\r\nMary was an odd, determined little person, and now she had something\r\ninteresting to be determined about, she was very much absorbed, indeed.\r\nShe worked and dug and pulled up weeds steadily, only becoming more\r\npleased with her work every hour instead of tiring of it. It seemed to\r\nher like a fascinating sort of play. She found many more of the\r\nsprouting pale green points than she had ever hoped to find. They\r\nseemed to be starting up everywhere and each day she was sure she found\r\ntiny new ones, some so tiny that they barely peeped above the earth.\r\nThere were so many that she remembered what Martha had said about the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9csnowdrops by the thousands,\xe2\x80\x9d and about bulbs spreading and making new\r\nones. These had been left to themselves for ten years and perhaps they\r\nhad spread, like the snowdrops, into thousands. She wondered how long\r\nit would be before they showed that they were flowers. Sometimes she\r\nstopped digging to look at the garden and try to imagine what it would\r\nbe like when it was covered with thousands of lovely things in bloom.\r\n\r\nDuring that week of sunshine, she became more intimate with Ben\r\nWeatherstaff. She surprised him several times by seeming to start up\r\nbeside him as if she sprang out of the earth. The truth was that she\r\nwas afraid that he would pick up his tools and go away if he saw her\r\ncoming, so she always walked toward him as silently as possible. But,\r\nin fact, he did not object to her as strongly as he had at first.\r\nPerhaps he was secretly rather flattered by her evident desire for his\r\nelderly company. Then, also, she was more civil than she had been.
