He\r\ndid not know that when she first saw him she spoke to him as she would\r\nhave spoken to a native, and had not known that a cross, sturdy old\r\nYorkshire man was not accustomed to salaam to his masters, and be\r\nmerely commanded by them to do things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99rt like th\xe2\x80\x99 robin,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her one morning when he lifted his\r\nhead and saw her standing by him. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knows when I shall see thee\r\nor which side tha\xe2\x80\x99ll come from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s friends with me now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s like him,\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Ben Weatherstaff. \xe2\x80\x9cMakin\xe2\x80\x99 up to th\xe2\x80\x99 women\r\nfolk just for vanity an\xe2\x80\x99 flightiness. She had\r\nnever thought of this. She felt miserable. And she felt as if she went\r\nred and then pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99s got a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 garden, hasn\xe2\x80\x99t tha\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon said.\r\n\r\nIt was true that she had turned red and then pale. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nanybody\xe2\x80\x99s. Nobody wants it, nobody cares for it, nobody ever goes into\r\nit. Perhaps everything is dead in it already. I was only just like the robin, and\r\nthey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take it from the robin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dickon in a dropped voice.\r\n\r\nMistress Mary got up from the log at once. She knew she felt contrary\r\nagain, and obstinate, and she did not care at all. She was imperious\r\nand Indian, and at the same time hot and sorrowful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome with me and I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nShe led him round the laurel path and to the walk where the ivy grew so\r\nthickly. It\xe2\x80\x99s like as\r\nif a body was in a dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\nTHE NEST OF THE MISSEL THRUSH\r\n\r\n\r\nFor two or three minutes he stood looking round him, while Mary watched\r\nhim, and then he began to walk about softly, even more lightly than\r\nMary had walked the first time she had found herself inside the four\r\nwalls. His eyes seemed to be taking in everything\xe2\x80\x94the gray trees with\r\nthe gray creepers climbing over them and hanging from their branches,\r\nthe tangle on the walls and among the grass, the evergreen alcoves with\r\nthe stone seats and tall flower urns standing in them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought I\xe2\x80\x99d see this place,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\nShe had spoken aloud and he made a sign to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must talk low,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cor someone\xe2\x80\x99ll hear us an\xe2\x80\x99 wonder what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nto do in here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Seems to me as if\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d been a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 prunin\xe2\x80\x99 done here an\xe2\x80\x99 there, later than ten year\xe2\x80\x99\r\nago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how could it have been done?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nHe was examining a branch of a standard rose and he shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye! how could it!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWith th\xe2\x80\x99 door locked an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 key\r\nburied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMistress Mary always felt that however many years she lived she should\r\nnever forget that first morning when her garden began to grow. Of\r\ncourse, it did seem to begin to grow for her that morning. When Dickon\r\nbegan to clear places to plant seeds, she remembered what Basil had\r\nsung at her when he wanted to tease her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there any flowers that look like bells?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLilies o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 valley does,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, digging away with the trowel,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99s Canterbury bells, an\xe2\x80\x99 campanulas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s plant some,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s lilies o\xe2\x80\x99 th, valley here already; I saw \xe2\x80\x99em. They\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\ngrowed too close an\xe2\x80\x99 we\xe2\x80\x99ll have to separate \xe2\x80\x99em, but there\xe2\x80\x99s plenty.\r\nTh\xe2\x80\x99 other ones takes two years to bloom from seed, but I can bring you\r\nsome bits o\xe2\x80\x99 plants from our cottage garden. Why does tha\xe2\x80\x99 want \xe2\x80\x99em?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mary told him about Basil and his brothers and sisters in India\r\nand of how she had hated them and of their calling her \xe2\x80\x9cMistress Mary\r\nQuite Contrary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey used to dance round and sing at me. They sang\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mistress Mary, quite contrary,\r\n    How does your garden grow?\r\nWith silver bells, and cockle shells,\r\n    And marigolds all in a row.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI just remembered it and it made me wonder if there were really flowers\r\nlike silver bells.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe frowned a little and gave her trowel a rather spiteful dig into the\r\nearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t as contrary as they were.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Dickon laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and as he crumbled the rich black soil she saw he was\r\nsniffing up the scent of it. \xe2\x80\x9cThere doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be no need for no\r\none to be contrary when there\xe2\x80\x99s flowers an\xe2\x80\x99 such like, an\xe2\x80\x99 such lots o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfriendly wild things runnin\xe2\x80\x99 about makin\xe2\x80\x99 homes for themselves, or\r\nbuildin\xe2\x80\x99 nests an\xe2\x80\x99 singin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 whistlin\xe2\x80\x99, does there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary, kneeling by him holding the seeds, looked at him and stopped\r\nfrowning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDickon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are as nice as Martha said you were. I like\r\nyou, and you make the fifth person. I never thought I should like five\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon sat up on his heels as Martha did when she was polishing the\r\ngrate. He did look funny and delightful, Mary thought, with his round\r\nblue eyes and red cheeks and happy looking turned-up nose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly five folk as tha\xe2\x80\x99 likes?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is th\xe2\x80\x99 other four?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother and Martha,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary checked them off on her fingers, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nthe robin and Ben Weatherstaff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon laughed so that he was obliged to stifle the sound by putting\r\nhis arm over his mouth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know tha\xe2\x80\x99 thinks I\xe2\x80\x99m a queer lad,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think tha\xe2\x80\x99 art\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 queerest little lass I ever saw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mary did a strange thing. She leaned forward and asked him a\r\nquestion she had never dreamed of asking anyone before. And she tried\r\nto ask it in Yorkshire because that was his language, and in India a\r\nnative was always pleased if you knew his speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes tha\xe2\x80\x99 like me?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered heartily, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I does. Mary\r\nwas startled and sorry when she heard the big clock in the courtyard\r\nstrike the hour of her midday dinner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to go,\xe2\x80\x9d she said mournfully. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will have to go\r\ntoo, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon grinned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dinner\xe2\x80\x99s easy to carry about with me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMother always lets\r\nme put a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 somethin\xe2\x80\x99 in my pocket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe picked up his coat from the grass and brought out of a pocket a\r\nlumpy little bundle tied up in a quite clean, coarse, blue and white\r\nhandkerchief. It held two thick pieces of bread with a slice of\r\nsomething laid between them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s oftenest naught but bread,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a fine slice\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 fat bacon with it today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary thought it looked a queer dinner, but he seemed ready to enjoy it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun on an\xe2\x80\x99 get thy victuals,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be done with mine first.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll get some more work done before I start back home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat down with his back against a tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll call th\xe2\x80\x99 robin up,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand give him th\xe2\x80\x99 rind o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 bacon\r\nto peck at. They likes a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 fat wonderful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary could scarcely bear to leave him. Suddenly it seemed as if he\r\nmight be a sort of wood fairy who might be gone when she came into the\r\ngarden again. He seemed too good to be true. She went slowly half-way\r\nto the door in the wall and then she stopped and went back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever happens, you\xe2\x80\x94you never would tell?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHis poppy-colored cheeks were distended with his first big bite of\r\nbread and bacon, but he managed to smile encouragingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf tha\xe2\x80\x99 was a missel thrush an\xe2\x80\x99 showed me where thy nest was, does\r\ntha\xe2\x80\x99 think I\xe2\x80\x99d tell anyone? Not me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 art as safe as a\r\nmissel thrush.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd she was quite sure she was.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMIGHT I HAVE A BIT OF EARTH?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nMary ran so fast that she was rather out of breath when she reached her\r\nroom. How did tha\xe2\x80\x99 like th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nseeds an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 garden tools?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you know he brought them?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! He\xe2\x80\x99s such a trusty lad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary was afraid that she might begin to ask difficult questions, but\r\nshe did not. She was very much interested in the seeds and gardening\r\ntools, and there was only one moment when Mary was frightened. This was\r\nwhen she began to ask where the flowers were to be planted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho did tha\xe2\x80\x99 ask about it?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t asked anybody yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, hesitating.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask th\xe2\x80\x99 head gardener. He\xe2\x80\x99s too grand, Mr. Roach is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve only seen undergardeners and\r\nBen Weatherstaff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I was you, I\xe2\x80\x99d ask Ben Weatherstaff,\xe2\x80\x9d advised Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\nhalf as bad as he looks, for all he\xe2\x80\x99s so crabbed. Perhaps he\xe2\x80\x99d find you a corner\r\nsomewhere out o\xe2\x80\x99 the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it was out of the way and no one wanted it, no one _could_ mind my\r\nhaving it, could they?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no reason,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do no\r\nharm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary ate her dinner as quickly as she could and when she rose from the\r\ntable she was going to run to her room to put on her hat again, but\r\nMartha stopped her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got somethin\xe2\x80\x99 to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I\xe2\x80\x99d let you eat\r\nyour dinner first. Mr. Craven came back this mornin\xe2\x80\x99 and I think he\r\nwants to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary turned quite pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy! Why! He didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see me when I came. I\r\nheard Pitcher say he didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Martha, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Medlock says it\xe2\x80\x99s because o\xe2\x80\x99 mother.\r\nShe was walkin\xe2\x80\x99 to Thwaite village an\xe2\x80\x99 she met him. She\xe2\x80\x99d never spoke\r\nto him before, but Mrs. Craven had been to our cottage two or three\r\ntimes. He\xe2\x80\x99d forgot, but mother hadn\xe2\x80\x99t an\xe2\x80\x99 she made bold to stop him. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what she said to him about you but she said somethin\xe2\x80\x99 as put\r\nhim in th\xe2\x80\x99 mind to see you before he goes away again, tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cis he going away tomorrow? I am so glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 for a long time. He mayn\xe2\x80\x99t come back till autumn or winter.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to travel in foreign places. He\xe2\x80\x99s always doin\xe2\x80\x99 it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Even if he found out then\r\nand took it away from her she would have had that much at least.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen do you think he will want to see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not finish the sentence, because the door opened, and Mrs.\r\nMedlock walked in. She had on her best black dress and cap, and her\r\ncollar was fastened with a large brooch with a picture of a man\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\non it. It was a colored photograph of Mr. Medlock who had died years\r\nago, and she always wore it when she was dressed up. She looked nervous\r\nand excited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour hair\xe2\x80\x99s rough,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cGo and brush it. Martha, help\r\nher to slip on her best dress. Mr. Craven sent me to bring her to him\r\nin his study.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the pink left Mary\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks. Her heart began to thump and she felt\r\nherself changing into a stiff, plain, silent child again. She did not\r\neven answer Mrs. Medlock, but turned and walked into her bedroom,\r\nfollowed by Martha. She said nothing while her dress was changed, and\r\nher hair brushed, and after she was quite tidy she followed Mrs.\r\nMedlock down the corridors, in silence. What was there for her to say?\r\nShe was obliged to go and see Mr. Craven and he would not like her, and\r\nshe would not like him. She knew what he would think of her.\r\n\r\nShe was taken to a part of the house she had not been into before. At\r\nlast Mrs. Medlock knocked at a door, and when someone said, \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthey entered the room together. A man was sitting in an armchair before\r\nthe fire, and Mrs. Medlock spoke to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is Miss Mary, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can go and leave her here. I will ring for you when I want you to\r\ntake her away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Craven.\r\n\r\nWhen she went out and closed the door, Mary could only stand waiting, a\r\nplain little thing, twisting her thin hands together. She could see\r\nthat the man in the chair was not so much a hunchback as a man with\r\nhigh, rather crooked shoulders, and he had black hair streaked with\r\nwhite. He turned his head over his high shoulders and spoke to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMary went to him.\r\n\r\nHe was not ugly. His face would have been handsome if it had not been\r\nso miserable. He looked as if the sight of her worried and fretted him\r\nand as if he did not know what in the world to do with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you well?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they take good care of you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rubbed his forehead fretfully as he looked her over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very thin,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am getting fatter,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary answered in what she knew was her stiffest\r\nway.\r\n\r\nWhat an unhappy face he had! His black eyes seemed as if they scarcely\r\nsaw her, as if they were seeing something else, and he could hardly\r\nkeep his thoughts upon her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgot you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHow could I remember you? I intended to send\r\nyou a governess or a nurse, or someone of that sort, but I forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease,\xe2\x80\x9d began Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and then the lump in her throat choked\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want to say?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am\xe2\x80\x94I am too big for a nurse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd please\xe2\x80\x94please don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake me have a governess yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rubbed his forehead again and stared at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was what the Sowerby woman said,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered absent-mindedly.\r\n\r\nThen Mary gathered a scrap of courage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she\xe2\x80\x94is she Martha\xe2\x80\x99s mother?\xe2\x80\x9d she stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think so,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knows about children,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has twelve. She knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed to rouse himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to play out of doors,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary answered, hoping that her voice did\r\nnot tremble. \xe2\x80\x9cI never liked it in India. It makes me hungry here, and I\r\nam getting fatter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was watching her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Sowerby said it would do you good. Perhaps it will,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe thought you had better get stronger before you had a governess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt makes me feel strong when I play and the wind comes over the moor,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nargued Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you play?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked next.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cMartha\xe2\x80\x99s mother sent me a skipping-rope. I\r\nskip and run\xe2\x80\x94and I look about to see if things are beginning to stick\r\nup out of the earth. I don\xe2\x80\x99t do any harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t look so frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a worried voice. \xe2\x80\x9cYou could not\r\ndo any harm, a child like you! You may do what you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary put her hand up to her throat because she was afraid he might see\r\nthe excited lump which she felt jump into it. She came a step nearer to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I?\xe2\x80\x9d she said tremulously.\r\n\r\nHer anxious little face seemed to worry him more than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t look so frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course you may. I am your\r\nguardian, though I am a poor one for any child. I cannot give you time\r\nor attention. I am too ill, and wretched and distracted; but I wish you\r\nto be happy and comfortable. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about children, but\r\nMrs. Medlock is to see that you have all you need. I sent for you today\r\nbecause Mrs. Sowerby said I ought to see you. Her daughter had talked\r\nabout you. She thought you needed fresh air and freedom and running\r\nabout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knows all about children,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said again in spite of herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe ought to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Craven. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought her rather bold to stop me\r\non the moor, but she said\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Craven had been kind to her.\xe2\x80\x9d It seemed\r\nhard for him to speak his dead wife\xe2\x80\x99s name. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is a respectable\r\nwoman. Mr. Craven looked\r\nquite startled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEarth!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo plant seeds in\xe2\x80\x94to make things grow\xe2\x80\x94to see them come alive,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\r\nfaltered.\r\n\r\nHe gazed at her a moment and then passed his hand quickly over his\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you\xe2\x80\x94care about gardens so much,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know about them in India,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI was always ill and\r\ntired and it was too hot. I sometimes made little beds in the sand and\r\nstuck flowers in them. But here it is different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Craven got up and began to walk slowly across the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bit of earth,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, and Mary thought that somehow she\r\nmust have reminded him of something. When you see a\r\nbit of earth you want,\xe2\x80\x9d with something like a smile, \xe2\x80\x9ctake it, child,\r\nand make it come alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I take it from anywhere\xe2\x80\x94if it\xe2\x80\x99s not wanted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThere! I shall be away all\r\nsummer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Medlock came so quickly that Mary thought she must have been\r\nwaiting in the corridor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Medlock,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Craven said to her, \xe2\x80\x9cnow I have seen the child I\r\nunderstand what Mrs. Sowerby meant. She must be less delicate before\r\nshe begins lessons. Give her simple, healthy food. Let her run wild in\r\nthe garden. Don\xe2\x80\x99t look after her too much. She needs liberty and fresh\r\nair and romping about. Mrs. Sowerby is to come and see her now and then\r\nand she may sometimes go to the cottage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Medlock looked pleased. She was relieved to hear that she need not\r\n\xe2\x80\x9clook after\xe2\x80\x9d Mary too much. She had felt her a tiresome charge and had\r\nindeed seen as little of her as she dared. In addition to this she was\r\nfond of Martha\xe2\x80\x99s mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cSusan Sowerby and me went to school\r\ntogether and she\xe2\x80\x99s as sensible and good-hearted a woman as you\xe2\x80\x99d find\r\nin a day\xe2\x80\x99s walk. I never had any children myself and she\xe2\x80\x99s had twelve,\r\nand there never was healthier or better ones. Miss Mary can get no harm\r\nfrom them. I\xe2\x80\x99d always take Susan Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s advice about children\r\nmyself. She\xe2\x80\x99s what you might call healthy-minded\xe2\x80\x94if you understand me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Craven answered. \xe2\x80\x9cTake Miss Mary away now and send\r\nPitcher to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Mrs. Medlock left her at the end of her own corridor Mary flew\r\nback to her room. She found Martha waiting there. Martha had, in fact,\r\nhurried back after she had removed the dinner service.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can have my garden!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI may have it where I like! I am\r\nnot going to have a governess for a long time! Your mother is coming to\r\nsee me and I may go to your cottage! He says a little girl like me\r\ncould not do any harm and I may do what I like\xe2\x80\x94anywhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha delightedly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat was nice of him wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMartha,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary solemnly, \xe2\x80\x9che is really a nice man, only his face\r\nis so miserable and his forehead is all drawn together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe ran as quickly as she could to the garden. She had been away so\r\nmuch longer than she had thought she should and she knew Dickon would\r\nhave to set out early on his five-mile walk. When she slipped through\r\nthe door under the ivy, she saw he was not working where she had left\r\nhim. The gardening tools were laid together under a tree. She ran to\r\nthem, looking all round the place, but there was no Dickon to be seen.\r\nHe had gone away and the secret garden was empty\xe2\x80\x94except for the robin\r\nwho had just flown across the wall and sat on a standard rose-bush\r\nwatching her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone,\xe2\x80\x9d she said woefully. \xe2\x80\x9cOh! was he\xe2\x80\x94was he\xe2\x80\x94was he only a wood\r\nfairy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething white fastened to the standard rose-bush caught her eye. It\r\nwas a piece of paper, in fact, it was a piece of the letter she had\r\nprinted for Martha to send to Dickon. It was fastened on the bush with\r\na long thorn, and in a minute she knew Dickon had left it there. There\r\nwere some roughly printed letters on it and a sort of picture. At first\r\nshe could not tell what it was. Then she saw it was meant for a nest\r\nwith a bird sitting on it. Underneath were the printed letters and they\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will cum bak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI AM COLIN\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nMary took the picture back to the house when she went to her supper and\r\nshe showed it to Martha.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha with great pride. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knew our Dickon was as\r\nclever as that. That there\xe2\x80\x99s a picture of a missel thrush on her nest,\r\nas large as life an\xe2\x80\x99 twice as natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mary knew Dickon had meant the picture to be a message. He had\r\nmeant that she might be sure he would keep her secret. Her garden was\r\nher nest and she was like a missel thrush. Oh, how she did like that\r\nqueer, common boy!\r\n\r\nShe hoped he would come back the very next day and she fell asleep\r\nlooking forward to the morning.\r\n\r\nBut you never know what the weather will do in Yorkshire, particularly\r\nin the springtime. She was awakened in the night by the sound of rain\r\nbeating with heavy drops against her window. It was pouring down in\r\ntorrents and the wind was \xe2\x80\x9cwuthering\xe2\x80\x9d round the corners and in the\r\nchimneys of the huge old house. Mary sat up in bed and felt miserable\r\nand angry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rain is as contrary as I ever was,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt came because it\r\nknew I did not want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe threw herself back on her pillow and buried her face. She did not\r\ncry, but she lay and hated the sound of the heavily beating rain, she\r\nhated the wind and its \xe2\x80\x9cwuthering.\xe2\x80\x9d She could not go to sleep again.\r\nThe mournful sound kept her awake because she felt mournful herself. If\r\nshe had felt happy it would probably have lulled her to sleep. How it\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwuthered\xe2\x80\x9d and how the big raindrops poured down and beat against the\r\npane!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds just like a person lost on the moor and wandering on and on\r\ncrying,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nShe had been lying awake turning from side to side for about an hour,\r\nwhen suddenly something made her sit up in bed and turn her head toward\r\nthe door listening. She listened and she listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t the wind now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a loud whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cThat isn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nwind. It is different. It is that crying I heard before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door of her room was ajar and the sound came down the corridor, a\r\nfar-off faint sound of fretful crying. She listened for a few minutes\r\nand each minute she became more and more sure. She felt as if she must\r\nfind out what it was. It seemed even stranger than the secret garden\r\nand the buried key. Perhaps the fact that she was in a rebellious mood\r\nmade her bold. She put her foot out of bed and stood on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to find out what it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cEverybody is in bed and\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t care about Mrs. Medlock\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t care!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a candle by her bedside and she took it up and went softly\r\nout of the room. The corridor looked very long and dark, but she was\r\ntoo excited to mind that. She thought she remembered the corners she\r\nmust turn to find the short corridor with the door covered with\r\ntapestry\xe2\x80\x94the one Mrs. Medlock had come through the day she lost\r\nherself. The sound had come up that passage. So she went on with her\r\ndim light, almost feeling her way, her heart beating so loud that she\r\nfancied she could hear it. The far-off faint crying went on and led\r\nher. Sometimes it stopped for a moment or so and then began again. Was\r\nthis the right corner to turn? She stopped and thought. Yes it was.\r\nDown this passage and then to the left, and then up two broad steps,\r\nand then to the right again. Yes, there was the tapestry door.\r\n\r\nShe pushed it open very gently and closed it behind her, and she stood\r\nin the corridor and could hear the crying quite plainly, though it was\r\nnot loud. It was on the other side of the wall at her left and a few\r\nyards farther on there was a door. She could see a glimmer of light\r\ncoming from beneath it. The Someone was crying in that room, and it was\r\nquite a young Someone.\r\n\r\nSo she walked to the door and pushed it open, and there she was\r\nstanding in the room!\r\n\r\nIt was a big room with ancient, handsome furniture in it. There was a\r\nlow fire glowing faintly on the hearth and a night light burning by the\r\nside of a carved four-posted bed hung with brocade, and on the bed was\r\nlying a boy, crying fretfully.\r\n\r\nMary wondered if she was in a real place or if she had fallen asleep\r\nagain and was dreaming without knowing it.\r\n\r\nThe boy had a sharp, delicate face the color of ivory and he seemed to\r\nhave eyes too big for it. He had also a lot of hair which tumbled over\r\nhis forehead in heavy locks and made his thin face seem smaller. He\r\nlooked like a boy who had been ill, but he was crying more as if he\r\nwere tired and cross than as if he were in pain.\r\n\r\nMary stood near the door with her candle in her hand, holding her\r\nbreath. Then she crept across the room, and, as she drew nearer, the\r\nlight attracted the boy\xe2\x80\x99s attention and he turned his head on his\r\npillow and stared at her, his gray eyes opening so wide that they\r\nseemed immense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last in a half-frightened whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you a\r\nghost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am not,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary answered, her own whisper sounding half\r\nfrightened. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stared and stared and stared. Mary could not help noticing what\r\nstrange eyes he had. They were agate gray and they looked too big for\r\nhis face because they had black lashes all round them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied after waiting a moment or so. \xe2\x80\x9cI am Colin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Colin?\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Colin Craven. Who are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Mary Lennox. Why\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, still keeping his strange eyes fixed on her with\r\nan anxious expression.\r\n\r\nShe came close to the bed and he put out his hand and touched her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are real, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have such real dreams very\r\noften. You might be one of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had slipped on a woolen wrapper before she left her room and she\r\nput a piece of it between his fingers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRub that and see how thick and warm it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI will pinch\r\nyou a little if you like, to show you how real I am. For a minute I\r\nthought you might be a dream too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you come from?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom my own room. The wind wuthered so I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go to sleep and I\r\nheard someone crying and wanted to find out who it was. What were you\r\ncrying for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go to sleep either and my head ached. Tell me your\r\nname again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary Lennox. I won\xe2\x80\x99t let people\r\nsee me and talk me over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary asked again, feeling more mystified every moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I am like this always, ill and having to lie down. My father\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t let people talk me over either. The servants are not allowed to\r\nspeak about me. If I live I may be a hunchback, but I shan\xe2\x80\x99t live. My\r\nfather hates to think I may be like him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what a queer house this is!\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a queer house!\r\nEverything is a kind of secret. Have you been locked up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I stay in this room because I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be moved out of it. It\r\ntires me too much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes your father come and see you?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary ventured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes. Generally when I am asleep. I used to wear an iron\r\nthing to keep my back straight, but a grand doctor came from London to\r\nsee me and said it was stupid. He told them to take it off and keep me\r\nout in the fresh air. I hate fresh air and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t when first I came here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you keep looking\r\nat me like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause of the dreams that are so real,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered rather fretfully.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes when I open my eyes I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I\xe2\x80\x99m awake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re both awake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. If you\r\nare real, sit down on that big footstool and talk. I want to hear about\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary put down her candle on the table near the bed and sat down on the\r\ncushioned stool. She did not want to go away at all. She wanted to stay\r\nin the mysterious hidden-away room and talk to the mysterious boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want me to tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe wanted to know how long she had been at Misselthwaite; he wanted to\r\nknow which corridor her room was on; he wanted to know what she had\r\nbeen doing; if she disliked the moor as he disliked it; where she had\r\nlived before she came to Yorkshire. She answered all these questions\r\nand many more and he lay back on his pillow and listened. He made her\r\ntell him a great deal about India and about her voyage across the\r\nocean. She found out that because he had been an invalid he had not\r\nlearned things as other children had. One of his nurses had taught him\r\nto read when he was quite little and he was always reading and looking\r\nat pictures in splendid books.\r\n\r\nThough his father rarely saw him when he was awake, he was given all\r\nsorts of wonderful things to amuse himself with. He never seemed to\r\nhave been amused, however. He could have anything he asked for and was\r\nnever made to do anything he did not like to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone is obliged to do what pleases me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said indifferently. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nmakes me ill to be angry. No one believes I shall live to grow up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said it as if he was so accustomed to the idea that it had ceased to\r\nmatter to him at all. He seemed to like the sound of Mary\xe2\x80\x99s voice. As\r\nshe went on talking he listened in a drowsy, interested way. Once or\r\ntwice she wondered if he were not gradually falling into a doze. But at\r\nlast he asked a question which opened up a new subject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow old are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ten,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary, forgetting herself for the moment, \xe2\x80\x9cand so\r\nare you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded in a surprised voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause when you were born the garden door was locked and the key was\r\nburied. And it has been locked for ten years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin half sat up, turning toward her, leaning on his elbows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat garden door was locked? Who did it? Where was the key buried?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed as if he were suddenly very much interested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x94it was the garden Mr. Craven hates,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary nervously. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nlocked the door. No one\xe2\x80\x94no one knew where he buried the key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a garden is it?\xe2\x80\x9d Colin persisted eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one has been allowed to go into it for ten years,\xe2\x80\x9d was Mary\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncareful answer.\r\n\r\nBut it was too late to be careful. He was too much like herself. He too\r\nhad had nothing to think about and the idea of a hidden garden\r\nattracted him as it had attracted her. He asked question after\r\nquestion. Where was it? Had she never looked for the door? Had she\r\nnever asked the gardeners?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey won\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI think they have been told not\r\nto answer questions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would make them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary faltered, beginning to feel frightened. If he could\r\nmake people answer questions, who knew what might happen!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone is obliged to please me. I told you that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\r\nwere to live, this place would sometime belong to me. They all know\r\nthat. I would make them tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had not known that she herself had been spoiled, but she could see\r\nquite plainly that this mysterious boy had been. He thought that the\r\nwhole world belonged to him. How peculiar he was and how coolly he\r\nspoke of not living.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think you won\xe2\x80\x99t live?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, partly because she was\r\ncurious and partly in hope of making him forget the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I shall,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered as indifferently as he had\r\nspoken before. \xe2\x80\x9cEver since I remember anything I have heard people say\r\nI shan\xe2\x80\x99t. At first they thought I was too little to understand and now\r\nthey think I don\xe2\x80\x99t hear. But I do. My doctor is my father\xe2\x80\x99s cousin. He\r\nis quite poor and if I die he will have all Misselthwaite when my\r\nfather is dead. I should think he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want me to live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to live?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, in a cross, tired fashion. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to die.\r\nWhen I feel ill I lie here and think about it until I cry and cry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard you crying three times,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I did not know\r\nwho it was. Were you crying about that?\xe2\x80\x9d She did so want him to forget\r\nthe garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us talk about something else. Talk\r\nabout that garden. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you want to see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary, in quite a low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on persistently. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever really wanted to\r\nsee anything before, but I want to see that garden. I want the key dug\r\nup. I want the door unlocked. I would let them take me there in my\r\nchair. That would be getting fresh air. I am going to make them open\r\nthe door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had become quite excited and his strange eyes began to shine like\r\nstars and looked more immense than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have to please me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI will make them take me there and\r\nI will let you go, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s hands clutched each other. Everything would be\r\nspoiled\xe2\x80\x94everything! Dickon would never come back. She would never again\r\nfeel like a missel thrush with a safe-hidden nest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t do that!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out.\r\n\r\nHe stared as if he thought she had gone crazy!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou said you wanted to see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered almost with a sob in her throat, \xe2\x80\x9cbut if you make\r\nthem open the door and take you in like that it will never be a secret\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe leaned still farther forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA secret,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean? Tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s words almost tumbled over one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see\xe2\x80\x94you see,\xe2\x80\x9d she panted, \xe2\x80\x9cif no one knows but ourselves\xe2\x80\x94if there\r\nwas a door, hidden somewhere under the ivy\xe2\x80\x94if there was\xe2\x80\x94and we could\r\nfind it; and if we could slip through it together and shut it behind\r\nus, and no one knew anyone was inside and we called it our garden and\r\npretended that\xe2\x80\x94that we were missel thrushes and it was our nest, and if\r\nwe played there almost every day and dug and planted seeds and made it\r\nall come alive\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it dead?\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt soon will be if no one cares for it,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThe bulbs will\r\nlive but the roses\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped her again as excited as she was herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are bulbs?\xe2\x80\x9d he put in quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are daffodils and lilies and snowdrops. They are working in the\r\nearth now\xe2\x80\x94pushing up pale green points because the spring is coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the spring coming?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it like? You don\xe2\x80\x99t see it in\r\nrooms if you are ill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the sun shining on the rain and the rain falling on the\r\nsunshine, and things pushing up and working under the earth,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMary. \xe2\x80\x9cIf the garden was a secret and we could get into it we could\r\nwatch the things grow bigger every day, and see how many roses are\r\nalive. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see? Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see how much nicer it would be if\r\nit was a secret?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dropped back on his pillow and lay there with an odd expression on\r\nhis face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never had a secret,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept that one about not living to\r\ngrow up. They don\xe2\x80\x99t know I know that, so it is a sort of secret. But I\r\nlike this kind better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you won\xe2\x80\x99t make them take you to the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Mary,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cperhaps\xe2\x80\x94I feel almost sure I can find out how to get in sometime. And\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94if the doctor wants you to go out in your chair, and if you can\r\nalways do what you want to do, perhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps we might find some boy\r\nwho would push you, and we could go alone and it would always be a\r\nsecret garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should\xe2\x80\x94like\xe2\x80\x94that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said very slowly, his eyes looking dreamy. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshould like that. I should not mind fresh air in a secret garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary began to recover her breath and feel safer because the idea of\r\nkeeping the secret seemed to please him. She felt almost sure that if\r\nshe kept on talking and could make him see the garden in his mind as\r\nshe had seen it he would like it so much that he could not bear to\r\nthink that everybody might tramp in to it when they chose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I _think_ it would be like, if we could go into\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt has been shut up so long things have grown into a\r\ntangle perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lay quite still and listened while she went on talking about the\r\nroses which _might_ have clambered from tree to tree and hung\r\ndown\xe2\x80\x94about the many birds which _might_ have built their nests there\r\nbecause it was so safe. And then she told him about the robin and Ben\r\nWeatherstaff, and there was so much to tell about the robin and it was\r\nso easy and safe to talk about it that she ceased to be afraid. The\r\nrobin pleased him so much that he smiled until he looked almost\r\nbeautiful, and at first Mary had thought that he was even plainer than\r\nherself, with his big eyes and heavy locks of hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not know birds could be like that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you stay in\r\na room you never see things. What a lot of things you know. I feel as\r\nif you had been inside that garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not know what to say, so she did not say anything. He evidently\r\ndid not expect an answer and the next moment he gave her a surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to let you look at something,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you see that\r\nrose-colored silk curtain hanging on the wall over the mantel-piece?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had not noticed it before, but she looked up and saw it. It was a\r\ncurtain of soft silk hanging over what seemed to be some picture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a cord hanging from it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cGo and pull it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary got up, much mystified, and found the cord. When she pulled it the\r\nsilk curtain ran back on rings and when it ran back it uncovered a\r\npicture. It was the picture of a girl with a laughing face. She had\r\nbright hair tied up with a blue ribbon and her gay, lovely eyes were\r\nexactly like Colin\xe2\x80\x99s unhappy ones, agate gray and looking twice as big\r\nas they really were because of the black lashes all round them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is my mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin complainingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why she\r\ndied. Sometimes I hate her for doing it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow queer!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she had lived I believe I should not have been ill always,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ngrumbled. \xe2\x80\x9cI dare say I should have lived, too. And my father would not\r\nhave hated to look at me. I dare say I should have had a strong back.\r\nDraw the curtain again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary did as she was told and returned to her footstool.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is much prettier than you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut her eyes are just like\r\nyours\xe2\x80\x94at least they are the same shape and color. Why is the curtain\r\ndrawn over her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved uncomfortably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI made them do it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to see her\r\nlooking at me. She smiles too much when I am ill and miserable.\r\nBesides, she is mine and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want everyone to see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere were a few moments of silence and then Mary spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat would Mrs. Medlock do if she found out that I had been here?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would do as I told her to do,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I should tell her\r\nthat I wanted you to come here and talk to me every day. I am glad you\r\ncame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI will come as often as I can, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nhesitated\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to look every day for the garden door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you must,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, \xe2\x80\x9cand you can tell me about it afterward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lay thinking a few minutes, as he had done before, and then he spoke\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you shall be a secret, too,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI will not tell them\r\nuntil they find out. I can always send the nurse out of the room and\r\nsay that I want to be by myself. Do you know Martha?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know her very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cShe waits on me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe nodded his head toward the outer corridor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the one who is asleep in the other room. The nurse went away\r\nyesterday to stay all night with her sister and she always makes Martha\r\nattend to me when she wants to go out. Martha shall tell you when to\r\ncome here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mary understood Martha\xe2\x80\x99s troubled look when she had asked\r\nquestions about the crying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMartha knew about you all the time?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; she often attends to me. The nurse likes to get away from me and\r\nthen Martha comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been here a long time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I go away now? Your\r\neyes look sleepy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could go to sleep before you leave me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said rather shyly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShut your eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, drawing her footstool closer, \xe2\x80\x9cand I will\r\ndo what my Ayah used to do in India. I will pat your hand and stroke it\r\nand sing something quite low.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like that perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d he said drowsily.\r\n\r\nSomehow she was sorry for him and did not want him to lie awake, so she\r\nleaned against the bed and began to stroke and pat his hand and sing a\r\nvery low little chanting song in Hindustani.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is nice,\xe2\x80\x9d he said more drowsily still, and she went on chanting\r\nand stroking, but when she looked at him again his black lashes were\r\nlying close against his cheeks, for his eyes were shut and he was fast\r\nasleep. So she got up softly, took her candle and crept away without\r\nmaking a sound.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\nA YOUNG RAJAH\r\n\r\n\r\nThe moor was hidden in mist when the morning came, and the rain had not\r\nstopped pouring down. There could be no going out of doors. Martha was\r\nso busy that Mary had no opportunity of talking to her, but in the\r\nafternoon she asked her to come and sit with her in the nursery. She\r\ncame bringing the stocking she was always knitting when she was doing\r\nnothing else.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with thee?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked as soon as they sat down.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 looks as if tha\xe2\x80\x99d somethin\xe2\x80\x99 to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have. I have found out what the crying was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nMartha let her knitting drop on her knee and gazed at her with startled\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 hasn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cNever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard it in the night,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary went on. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I got up and went to see\r\nwhere it came from. It was Colin. I found him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha\xe2\x80\x99s face became red with fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! Miss Mary!\xe2\x80\x9d she said half crying. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94tha\xe2\x80\x99 shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t! Tha\xe2\x80\x99ll get me in trouble. I never told thee nothin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nabout him\xe2\x80\x94but tha\xe2\x80\x99ll get me in trouble. I shall lose my place and\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99ll mother do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t lose your place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was glad I came. We talked\r\nand talked and he said he was glad I came.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas he?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cArt tha\xe2\x80\x99 sure? Tha\xe2\x80\x99 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know what he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlike when anything vexes him. He\xe2\x80\x99s a big lad to cry like a baby, but\r\nwhen he\xe2\x80\x99s in a passion he\xe2\x80\x99ll fair scream just to frighten us. He knows\r\nus daren\xe2\x80\x99t call our souls our own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t vexed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI asked him if I should go away and he\r\nmade me stay. He asked me questions and I sat on a big footstool and\r\ntalked to him about India and about the robin and gardens. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlet me go. He let me see his mother\xe2\x80\x99s picture. Before I left him I sang\r\nhim to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha fairly gasped with amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can scarcely believe thee!\xe2\x80\x9d she protested. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s as if tha\xe2\x80\x99d walked\r\nstraight into a lion\xe2\x80\x99s den. If he\xe2\x80\x99d been like he is most times he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nhave throwed himself into one of his tantrums and roused th\xe2\x80\x99 house. He\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t let strangers look at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe let me look at him. I looked at him all the time and he looked at\r\nme. We stared!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do!\xe2\x80\x9d cried agitated Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cIf Mrs. Medlock\r\nfinds out, she\xe2\x80\x99ll think I broke orders and told thee and I shall be\r\npacked back to mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not going to tell Mrs. Medlock anything about it yet. It\xe2\x80\x99s to be\r\na sort of secret just at first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he says\r\neverybody is obliged to do as he pleases.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that\xe2\x80\x99s true enough\xe2\x80\x94th\xe2\x80\x99 bad lad!\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Martha, wiping her\r\nforehead with her apron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says Mrs. Medlock must. And he wants me to come and talk to him\r\nevery day.
