And they laughed so that in the end they were making as much\r\nnoise as if they had been two ordinary healthy natural ten-year-old\r\ncreatures\xe2\x80\x94instead of a hard, little, unloving girl and a sickly boy who\r\nbelieved that he was going to die.\r\n\r\nThey enjoyed themselves so much that they forgot the pictures and they\r\nforgot about the time. They had been laughing quite loudly over Ben\r\nWeatherstaff and his robin, and Colin was actually sitting up as if he\r\nhad forgotten about his weak back, when he suddenly remembered\r\nsomething.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know there is one thing we have never once thought of,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are cousins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed so queer that they had talked so much and never remembered\r\nthis simple thing that they laughed more than ever, because they had\r\ngot into the humor to laugh at anything. And in the midst of the fun\r\nthe door opened and in walked Dr. Craven and Mrs. Medlock.\r\n\r\nDr. Craven started in actual alarm and Mrs. Medlock almost fell back\r\nbecause he had accidentally bumped against her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed poor Mrs. Medlock with her eyes almost starting\r\nout of her head. \xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven, coming forward. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mary was reminded of the boy Rajah again. Colin answered as if\r\nneither the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s alarm nor Mrs. Medlock\xe2\x80\x99s terror were of the\r\nslightest consequence. He was as little disturbed or frightened as if\r\nan elderly cat and dog had walked into the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my cousin, Mary Lennox,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI asked her to come and\r\ntalk to me. I like her. She must come and talk to me whenever I send\r\nfor her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven turned reproachfully to Mrs. Medlock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sir\xe2\x80\x9d she panted. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how it\xe2\x80\x99s happened. There\xe2\x80\x99s not a\r\nservant on the place tha\xe2\x80\x99d dare to talk\xe2\x80\x94they all have their orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody told her anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cShe heard me crying and found\r\nme herself. I am glad she came. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be silly, Medlock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary saw that Dr. Craven did not look pleased, but it was quite plain\r\nthat he dare not oppose his patient. He sat down by Colin and felt his\r\npulse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid there has been too much excitement. Excitement is not good\r\nfor you, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be excited if she kept away,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Colin, his eyes\r\nbeginning to look dangerously sparkling. \xe2\x80\x9cI am better. She makes me\r\nbetter. The nurse must bring up her tea with mine. We will have tea\r\ntogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Medlock and Dr. Craven looked at each other in a troubled way, but\r\nthere was evidently nothing to be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does look rather better, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d ventured Mrs. Medlock.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94thinking the matter over\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9che looked better this morning before\r\nshe came into the room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe came into the room last night. She stayed with me a long time. She\r\nsang a Hindustani song to me and it made me go to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was better when I wakened up. I wanted my breakfast. I want my tea\r\nnow. Tell nurse, Medlock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven did not stay very long. He talked to the nurse for a few\r\nminutes when she came into the room and said a few words of warning to\r\nColin. He must not talk too much; he must not forget that he was ill;\r\nhe must not forget that he was very easily tired. Mary thought that\r\nthere seemed to be a number of uncomfortable things he was not to\r\nforget.\r\n\r\nColin looked fretful and kept his strange black-lashed eyes fixed on\r\nDr. Craven\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _want_ to forget it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cShe makes me forget it. That\r\nis why I want her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven did not look happy when he left the room. He gave a puzzled\r\nglance at the little girl sitting on the large stool. She had become a\r\nstiff, silent child again as soon as he entered and he could not see\r\nwhat the attraction was. The boy actually did look brighter,\r\nhowever\xe2\x80\x94and he sighed rather heavily as he went down the corridor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are always wanting me to eat things when I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nColin, as the nurse brought in the tea and put it on the table by the\r\nsofa. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll eat I will. Those muffins look so nice and hot.\r\nTell me about Rajahs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\nNEST BUILDING\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter another week of rain the high arch of blue sky appeared again and\r\nthe sun which poured down was quite hot. Though there had been no\r\nchance to see either the secret garden or Dickon, Mistress Mary had\r\nenjoyed herself very much. The week had not seemed long. She had spent\r\nhours of every day with Colin in his room, talking about Rajahs or\r\ngardens or Dickon and the cottage on the moor. They had looked at the\r\nsplendid books and pictures and sometimes Mary had read things to\r\nColin, and sometimes he had read a little to her. When he was amused\r\nand interested she thought he scarcely looked like an invalid at all,\r\nexcept that his face was so colorless and he was always on the sofa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a sly young one to listen and get out of your bed to go\r\nfollowing things up like you did that night,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Medlock said once.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s no saying it\xe2\x80\x99s not been a sort of blessing to the lot of\r\nus. He\xe2\x80\x99s not had a tantrum or a whining fit since you made friends. The\r\nnurse was just going to give up the case because she was so sick of\r\nhim, but she says she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind staying now you\xe2\x80\x99ve gone on duty with\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d laughing a little.\r\n\r\nIn her talks with Colin, Mary had tried to be very cautious about the\r\nsecret garden. There were certain things she wanted to find out from\r\nhim, but she felt that she must find them out without asking him direct\r\nquestions. In the first place, as she began to like to be with him, she\r\nwanted to discover whether he was the kind of boy you could tell a\r\nsecret to. He was not in the least like Dickon, but he was evidently so\r\npleased with the idea of a garden no one knew anything about that she\r\nthought perhaps he could be trusted. But she had not known him long\r\nenough to be sure. The second thing she wanted to find out was this: If\r\nhe could be trusted\xe2\x80\x94if he really could\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it be possible to take\r\nhim to the garden without having anyone find it out? The grand doctor\r\nhad said that he must have fresh air and Colin had said that he would\r\nnot mind fresh air in a secret garden. Perhaps if he had a great deal\r\nof fresh air and knew Dickon and the robin and saw things growing he\r\nmight not think so much about dying. Mary had seen herself in the glass\r\nsometimes lately when she had realized that she looked quite a\r\ndifferent creature from the child she had seen when she arrived from\r\nIndia. This child looked nicer. Even Martha had seen a change in her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 air from th\xe2\x80\x99 moor has done thee good already,\xe2\x80\x9d she had said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99rt not nigh so yeller and tha\xe2\x80\x99rt not nigh so scrawny. Even tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhair doesn\xe2\x80\x99t slamp down on tha\xe2\x80\x99 head so flat. It\xe2\x80\x99s got some life in it\r\nso as it sticks out a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s like me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s growing stronger and fatter. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s more of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks it, for sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha, ruffling it up a little round her\r\nface. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99rt not half so ugly when it\xe2\x80\x99s that way an\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99s a bit o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nred in tha\xe2\x80\x99 cheeks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf gardens and fresh air had been good for her perhaps they would be\r\ngood for Colin. But then, if he hated people to look at him, perhaps he\r\nwould not like to see Dickon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy does it make you angry when you are looked at?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired one\r\nday.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always hated it,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9ceven when I was very little. Then\r\nwhen they took me to the seaside and I used to lie in my carriage\r\neverybody used to stare and ladies would stop and talk to my nurse and\r\nthen they would begin to whisper and I knew then they were saying I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t live to grow up. Then sometimes the ladies would pat my\r\ncheeks and say \xe2\x80\x98Poor child!\xe2\x80\x99 Once when a lady did that I screamed out\r\nloud and bit her hand. She was so frightened she ran away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe thought you had gone mad like a dog,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, not at all\r\nadmiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care what she thought,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, frowning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder why you didn\xe2\x80\x99t scream and bite me when I came into your\r\nroom?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. Then she began to smile slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you were a ghost or a dream,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t bite a\r\nghost or a dream, and if you scream they don\xe2\x80\x99t care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you hate it if\xe2\x80\x94if a boy looked at you?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary asked uncertainly.\r\n\r\nHe lay back on his cushion and paused thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s one boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quite slowly, as if he were thinking over\r\nevery word, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s one boy I believe I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind. It\xe2\x80\x99s that boy\r\nwho knows where the foxes live\xe2\x80\x94Dickon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe birds don\xe2\x80\x99t and other animals,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, still thinking it over,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cperhaps that\xe2\x80\x99s why I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. He\xe2\x80\x99s a sort of animal charmer and I am\r\na boy animal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he laughed and she laughed too; in fact it ended in their both\r\nlaughing a great deal and finding the idea of a boy animal hiding in\r\nhis hole very funny indeed.\r\n\r\nWhat Mary felt afterward was that she need not fear about Dickon.\r\n\r\nOn that first morning when the sky was blue again Mary wakened very\r\nearly. The sun was pouring in slanting rays through the blinds and\r\nthere was something so joyous in the sight of it that she jumped out of\r\nbed and ran to the window. She drew up the blinds and opened the window\r\nitself and a great waft of fresh, scented air blew in upon her. The\r\nmoor was blue and the whole world looked as if something Magic had\r\nhappened to it. There were tender little fluting sounds here and there\r\nand everywhere, as if scores of birds were beginning to tune up for a\r\nconcert. Mary put her hand out of the window and held it in the sun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s warm\xe2\x80\x94warm!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will make the green points push up and\r\nup and up, and it will make the bulbs and roots work and struggle with\r\nall their might under the earth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe kneeled down and leaned out of the window as far as she could,\r\nbreathing big breaths and sniffing the air until she laughed because\r\nshe remembered what Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s mother had said about the end of his nose\r\nquivering like a rabbit\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be very early,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe little clouds are all pink and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen the sky look like this. No one is up. I don\xe2\x80\x99t even hear\r\nthe stable boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sudden thought made her scramble to her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t wait! I am going to see the garden!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had learned to dress herself by this time and she put on her\r\nclothes in five minutes. She knew a small side door which she could\r\nunbolt herself and she flew downstairs in her stocking feet and put on\r\nher shoes in the hall. She unchained and unbolted and unlocked and when\r\nthe door was open she sprang across the step with one bound, and there\r\nshe was standing on the grass, which seemed to have turned green, and\r\nwith the sun pouring down on her and warm sweet wafts about her and the\r\nfluting and twittering and singing coming from every bush and tree. She\r\nclasped her hands for pure joy and looked up in the sky and it was so\r\nblue and pink and pearly and white and flooded with springtime light\r\nthat she felt as if she must flute and sing aloud herself and knew that\r\nthrushes and robins and skylarks could not possibly help it. She ran\r\naround the shrubs and paths towards the secret garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all different already,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe grass is greener and\r\nthings are sticking up everywhere and things are uncurling and green\r\nbuds of leaves are showing. This afternoon I am sure Dickon will come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe long warm rain had done strange things to the herbaceous beds which\r\nbordered the walk by the lower wall. There were things sprouting and\r\npushing out from the roots of clumps of plants and there were actually\r\nhere and there glimpses of royal purple and yellow unfurling among the\r\nstems of crocuses. Six months before Mistress Mary would not have seen\r\nhow the world was waking up, but now she missed nothing.\r\n\r\nWhen she had reached the place where the door hid itself under the ivy,\r\nshe was startled by a curious loud sound. It was the caw\xe2\x80\x94caw of a crow\r\nand it came from the top of the wall, and when she looked up, there sat\r\na big glossy-plumaged blue-black bird, looking down at her very wisely\r\nindeed. She had never seen a crow so close before and he made her a\r\nlittle nervous, but the next moment he spread his wings and flapped\r\naway across the garden. She hoped he was not going to stay inside and\r\nshe pushed the door open wondering if he would. When she got fairly\r\ninto the garden she saw that he probably did intend to stay because he\r\nhad alighted on a dwarf apple-tree and under the apple-tree was lying a\r\nlittle reddish animal with a Bushy tail, and both of them were watching\r\nthe stooping body and rust-red head of Dickon, who was kneeling on the\r\ngrass working hard.\r\n\r\nMary flew across the grass to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dickon! Dickon!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out. \xe2\x80\x9cHow could you get here so early!\r\nHow could you! The sun has only just got up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe got up himself, laughing and glowing, and tousled; his eyes like a\r\nbit of the sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was up long before him. How could I have stayed abed!\r\nTh\xe2\x80\x99 world\xe2\x80\x99s all fair begun again this mornin\xe2\x80\x99, it has. An\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99s workin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 hummin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 scratchin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 pipin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 nest-buildin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 breathin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nout scents, till you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to be out on it \xe2\x80\x99stead o\xe2\x80\x99 lyin\xe2\x80\x99 on your\r\nback. When th\xe2\x80\x99 sun did jump up, th\xe2\x80\x99 moor went mad for joy, an\xe2\x80\x99 I was in\r\nthe midst of th\xe2\x80\x99 heather, an\xe2\x80\x99 I run like mad myself, shoutin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsingin\xe2\x80\x99. An\xe2\x80\x99 I come straight here. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have stayed away. Why,\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 garden was lyin\xe2\x80\x99 here waitin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary put her hands on her chest, panting, as if she had been running\r\nherself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dickon! Dickon!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m so happy I can scarcely breathe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeeing him talking to a stranger, the little bushy-tailed animal rose\r\nfrom its place under the tree and came to him, and the rook, cawing\r\nonce, flew down from its branch and settled quietly on his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is th\xe2\x80\x99 little fox cub,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, rubbing the little reddish\r\nanimal\xe2\x80\x99s head. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s named Captain. An\xe2\x80\x99 this here\xe2\x80\x99s Soot. Soot he flew\r\nacross th\xe2\x80\x99 moor with me an\xe2\x80\x99 Captain he run same as if th\xe2\x80\x99 hounds had\r\nbeen after him. They both felt same as I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNeither of the creatures looked as if he were the least afraid of Mary.\r\nWhen Dickon began to walk about, Soot stayed on his shoulder and\r\nCaptain trotted quietly close to his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee here!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cSee how these has pushed up, an\xe2\x80\x99 these an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthese! An\xe2\x80\x99 Eh! Look at these here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe threw himself upon his knees and Mary went down beside him. They had\r\ncome upon a whole clump of crocuses burst into purple and orange and\r\ngold. Mary bent her face down and kissed and kissed them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never kiss a person in that way,\xe2\x80\x9d she said when she lifted her\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cFlowers are so different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked puzzled but smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve kissed mother many a time that way when I come in\r\nfrom th\xe2\x80\x99 moor after a day\xe2\x80\x99s roamin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 she stood there at th\xe2\x80\x99 door in\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 sun, lookin\xe2\x80\x99 so glad an\xe2\x80\x99 comfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey ran from one part of the garden to another and found so many\r\nwonders that they were obliged to remind themselves that they must\r\nwhisper or speak low. He showed her swelling leafbuds on rose branches\r\nwhich had seemed dead. He showed her ten thousand new green points\r\npushing through the mould. They put their eager young noses close to\r\nthe earth and sniffed its warmed springtime breathing; they dug and\r\npulled and laughed low with rapture until Mistress Mary\xe2\x80\x99s hair was as\r\ntumbled as Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s and her cheeks were almost as poppy red as his.\r\n\r\nThere was every joy on earth in the secret garden that morning, and in\r\nthe midst of them came a delight more delightful than all, because it\r\nwas more wonderful. Swiftly something flew across the wall and darted\r\nthrough the trees to a close grown corner, a little flare of\r\nred-breasted bird with something hanging from its beak. Dickon stood\r\nquite still and put his hand on Mary almost as if they had suddenly\r\nfound themselves laughing in a church.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe munnot stir,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered in broad Yorkshire. \xe2\x80\x9cWe munnot scarce\r\nbreathe. I knowed he was mate-huntin\xe2\x80\x99 when I seed him last. It\xe2\x80\x99s Ben\r\nWeatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s robin. He\xe2\x80\x99s buildin\xe2\x80\x99 his nest. He\xe2\x80\x99ll stay here if us\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t flight him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey settled down softly upon the grass and sat there without moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUs mustn\xe2\x80\x99t seem as if us was watchin\xe2\x80\x99 him too close,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d be out with us for good if he got th\xe2\x80\x99 notion us was interferin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nnow. He\xe2\x80\x99ll be a good bit different till all this is over. He\xe2\x80\x99s settin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nup housekeepin\xe2\x80\x99. He\xe2\x80\x99ll be shyer an\xe2\x80\x99 readier to take things ill. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngot no time for visitin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 gossipin\xe2\x80\x99. Us must keep still a bit an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntry to look as if us was grass an\xe2\x80\x99 trees an\xe2\x80\x99 bushes. Then when he\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\nused to seein\xe2\x80\x99 us I\xe2\x80\x99ll chirp a bit an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99ll know us\xe2\x80\x99ll not be in his\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMistress Mary was not at all sure that she knew, as Dickon seemed to,\r\nhow to try to look like grass and trees and bushes. But he had said the\r\nqueer thing as if it were the simplest and most natural thing in the\r\nworld, and she felt it must be quite easy to him, and indeed she\r\nwatched him for a few minutes carefully, wondering if it was possible\r\nfor him to quietly turn green and put out branches and leaves. But he\r\nonly sat wonderfully still, and when he spoke dropped his voice to such\r\na softness that it was curious that she could hear him, but she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s part o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 springtime, this nest-buildin\xe2\x80\x99 is,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwarrant it\xe2\x80\x99s been goin\xe2\x80\x99 on in th\xe2\x80\x99 same way every year since th\xe2\x80\x99 world\r\nwas begun. They\xe2\x80\x99ve got their way o\xe2\x80\x99 thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 and doin\xe2\x80\x99 things an\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\nbody had better not meddle. You can lose a friend in springtime easier\r\nthan any other season if you\xe2\x80\x99re too curious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we talk about him I can\xe2\x80\x99t help looking at him,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said as softly\r\nas possible. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must talk of something else. There is something I want\r\nto tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll like it better if us talks o\xe2\x80\x99 somethin\xe2\x80\x99 else,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it tha\xe2\x80\x99s got to tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94do you know about Colin?\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered.\r\n\r\nHe turned his head to look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does tha\xe2\x80\x99 know about him?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him. I have been to talk to him every day this week. He\r\nwants me to come. He says I\xe2\x80\x99m making him forget about being ill and\r\ndying,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\nDickon looked actually relieved as soon as the surprise died away from\r\nhis round face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad o\xe2\x80\x99 that,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m right down glad. It makes me\r\neasier. I knowed I must say nothin\xe2\x80\x99 about him an\xe2\x80\x99 I don\xe2\x80\x99t like havin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nto hide things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you like hiding the garden?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never tell about it,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I says to mother,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mother,\xe2\x80\x99 I says, \xe2\x80\x98I got a secret to keep. It\xe2\x80\x99s not a bad \xe2\x80\x99un, tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nknows that. It\xe2\x80\x99s no worse than hidin\xe2\x80\x99 where a bird\xe2\x80\x99s nest is. Tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind it, does tha\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary always wanted to hear about mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she say?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, not at all afraid to hear.\r\n\r\nDickon grinned sweet-temperedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was just like her, what she said,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cShe give my head a\r\nbit of a rub an\xe2\x80\x99 laughed an\xe2\x80\x99 she says, \xe2\x80\x98Eh, lad, tha\xe2\x80\x99 can have all th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsecrets tha\xe2\x80\x99 likes. I\xe2\x80\x99ve knowed thee twelve year\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you know about Colin?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody as knowed about Mester Craven knowed there was a little lad\r\nas was like to be a cripple, an\xe2\x80\x99 they knowed Mester Craven didn\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nhim to be talked about. Folks is sorry for Mester Craven because Mrs.\r\nCraven was such a pretty young lady an\xe2\x80\x99 they was so fond of each other.\r\nMrs. Medlock stops in our cottage whenever she goes to Thwaite an\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind talkin\xe2\x80\x99 to mother before us children, because she knows us\r\nhas been brought up to be trusty. How did tha\xe2\x80\x99 find out about him?\r\nMartha was in fine trouble th\xe2\x80\x99 last time she came home. She said tha\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nheard him frettin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 was askin\xe2\x80\x99 questions an\xe2\x80\x99 she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary told him her story about the midnight wuthering of the wind which\r\nhad wakened her and about the faint far-off sounds of the complaining\r\nvoice which had led her down the dark corridors with her candle and had\r\nended with her opening of the door of the dimly lighted room with the\r\ncarven four-posted bed in the corner. When she described the small\r\nivory-white face and the strange black-rimmed eyes Dickon shook his\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThem\xe2\x80\x99s just like his mother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, only hers was always laughin\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nthey say,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey say as Mr. Craven can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to see him when\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s awake an\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99s because his eyes is so like his mother\xe2\x80\x99s an\xe2\x80\x99 yet\r\nlooks so different in his miserable bit of a face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he wants to die?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but he wishes he\xe2\x80\x99d never been born. Mother she says that\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nworst thing on earth for a child. Them as is not wanted scarce ever\r\nthrives. Mester Craven he\xe2\x80\x99d buy anythin\xe2\x80\x99 as money could buy for th\xe2\x80\x99\r\npoor lad but he\xe2\x80\x99d like to forget as he\xe2\x80\x99s on earth. For one thing, he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nafraid he\xe2\x80\x99ll look at him some day and find he\xe2\x80\x99s growed hunchback.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cColin\xe2\x80\x99s so afraid of it himself that he won\xe2\x80\x99t sit up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nsays he\xe2\x80\x99s always thinking that if he should feel a lump coming he\r\nshould go crazy and scream himself to death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! he oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to lie there thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 things like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo lad could get well as thought them sort o\xe2\x80\x99 things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fox was lying on the grass close by him, looking up to ask for a\r\npat now and then, and Dickon bent down and rubbed his neck softly and\r\nthought a few minutes in silence. Presently he lifted his head and\r\nlooked round the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen first we got in here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit seemed like everything was\r\ngray. Look round now and tell me if tha\xe2\x80\x99 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t see a difference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary looked and caught her breath a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthe gray wall is changing. It is as if a green mist\r\nwere creeping over it. It\xe2\x80\x99s almost like a green gauze veil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99ll be greener and greener till th\xe2\x80\x99 gray\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall gone. Can tha\xe2\x80\x99 guess what I was thinkin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it was something nice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe it was\r\nsomething about Colin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 that if he was out here he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be watchin\xe2\x80\x99 for\r\nlumps to grow on his back; he\xe2\x80\x99d be watchin\xe2\x80\x99 for buds to break on th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nrose-bushes, an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99d likely be healthier,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cI was\r\nwonderin\xe2\x80\x99 if us could ever get him in th\xe2\x80\x99 humor to come out here an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlie under th\xe2\x80\x99 trees in his carriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been wondering that myself. I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought of it almost every time\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve talked to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve wondered if he could keep a\r\nsecret and I\xe2\x80\x99ve wondered if we could bring him here without anyone\r\nseeing us. I thought perhaps you could push his carriage. The doctor\r\nsaid he must have fresh air and if he wants us to take him out no one\r\ndare disobey him. He won\xe2\x80\x99t go out for other people and perhaps they\r\nwill be glad if he will go out with us. He could order the gardeners to\r\nkeep away so they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t find out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon was thinking very hard as he scratched Captain\xe2\x80\x99s back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99d be good for him, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cUs\xe2\x80\x99d not be thinkin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d better never been born. Us\xe2\x80\x99d be just two children watchin\xe2\x80\x99 a\r\ngarden grow, an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99d be another. Two lads an\xe2\x80\x99 a little lass just\r\nlookin\xe2\x80\x99 on at th\xe2\x80\x99 springtime. I warrant it\xe2\x80\x99d be better than doctor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstuff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been lying in his room so long and he\xe2\x80\x99s always been so afraid of\r\nhis back that it has made him queer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe knows a good many\r\nthings out of books but he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know anything else. He says he has\r\nbeen too ill to notice things and he hates going out of doors and hates\r\ngardens and gardeners. But he likes to hear about this garden because\r\nit is a secret. I daren\xe2\x80\x99t tell him much but he said he wanted to see\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUs\xe2\x80\x99ll have him out here sometime for sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cI could push\r\nhis carriage well enough. Has tha\xe2\x80\x99 noticed how th\xe2\x80\x99 robin an\xe2\x80\x99 his mate\r\nhas been workin\xe2\x80\x99 while we\xe2\x80\x99ve been sittin\xe2\x80\x99 here? Look at him perched on\r\nthat branch wonderin\xe2\x80\x99 where it\xe2\x80\x99d be best to put that twig he\xe2\x80\x99s got in\r\nhis beak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made one of his low whistling calls and the robin turned his head\r\nand looked at him inquiringly, still holding his twig. Dickon spoke to\r\nhim as Ben Weatherstaff did, but Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s tone was one of friendly\r\nadvice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWheres\xe2\x80\x99ever tha\xe2\x80\x99 puts it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99ll be all right. Tha\xe2\x80\x99 knew how\r\nto build tha\xe2\x80\x99 nest before tha\xe2\x80\x99 came out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 egg. Get on with thee,\r\nlad. Tha\xe2\x80\x99st got no time to lose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I do like to hear you talk to him!\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said, laughing\r\ndelightedly. \xe2\x80\x9cBen Weatherstaff scolds him and makes fun of him, and he\r\nhops about and looks as if he understood every word, and I know he\r\nlikes it. Ben Weatherstaff says he is so conceited he would rather have\r\nstones thrown at him than not be noticed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon laughed too and went on talking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 knows us won\xe2\x80\x99t trouble thee,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the robin. \xe2\x80\x9cUs is near\r\nbein\xe2\x80\x99 wild things ourselves. Us is nest-buildin\xe2\x80\x99 too, bless thee. Look\r\nout tha\xe2\x80\x99 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tell on us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd though the robin did not answer, because his beak was occupied,\r\nMary knew that when he flew away with his twig to his own corner of the\r\ngarden the darkness of his dew-bright eye meant that he would not tell\r\ntheir secret for the world.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI WON\xe2\x80\x99T!\xe2\x80\x9d SAID MARY\r\n\r\n\r\nThey found a great deal to do that morning and Mary was late in\r\nreturning to the house and was also in such a hurry to get back to her\r\nwork that she quite forgot Colin until the last moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell Colin that I can\xe2\x80\x99t come and see him yet,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Martha.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very busy in the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMartha looked rather frightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! Miss Mary,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cit may put him all out of humor when I tell\r\nhim that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Mary was not as afraid of him as other people were and she was not\r\na self-sacrificing person.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stay,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cDickon\xe2\x80\x99s waiting for me;\xe2\x80\x9d and she ran\r\naway.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was even lovelier and busier than the morning had been.\r\nAlready nearly all the weeds were cleared out of the garden and most of\r\nthe roses and trees had been pruned or dug about. Dickon had brought a\r\nspade of his own and he had taught Mary to use all her tools, so that\r\nby this time it was plain that though the lovely wild place was not\r\nlikely to become a \xe2\x80\x9cgardener\xe2\x80\x99s garden\xe2\x80\x9d it would be a wilderness of\r\ngrowing things before the springtime was over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99ll be apple blossoms an\xe2\x80\x99 cherry blossoms overhead,\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon said,\r\nworking away with all his might. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99ll be peach an\xe2\x80\x99 plum trees\r\nin bloom against th\xe2\x80\x99 walls, an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 grass\xe2\x80\x99ll be a carpet o\xe2\x80\x99 flowers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little fox and the rook were as happy and busy as they were, and\r\nthe robin and his mate flew backward and forward like tiny streaks of\r\nlightning. Sometimes the rook flapped his black wings and soared away\r\nover the tree-tops in the park. Each time he came back and perched near\r\nDickon and cawed several times as if he were relating his adventures,\r\nand Dickon talked to him just as he had talked to the robin. Once when\r\nDickon was so busy that he did not answer him at first, Soot flew on to\r\nhis shoulders and gently tweaked his ear with his large beak. When Mary\r\nwanted to rest a little Dickon sat down with her under a tree and once\r\nhe took his pipe out of his pocket and played the soft strange little\r\nnotes and two squirrels appeared on the wall and looked and listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99s a good bit stronger than tha\xe2\x80\x99 was,\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon said, looking at her\r\nas she was digging. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99s beginning to look different, for sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary was glowing with exercise and good spirits.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m getting fatter and fatter every day,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quite exultantly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Medlock will have to get me some bigger dresses. Martha says my\r\nhair is growing thicker. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t so flat and stringy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sun was beginning to set and sending deep gold-colored rays\r\nslanting under the trees when they parted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be fine tomorrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be at work by sunrise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo will I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nShe ran back to the house as quickly as her feet would carry her. She\r\nwanted to tell Colin about Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s fox cub and the rook and about what\r\nthe springtime had been doing. She felt sure he would like to hear. So\r\nit was not very pleasant when she opened the door of her room, to see\r\nMartha standing waiting for her with a doleful face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did Colin say when you told him\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish tha\xe2\x80\x99d gone. He was nigh goin\xe2\x80\x99 into one o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhis tantrums. There\xe2\x80\x99s been a nice to do all afternoon to keep him\r\nquiet. He would watch the clock all th\xe2\x80\x99 time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s lips pinched themselves together. She was no more used to\r\nconsidering other people than Colin was and she saw no reason why an\r\nill-tempered boy should interfere with the thing she liked best. She\r\nknew nothing about the pitifulness of people who had been ill and\r\nnervous and who did not know that they could control their tempers and\r\nneed not make other people ill and nervous, too. When she had had a\r\nheadache in India she had done her best to see that everybody else also\r\nhad a headache or something quite as bad. And she felt she was quite\r\nright; but of course now she felt that Colin was quite wrong.\r\n\r\nHe was not on his sofa when she went into his room. He was lying flat\r\non his back in bed and he did not turn his head toward her as she came\r\nin. This was a bad beginning and Mary marched up to him with her stiff\r\nmanner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you get up?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did get up this morning when I thought you were coming,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nanswered, without looking at her. \xe2\x80\x9cI made them put me back in bed this\r\nafternoon. My back ached and my head ached and I was tired. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was working in the garden with Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nColin frowned and condescended to look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t let that boy come here if you go and stay with him instead of\r\ncoming to talk to me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMary flew into a fine passion. She could fly into a passion without\r\nmaking a noise. She just grew sour and obstinate and did not care what\r\nhappened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you send Dickon away, I\xe2\x80\x99ll never come into this room again!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nretorted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to if I want you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll make you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cThey shall drag you in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall they, Mr. Rajah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cThey may drag me in but\r\nthey can\xe2\x80\x99t make me talk when they get me here. I\xe2\x80\x99ll sit and clench my\r\nteeth and never tell you one thing. I won\xe2\x80\x99t even look at you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nstare at the floor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were a nice agreeable pair as they glared at each other. If they\r\nhad been two little street boys they would have sprung at each other\r\nand had a rough-and-tumble fight. As it was, they did the next thing to\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a selfish thing!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cSelfish people always say that. Anyone is\r\nselfish who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do what they want. You\xe2\x80\x99re more selfish than I am.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re the most selfish boy I ever saw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not!\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not as selfish as your fine Dickon is!\r\nHe keeps you playing in the dirt when he knows I am all by myself. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nselfish, if you like!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s eyes flashed fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s nicer than any other boy that ever lived!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlike an angel!\xe2\x80\x9d It might sound rather silly to say that but she did not\r\ncare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice angel!\xe2\x80\x9d Colin sneered ferociously. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a common cottage boy\r\noff the moor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s better than a common Rajah!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a thousand\r\ntimes better!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBecause she was the stronger of the two she was beginning to get the\r\nbetter of him. The truth was that he had never had a fight with anyone\r\nlike himself in his life and, upon the whole, it was rather good for\r\nhim, though neither he nor Mary knew anything about that. He turned his\r\nhead on his pillow and shut his eyes and a big tear was squeezed out\r\nand ran down his cheek. He was beginning to feel pathetic and sorry for\r\nhimself\xe2\x80\x94not for anyone else.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not as selfish as you, because I\xe2\x80\x99m always ill, and I\xe2\x80\x99m sure there\r\nis a lump coming on my back,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am going to die besides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not!\xe2\x80\x9d contradicted Mary unsympathetically.\r\n\r\nHe opened his eyes quite wide with indignation. He had never heard such\r\na thing said before. He was at once furious and slightly pleased, if a\r\nperson could be both at one time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI am! You know I am! Everybody says so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary sourly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou just say that to make\r\npeople sorry. I believe you\xe2\x80\x99re proud of it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it! If you\r\nwere a nice boy it might be true\xe2\x80\x94but you\xe2\x80\x99re too nasty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of his invalid back Colin sat up in bed in quite a healthy\r\nrage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet out of the room!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted and he caught hold of his pillow and\r\nthrew it at her. He was not strong enough to throw it far and it only\r\nfell at her feet, but Mary\xe2\x80\x99s face looked as pinched as a nutcracker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I won\xe2\x80\x99t come back!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe walked to the door and when she reached it she turned round and\r\nspoke again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was going to tell you all sorts of nice things,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cDickon\r\nbrought his fox and his rook and I was going to tell you all about\r\nthem. Now I won\xe2\x80\x99t tell you a single thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe marched out of the door and closed it behind her, and there to her\r\ngreat astonishment she found the trained nurse standing as if she had\r\nbeen listening and, more amazing still\xe2\x80\x94she was laughing. She was a big\r\nhandsome young woman who ought not to have been a trained nurse at all,\r\nas she could not bear invalids and she was always making excuses to\r\nleave Colin to Martha or anyone else who would take her place. Mary had\r\nnever liked her, and she simply stood and gazed up at her as she stood\r\ngiggling into her handkerchief..\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you laughing at?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt you two young ones,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the best thing that\r\ncould happen to the sickly pampered thing to have someone to stand up\r\nto him that\xe2\x80\x99s as spoiled as himself;\xe2\x80\x9d and she laughed into her\r\nhandkerchief again. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he\xe2\x80\x99d had a young vixen of a sister to fight\r\nwith it would have been the saving of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he going to die?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know and I don\xe2\x80\x99t care,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse. \xe2\x80\x9cHysterics and temper\r\nare half what ails him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are hysterics?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find out if you work him into a tantrum after this\xe2\x80\x94but at any\r\nrate you\xe2\x80\x99ve given him something to have hysterics about, and I\xe2\x80\x99m glad\r\nof it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary went back to her room not feeling at all as she had felt when she\r\nhad come in from the garden. She was cross and disappointed but not at\r\nall sorry for Colin. She had looked forward to telling him a great many\r\nthings and she had meant to try to make up her mind whether it would be\r\nsafe to trust him with the great secret. She had been beginning to\r\nthink it would be, but now she had changed her mind entirely. She would\r\nnever tell him and he could stay in his room and never get any fresh\r\nair and die if he liked! It would serve him right! She felt so sour and\r\nunrelenting that for a few minutes she almost forgot about Dickon and\r\nthe green veil creeping over the world and the soft wind blowing down\r\nfrom the moor.\r\n\r\nMartha was waiting for her and the trouble in her face had been\r\ntemporarily replaced by interest and curiosity. There was a wooden box\r\non the table and its cover had been removed and revealed that it was\r\nfull of neat packages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Craven sent it to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Martha. \xe2\x80\x9cIt looks as if it had\r\npicture-books in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary remembered what he had asked her the day she had gone to his room.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want anything\xe2\x80\x94dolls\xe2\x80\x94toys\xe2\x80\x94books?\xe2\x80\x9d She opened the package\r\nwondering if he had sent a doll, and also wondering what she should do\r\nwith it if he had. But he had not sent one. There were several\r\nbeautiful books such as Colin had, and two of them were about gardens\r\nand were full of pictures. There were two or three games and there was\r\na beautiful little writing-case with a gold monogram on it and a gold\r\npen and inkstand.\r\n\r\nEverything was so nice that her pleasure began to crowd her anger out\r\nof her mind. She had not expected him to remember her at all and her\r\nhard little heart grew quite warm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can write better than I can print,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand the first thing I\r\nshall write with that pen will be a letter to tell him I am much\r\nobliged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf she had been friends with Colin she would have run to show him her\r\npresents at once, and they would have looked at the pictures and read\r\nsome of the gardening books and perhaps tried playing the games, and he\r\nwould have enjoyed himself so much he would never once have thought he\r\nwas going to die or have put his hand on his spine to see if there was\r\na lump coming. He had a way of doing that which she could not bear. It\r\ngave her an uncomfortable frightened feeling because he always looked\r\nso frightened himself. He said that if he felt even quite a little lump\r\nsome day he should know his hunch had begun to grow. Something he had\r\nheard Mrs. Medlock whispering to the nurse had given him the idea and\r\nhe had thought over it in secret until it was quite firmly fixed in his\r\nmind. Mrs. Medlock had said his father\xe2\x80\x99s back had begun to show its\r\ncrookedness in that way when he was a child. He had never told anyone\r\nbut Mary that most of his \xe2\x80\x9ctantrums\xe2\x80\x9d as they called them grew out of\r\nhis hysterical hidden fear. Mary had been sorry for him when he had\r\ntold her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe always began to think about it when he was cross or tired,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid to herself. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he has been cross today. Perhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps he has\r\nbeen thinking about it all afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stood still, looking down at the carpet and thinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said I would never go back again\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she hesitated, knitting her\r\nbrows\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbut perhaps, just perhaps, I will go and see\xe2\x80\x94if he wants me\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nthe morning. Perhaps he\xe2\x80\x99ll try to throw his pillow at me again, but\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nthink\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\nA TANTRUM\r\n\r\n\r\nShe had got up very early in the morning and had worked hard in the\r\ngarden and she was tired and sleepy, so as soon as Martha had brought\r\nher supper and she had eaten it, she was glad to go to bed. As she laid\r\nher head on the pillow she murmured to herself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go out before breakfast and work with Dickon and then afterward\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nbelieve\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe thought it was the middle of the night when she was awakened by\r\nsuch dreadful sounds that she jumped out of bed in an instant. What was\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94what was it? The next minute she felt quite sure she knew. Doors\r\nwere opened and shut and there were hurrying feet in the corridors and\r\nsomeone was crying and screaming at the same time, screaming and crying\r\nin a horrible way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s having one of those tantrums the nurse\r\ncalled hysterics. How awful it sounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs she listened to the sobbing screams she did not wonder that people\r\nwere so frightened that they gave him his own way in everything rather\r\nthan hear them. She put her hands over her ears and felt sick and\r\nshivering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do,\xe2\x80\x9d she kept saying. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t bear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce she wondered if he would stop if she dared go to him and then she\r\nremembered how he had driven her out of the room and thought that\r\nperhaps the sight of her might make him worse. Even when she pressed\r\nher hands more tightly over her ears she could not keep the awful\r\nsounds out. She hated them so and was so terrified by them that\r\nsuddenly they began to make her angry and she felt as if she should\r\nlike to fly into a tantrum herself and frighten him as he was\r\nfrightening her. She was not used to anyone\xe2\x80\x99s tempers but her own. She\r\ntook her hands from her ears and sprang up and stamped her foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ought to be stopped! Somebody ought to make him stop! Somebody\r\nought to beat him!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out.\r\n\r\nJust then she heard feet almost running down the corridor and her door\r\nopened and the nurse came in. She was not laughing now by any means.\r\nShe even looked rather pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s worked himself into hysterics,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a great hurry. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndo himself harm. No one can do anything with him. You come and try,\r\nlike a good child. He likes you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe turned me out of the room this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, stamping her\r\nfoot with excitement.\r\n\r\nThe stamp rather pleased the nurse. The truth was that she had been\r\nafraid she might find Mary crying and hiding her head under the\r\nbed-clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s right,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re in the right humor. You go and scold\r\nhim. Give him something new to think of. Do go, child, as quick as ever\r\nyou can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not until afterward that Mary realized that the thing had been\r\nfunny as well as dreadful\xe2\x80\x94that it was funny that all the grown-up\r\npeople were so frightened that they came to a little girl just because\r\nthey guessed she was almost as bad as Colin himself.\r\n\r\nShe flew along the corridor and the nearer she got to the screams the\r\nhigher her temper mounted. She felt quite wicked by the time she\r\nreached the door. She slapped it open with her hand and ran across the\r\nroom to the four-posted bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou stop!\xe2\x80\x9d she almost shouted. \xe2\x80\x9cYou stop! I hate you! Everybody hates\r\nyou! I wish everybody would run out of the house and let you scream\r\nyourself to death! You _will_ scream yourself to death in a minute, and\r\nI wish you would!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA nice sympathetic child could neither have thought nor said such\r\nthings, but it just happened that the shock of hearing them was the\r\nbest possible thing for this hysterical boy whom no one had ever dared\r\nto restrain or contradict.\r\n\r\nHe had been lying on his face beating his pillow with his hands and he\r\nactually almost jumped around, he turned so quickly at the sound of the\r\nfurious little voice. His face looked dreadful, white and red and\r\nswollen, and he was gasping and choking; but savage little Mary did not\r\ncare an atom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you scream another scream,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll scream too\xe2\x80\x94and I can\r\nscream louder than you can and I\xe2\x80\x99ll frighten you, I\xe2\x80\x99ll frighten you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe actually had stopped screaming because she had startled him so. The\r\nscream which had been coming almost choked him. The tears were\r\nstreaming down his face and he shook all over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stop!\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped and sobbed. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHalf that ails you is hysterics and\r\ntemper\xe2\x80\x94just hysterics\xe2\x80\x94hysterics\xe2\x80\x94hysterics!\xe2\x80\x9d and she stamped each time\r\nshe said it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI felt the lump\xe2\x80\x94I felt it,\xe2\x80\x9d choked out Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew I should. I\r\nshall have a hunch on my back and then I shall die,\xe2\x80\x9d and he began to\r\nwrithe again and turned on his face and sobbed and wailed but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nscream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel a lump!\xe2\x80\x9d contradicted Mary fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you did it\r\nwas only a hysterical lump. Hysterics makes lumps. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing the\r\nmatter with your horrid back\xe2\x80\x94nothing but hysterics! Turn over and let\r\nme look at it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe liked the word \xe2\x80\x9chysterics\xe2\x80\x9d and felt somehow as if it had an effect\r\non him. He was probably like herself and had never heard it before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNurse,\xe2\x80\x9d she commanded, \xe2\x80\x9ccome here and show me his back this minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nurse, Mrs. Medlock and Martha had been standing huddled together\r\nnear the door staring at her, their mouths half open. All three had\r\ngasped with fright more than once. The nurse came forward as if she\r\nwere half afraid. Colin was heaving with great breathless sobs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he\xe2\x80\x94he won\xe2\x80\x99t let me,\xe2\x80\x9d she hesitated in a low voice.\r\n\r\nColin heard her, however, and he gasped out between two sobs:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh-show her! She-she\xe2\x80\x99ll see then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a poor thin back to look at when it was bared. Every rib could\r\nbe counted and every joint of the spine, though Mistress Mary did not\r\ncount them as she bent over and examined them with a solemn savage\r\nlittle face. She looked so sour and old-fashioned that the nurse turned\r\nher head aside to hide the twitching of her mouth. There was just a\r\nminute\xe2\x80\x99s silence, for even Colin tried to hold his breath while Mary\r\nlooked up and down his spine, and down and up, as intently as if she\r\nhad been the great doctor from London.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s not a single lump there!\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s not a\r\nlump as big as a pin\xe2\x80\x94except backbone lumps, and you can only feel them\r\nbecause you\xe2\x80\x99re thin. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got backbone lumps myself, and they used to\r\nstick out as much as yours do, until I began to get fatter, and I am\r\nnot fat enough yet to hide them. There\xe2\x80\x99s not a lump as big as a pin! If\r\nyou ever say there is again, I shall laugh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one but Colin himself knew what effect those crossly spoken childish\r\nwords had on him. If he had ever had anyone to talk to about his secret\r\nterrors\xe2\x80\x94if he had ever dared to let himself ask questions\xe2\x80\x94if he had had\r\nchildish companions and had not lain on his back in the huge closed\r\nhouse, breathing an atmosphere heavy with the fears of people who were\r\nmost of them ignorant and tired of him, he would have found out that\r\nmost of his fright and illness was created by himself. But he had lain\r\nand thought of himself and his aches and weariness for hours and days\r\nand months and years. And now that an angry unsympathetic little girl\r\ninsisted obstinately that he was not as ill as he thought he was he\r\nactually felt as if she might be speaking the truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d ventured the nurse, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he thought he had a lump on\r\nhis spine. His back is weak because he won\xe2\x80\x99t try to sit up. I could\r\nhave told him there was no lump there.\xe2\x80\x9d Colin gulped and turned his\r\nface a little to look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cC-could you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said pathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, and she gulped too.\r\n\r\nColin turned on his face again and but for his long-drawn broken\r\nbreaths, which were the dying down of his storm of sobbing, he lay\r\nstill for a minute, though great tears streamed down his face and wet\r\nthe pillow. Actually the tears meant that a curious great relief had\r\ncome to him. Presently he turned and looked at the nurse again and\r\nstrangely enough he was not like a Rajah at all as he spoke to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think\xe2\x80\x94I could\xe2\x80\x94live to grow up?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThe nurse was neither clever nor soft-hearted but she could repeat some\r\nof the London doctor\xe2\x80\x99s words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou probably will if you will do what you are told to do and not give\r\nway to your temper, and stay out a great deal in the fresh air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin\xe2\x80\x99s tantrum had passed and he was weak and worn out with crying and\r\nthis perhaps made him feel gentle. He put out his hand a little toward\r\nMary, and I am glad to say that, her own tantum having passed, she was\r\nsoftened too and met him half-way with her hand, so that it was a sort\r\nof making up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll go out with you, Mary,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI shan\xe2\x80\x99t hate fresh air if\r\nwe can find\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He remembered just in time to stop himself from saying\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cif we can find the secret garden\xe2\x80\x9d and he ended, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall like to go\r\nout with you if Dickon will come and push my chair. I do so want to see\r\nDickon and the fox and the crow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nurse remade the tumbled bed and shook and straightened the\r\npillows. Then she made Colin a cup of beef tea and gave a cup to Mary,\r\nwho really was very glad to get it after her excitement. Mrs. Medlock\r\nand Martha gladly slipped away, and after everything was neat and calm\r\nand in order the nurse looked as if she would very gladly slip away\r\nalso. She was a healthy young woman who resented being robbed of her\r\nsleep and she yawned quite openly as she looked at Mary, who had pushed\r\nher big footstool close to the four-posted bed and was holding Colin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must go back and get your sleep out,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll drop off\r\nafter a while\xe2\x80\x94if he\xe2\x80\x99s not too upset. Then I\xe2\x80\x99ll lie down myself in the\r\nnext room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you like me to sing you that song I learned from my Ayah?\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\r\nwhispered to Colin.\r\n\r\nHis hand pulled hers gently and he turned his tired eyes on her\r\nappealingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s such a soft song. I shall go to sleep in\r\na minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will put him to sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary said to the yawning nurse. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can go\r\nif you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse, with an attempt at reluctance. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo to sleep in half an hour you must call me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\nThe nurse was out of the room in a minute and as soon as she was gone\r\nColin pulled Mary\xe2\x80\x99s hand again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI almost told,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I stopped myself in time. I won\xe2\x80\x99t talk\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to sleep, but you said you had a whole lot of nice things\r\nto tell me. Have you\xe2\x80\x94do you think you have found out anything at all\r\nabout the way into the secret garden?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary looked at his poor little tired face and swollen eyes and her\r\nheart relented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe-es,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI think I have. And if you will go to sleep I\r\nwill tell you tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d His hand quite trembled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mary!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mary! If I could get into it I think I should\r\nlive to grow up! Do you suppose that instead of singing the Ayah\r\nsong\xe2\x80\x94you could just tell me softly as you did that first day what you\r\nimagine it looks like inside? I am sure it will make me go to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cShut your eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed his eyes and lay quite still and she held his hand and began\r\nto speak very slowly and in a very low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it has been left alone so long\xe2\x80\x94that it has grown all into a\r\nlovely tangle. I think the roses have climbed and climbed and climbed\r\nuntil they hang from the branches and walls and creep over the\r\nground\xe2\x80\x94almost like a strange gray mist. Some of them have died but\r\nmany\xe2\x80\x94are alive and when the summer comes there will be curtains and\r\nfountains of roses. I think the ground is full of daffodils and\r\nsnowdrops and lilies and iris working their way out of the dark. Now\r\nthe spring has begun\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soft drone of her voice was making him stiller and stiller and she\r\nsaw it and went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps they are coming up through the grass\xe2\x80\x94perhaps there are\r\nclusters of purple crocuses and gold ones\xe2\x80\x94even now. Perhaps the leaves\r\nare beginning to break out and uncurl\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps\xe2\x80\x94the gray is changing\r\nand a green gauze veil is creeping\xe2\x80\x94and creeping over\xe2\x80\x94everything. And\r\nthe birds are coming to look at it\xe2\x80\x94because it is\xe2\x80\x94so safe and still. And\r\nperhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d very softly and slowly indeed, \xe2\x80\x9cthe robin has\r\nfound a mate\xe2\x80\x94and is building a nest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Colin was asleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHA\xe2\x80\x99 MUNNOT WASTE NO TIME\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nOf course Mary did not waken early the next morning. She slept late\r\nbecause she was tired, and when Martha brought her breakfast she told\r\nher that though Colin was quite quiet he was ill and feverish as he\r\nalways was after he had worn himself out with a fit of crying. Mary ate\r\nher breakfast slowly as she listened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says he wishes tha\xe2\x80\x99 would please go and see him as soon as tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncan,\xe2\x80\x9d Martha said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s queer what a fancy he\xe2\x80\x99s took to thee. Tha\xe2\x80\x99 did\r\ngive it him last night for sure\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t tha? Nobody else would have\r\ndared to do it. Eh! poor lad! He\xe2\x80\x99s been spoiled till salt won\xe2\x80\x99t save\r\nhim. Mother says as th\xe2\x80\x99 two worst things as can happen to a child is\r\nnever to have his own way\xe2\x80\x94or always to have it. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know which\r\nis th\xe2\x80\x99 worst. Tha\xe2\x80\x99 was in a fine temper tha\xe2\x80\x99self, too. But he says to\r\nme when I went into his room, \xe2\x80\x98Please ask Miss Mary if she\xe2\x80\x99ll please\r\ncome an\xe2\x80\x99 talk to me?\xe2\x80\x99 Think o\xe2\x80\x99 him saying please! Will you go, Miss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll run and see Dickon first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see Colin\r\nfirst and tell him\xe2\x80\x94I know what I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell him,\xe2\x80\x9d with a sudden\r\ninspiration.\r\n\r\nShe had her hat on when she appeared in Colin\xe2\x80\x99s room and for a second\r\nhe looked disappointed. He was in bed. His face was pitifully white and\r\nthere were dark circles round his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you came,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy head aches and I ache all over\r\nbecause I\xe2\x80\x99m so tired. Are you going somewhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary went and leaned against his bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t be long,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to Dickon, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll come back.\r\nColin, it\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s something about the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis whole face brightened and a little color came into it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out. \xe2\x80\x9cI dreamed about it all night. I heard you\r\nsay something about gray changing into green, and I dreamed I was\r\nstanding in a place all filled with trembling little green leaves\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthere were birds on nests everywhere and they looked so soft and still.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll lie and think about it until you come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn five minutes Mary was with Dickon in their garden. The fox and the\r\ncrow were with him again and this time he had brought two tame\r\nsquirrels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came over on the pony this mornin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! he is a good\r\nlittle chap\xe2\x80\x94Jump is! I brought these two in my pockets. This here one\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s called Nut an\xe2\x80\x99 this here other one\xe2\x80\x99s called Shell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he said \xe2\x80\x9cNut\xe2\x80\x9d one squirrel leaped on to his right shoulder and\r\nwhen he said \xe2\x80\x9cShell\xe2\x80\x9d the other one leaped on to his left shoulder.\r\n\r\nWhen they sat down on the grass with Captain curled at their feet, Soot\r\nsolemnly listening on a tree and Nut and Shell nosing about close to\r\nthem, it seemed to Mary that it would be scarcely bearable to leave\r\nsuch delightfulness, but when she began to tell her story somehow the\r\nlook in Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s funny face gradually changed her mind. She could see\r\nhe felt sorrier for Colin than she did. He looked up at the sky and all\r\nabout him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust listen to them birds\xe2\x80\x94th\xe2\x80\x99 world seems full of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94all whistlin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 pipin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at \xe2\x80\x99em dartin\xe2\x80\x99 about, an\xe2\x80\x99 hearken at \xe2\x80\x99em\r\ncallin\xe2\x80\x99 to each other. Come springtime seems like as if all th\xe2\x80\x99 world\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncallin\xe2\x80\x99. The leaves is uncurlin\xe2\x80\x99 so you can see \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99, my word, th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nnice smells there is about!\xe2\x80\x9d sniffing with his happy turned-up nose.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 that poor lad lyin\xe2\x80\x99 shut up an\xe2\x80\x99 seein\xe2\x80\x99 so little that he gets to\r\nthinkin\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 things as sets him screamin\xe2\x80\x99. Eh! my! we mun get him out\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94we mun get him watchin\xe2\x80\x99 an listenin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 sniffin\xe2\x80\x99 up th\xe2\x80\x99 air an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nget him just soaked through wi\xe2\x80\x99 sunshine. An\xe2\x80\x99 we munnot lose no time\r\nabout it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he was very much interested he often spoke quite broad Yorkshire\r\nthough at other times he tried to modify his dialect so that Mary could\r\nbetter understand. But she loved his broad Yorkshire and had in fact\r\nbeen trying to learn to speak it herself. So she spoke a little now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that we mun,\xe2\x80\x9d she said (which meant \xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed, we must\xe2\x80\x9d).\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell thee what us\xe2\x80\x99ll do first,\xe2\x80\x9d she proceeded, and Dickon\r\ngrinned, because when the little wench tried to twist her tongue into\r\nspeaking Yorkshire it amused him very much. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s took a graidely fancy\r\nto thee. He wants to see thee and he wants to see Soot an\xe2\x80\x99 Captain.\r\nWhen I go back to the house to talk to him I\xe2\x80\x99ll ax him if tha\xe2\x80\x99 canna\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncome an\xe2\x80\x99 see him tomorrow mornin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 bring tha\xe2\x80\x99 creatures wi\xe2\x80\x99 thee\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94in a bit, when there\xe2\x80\x99s more leaves out, an\xe2\x80\x99 happen a bud or two,\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll get him to come out an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 shall push him in his chair an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll bring him here an\xe2\x80\x99 show him everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she stopped she was quite proud of herself. She had never made a\r\nlong speech in Yorkshire before and she had remembered very well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 mun talk a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 Yorkshire like that to Mester Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon\r\nchuckled. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99ll make him laugh an\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99s nowt as good for ill folk\r\nas laughin\xe2\x80\x99 is. Mother says she believes as half a hour\xe2\x80\x99s good laugh\r\nevery mornin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99ud cure a chap as was makin\xe2\x80\x99 ready for typhus fever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to talk Yorkshire to him this very day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary,\r\nchuckling herself.\r\n\r\nThe garden had reached the time when every day and every night it\r\nseemed as if Magicians were passing through it drawing loveliness out\r\nof the earth and the boughs with wands. It was hard to go away and\r\nleave it all, particularly as Nut had actually crept on to her dress\r\nand Shell had scrambled down the trunk of the apple-tree they sat under\r\nand stayed there looking at her with inquiring eyes. But she went back\r\nto the house and when she sat down close to Colin\xe2\x80\x99s bed he began to\r\nsniff as Dickon did though not in such an experienced way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou smell like flowers and\xe2\x80\x94and fresh things,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out quite\r\njoyously. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you smell of? It\xe2\x80\x99s cool and warm and sweet all at\r\nthe same time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 wind from th\xe2\x80\x99 moor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cIt comes o\xe2\x80\x99 sittin\xe2\x80\x99 on th\xe2\x80\x99\r\ngrass under a tree wi\xe2\x80\x99 Dickon an\xe2\x80\x99 wi\xe2\x80\x99 Captain an\xe2\x80\x99 Soot an\xe2\x80\x99 Nut an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nShell. It\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 springtime an\xe2\x80\x99 out o\xe2\x80\x99 doors an\xe2\x80\x99 sunshine as smells so\r\ngraidely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe said it as broadly as she could, and you do not know how broadly\r\nYorkshire sounds until you have heard someone speak it. Colin began to\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI never heard you talk like that\r\nbefore. How funny it sounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m givin\xe2\x80\x99 thee a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 Yorkshire,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncanna\xe2\x80\x99 talk as graidely as Dickon an\xe2\x80\x99 Martha can but tha\xe2\x80\x99 sees I can\r\nshape a bit. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t tha\xe2\x80\x99 understand a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 Yorkshire when tha\xe2\x80\x99 hears\r\nit? An\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 a Yorkshire lad thysel\xe2\x80\x99 bred an\xe2\x80\x99 born! Eh! I wonder tha\xe2\x80\x99rt\r\nnot ashamed o\xe2\x80\x99 thy face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then she began to laugh too and they both laughed until they could\r\nnot stop themselves and they laughed until the room echoed and Mrs.\r\nMedlock opening the door to come in drew back into the corridor and\r\nstood listening amazed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, upon my word!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, speaking rather broad Yorkshire herself\r\nbecause there was no one to hear her and she was so astonished.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhoever heard th\xe2\x80\x99 like! Whoever on earth would ha\xe2\x80\x99 thought it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was so much to talk about. It seemed as if Colin could never hear\r\nenough of Dickon and Captain and Soot and Nut and Shell and the pony\r\nwhose name was Jump. Mary had run round into the wood with Dickon to\r\nsee Jump. He was a tiny little shaggy moor pony with thick locks\r\nhanging over his eyes and with a pretty face and a nuzzling velvet\r\nnose. He was rather thin with living on moor grass but he was as tough\r\nand wiry as if the muscle in his little legs had been made of steel\r\nsprings. He had lifted his head and whinnied softly the moment he saw\r\nDickon and he had trotted up to him and put his head across his\r\nshoulder and then Dickon had talked into his ear and Jump had talked\r\nback in odd little whinnies and puffs and snorts. Dickon had made him\r\ngive Mary his small front hoof and kiss her on her cheek with his\r\nvelvet muzzle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he really understand everything Dickon says?\xe2\x80\x9d Colin asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems as if he does,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cDickon says anything will\r\nunderstand if you\xe2\x80\x99re friends with it for sure, but you have to be\r\nfriends for sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin lay quiet a little while and his strange gray eyes seemed to be\r\nstaring at the wall, but Mary saw he was thinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I was friends with things,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m not. I\r\nnever had anything to be friends with, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you bear me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I can,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s funny but I even like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBen Weatherstaff said I was like him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe said he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nwarrant we\xe2\x80\x99d both got the same nasty tempers. I think you are like him\r\ntoo. We are all three alike\xe2\x80\x94you and I and Ben Weatherstaff. He said we\r\nwere neither of us much to look at and we were as sour as we looked.\r\nBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel as sour as I used to before I knew the robin and\r\nDickon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you feel as if you hated people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary without any affectation. \xe2\x80\x9cI should have detested\r\nyou if I had seen you before I saw the robin and Dickon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin put out his thin hand and touched her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t said what I did about sending Dickon\r\naway. I hated you when you said he was like an angel and I laughed at\r\nyou but\xe2\x80\x94but perhaps he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it was rather funny to say it,\xe2\x80\x9d she admitted frankly, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\r\nhis nose does turn up and he has a big mouth and his clothes have\r\npatches all over them and he talks broad Yorkshire, but\xe2\x80\x94but if an angel\r\ndid come to Yorkshire and live on the moor\xe2\x80\x94if there was a Yorkshire\r\nangel\xe2\x80\x94I believe he\xe2\x80\x99d understand the green things and know how to make\r\nthem grow and he would know how to talk to the wild creatures as Dickon\r\ndoes and they\xe2\x80\x99d know he was friends for sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind Dickon looking at me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin; \xe2\x80\x9cI want to see\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you said that,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause\xe2\x80\x94because\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite suddenly it came into her mind that this was the minute to tell\r\nhim. Colin knew something new was coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause what?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried eagerly.\r\n\r\nMary was so anxious that she got up from her stool and came to him and\r\ncaught hold of both his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan I trust you? I trusted Dickon because birds trusted him. Can I\r\ntrust you\xe2\x80\x94for sure\xe2\x80\x94_for sure?_\xe2\x80\x9d she implored.\r\n\r\nHer face was so solemn that he almost whispered his answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Dickon will come to see you tomorrow morning, and he\xe2\x80\x99ll bring\r\nhis creatures with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!
