There is a door into the garden. I found it. It is\r\nunder the ivy on the wall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf he had been a strong healthy boy Colin would probably have shouted\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHooray! Hooray! Hooray!\xe2\x80\x9d but he was weak and rather hysterical; his\r\neyes grew bigger and bigger and he gasped for breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Mary!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out with a half sob. \xe2\x80\x9cShall I see it? Shall I get\r\ninto it? Shall I _live_ to get into it?\xe2\x80\x9d and he clutched her hands and\r\ndragged her toward him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you\xe2\x80\x99ll see it!\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Mary indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlive to get into it! Don\xe2\x80\x99t be silly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd she was so un-hysterical and natural and childish that she brought\r\nhim to his senses and he began to laugh at himself and a few minutes\r\nafterward she was sitting on her stool again telling him not what she\r\nimagined the secret garden to be like but what it really was, and\r\nColin\xe2\x80\x99s aches and tiredness were forgotten and he was listening\r\nenraptured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is just what you thought it would be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds\r\njust as if you had really seen it. You know I said that when you told\r\nme first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary hesitated about two minutes and then boldly spoke the truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had seen it\xe2\x80\x94and I had been in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI found the key and got\r\nin weeks ago. But I daren\xe2\x80\x99t tell you\xe2\x80\x94I daren\xe2\x80\x99t because I was so afraid\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t trust you\xe2\x80\x94_for sure!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIT HAS COME!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nOf course Dr. Craven had been sent for the morning after Colin had had\r\nhis tantrum. He was always sent for at once when such a thing occurred\r\nand he always found, when he arrived, a white shaken boy lying on his\r\nbed, sulky and still so hysterical that he was ready to break into\r\nfresh sobbing at the least word. In fact, Dr. Craven dreaded and\r\ndetested the difficulties of these visits. On this occasion he was away\r\nfrom Misselthwaite Manor until afternoon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is he?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked Mrs. Medlock rather irritably when he arrived.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will break a blood-vessel in one of those fits some day. The boy is\r\nhalf insane with hysteria and self-indulgence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mrs. Medlock, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll scarcely believe your eyes\r\nwhen you see him. That plain sour-faced child that\xe2\x80\x99s almost as bad as\r\nhimself has just bewitched him. How she\xe2\x80\x99s done it there\xe2\x80\x99s no telling.\r\nThe Lord knows she\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to look at and you scarcely ever hear her\r\nspeak, but she did what none of us dare do. She just flew at him like a\r\nlittle cat last night, and stamped her feet and ordered him to stop\r\nscreaming, and somehow she startled him so that he actually did stop,\r\nand this afternoon\xe2\x80\x94well just come up and see, sir. It\xe2\x80\x99s past\r\ncrediting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe scene which Dr. Craven beheld when he entered his patient\xe2\x80\x99s room\r\nwas indeed rather astonishing to him. As Mrs. Medlock opened the door\r\nhe heard laughing and chattering. Colin was on his sofa in his\r\ndressing-gown and he was sitting up quite straight looking at a picture\r\nin one of the garden books and talking to the plain child who at that\r\nmoment could scarcely be called plain at all because her face was so\r\nglowing with enjoyment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose long spires of blue ones\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a lot of those,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin was\r\nannouncing. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re called Del-phin-iums.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDickon says they\xe2\x80\x99re larkspurs made big and grand,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mistress\r\nMary. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are clumps there already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they saw Dr. Craven and stopped. Mary became quite still and Colin\r\nlooked fretful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear you were ill last night, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Dr. Craven said a\r\ntrifle nervously. He was rather a nervous man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m better now\xe2\x80\x94much better,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin answered, rather like a Rajah. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngoing out in my chair in a day or two if it is fine. I want some fresh\r\nair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven sat down by him and felt his pulse and looked at him\r\ncuriously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be a very fine day,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand you must be very careful\r\nnot to tire yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFresh air won\xe2\x80\x99t tire me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young Rajah.\r\n\r\nAs there had been occasions when this same young gentleman had shrieked\r\naloud with rage and had insisted that fresh air would give him cold and\r\nkill him, it is not to be wondered at that his doctor felt somewhat\r\nstartled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you did not like fresh air,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t when I am by myself,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Rajah; \xe2\x80\x9cbut my cousin is\r\ngoing out with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the nurse, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Dr. Craven.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I will not have the nurse,\xe2\x80\x9d so magnificently that Mary could not\r\nhelp remembering how the young native Prince had looked with his\r\ndiamonds and emeralds and pearls stuck all over him and the great\r\nrubies on the small dark hand he had waved to command his servants to\r\napproach with salaams and receive his orders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy cousin knows how to take care of me. I am always better when she is\r\nwith me. She made me better last night. A very strong boy I know will\r\npush my carriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven felt rather alarmed. If this tiresome hysterical boy should\r\nchance to get well he himself would lose all chance of inheriting\r\nMisselthwaite; but he was not an unscrupulous man, though he was a weak\r\none, and he did not intend to let him run into actual danger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must be a strong boy and a steady boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I must know\r\nsomething about him. Who is he? What is his name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary spoke up suddenly. She felt somehow that everybody\r\nwho knew the moor must know Dickon. And she was right, too. She saw\r\nthat in a moment Dr. Craven\xe2\x80\x99s serious face relaxed into a relieved\r\nsmile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is Dickon you will be safe enough. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nas strong as a moor pony, is Dickon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he\xe2\x80\x99s trusty,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 trustiest lad i\xe2\x80\x99 Yorkshire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe had been talking Yorkshire to Colin and she forgot herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Dickon teach you that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Dr. Craven, laughing outright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m learning it as if it was French,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary rather coldly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlike a native dialect in India. Very clever people try to learn them. I\r\nlike it and so does Colin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it amuses you perhaps it won\xe2\x80\x99t do you any\r\nharm. Did you take your bromide last night, Colin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take it at first and after Mary made\r\nme quiet she talked me to sleep\xe2\x80\x94in a low voice\xe2\x80\x94about the spring\r\ncreeping into a garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat sounds soothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven, more perplexed than ever and\r\nglancing sideways at Mistress Mary sitting on her stool and looking\r\ndown silently at the carpet. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are evidently better, but you must\r\nremember\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to remember,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted the Rajah, appearing again.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I lie by myself and remember I begin to have pains everywhere and\r\nI think of things that make me begin to scream because I hate them so.\r\nIf there was a doctor anywhere who could make you forget you were ill\r\ninstead of remembering it I would have him brought here.\xe2\x80\x9d And he waved\r\na thin hand which ought really to have been covered with royal signet\r\nrings made of rubies. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is because my cousin makes me forget that she\r\nmakes me better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven had never made such a short stay after a \xe2\x80\x9ctantrum\xe2\x80\x9d; usually\r\nhe was obliged to remain a very long time and do a great many things.\r\nThis afternoon he did not give any medicine or leave any new orders and\r\nhe was spared any disagreeable scenes. When he went downstairs he\r\nlooked very thoughtful and when he talked to Mrs. Medlock in the\r\nlibrary she felt that he was a much puzzled man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she ventured, \xe2\x80\x9ccould you have believed it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is certainly a new state of affairs,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno denying it is better than the old one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe Susan Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s right\xe2\x80\x94I do that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Medlock. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nstopped in her cottage on my way to Thwaite yesterday and had a bit of\r\ntalk with her. And she says to me, \xe2\x80\x98Well, Sarah Ann, she mayn\xe2\x80\x99t be a\r\ngood child, an\xe2\x80\x99 she mayn\xe2\x80\x99t be a pretty one, but she\xe2\x80\x99s a child, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nchildren needs children.\xe2\x80\x99 We went to school together, Susan Sowerby and\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s the best sick nurse I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I find her\r\nin a cottage I know the chances are that I shall save my patient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Medlock smiled. She was fond of Susan Sowerby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s got a way with her, has Susan,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on quite volubly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen thinking all morning of one thing she said yesterday. She says,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Once when I was givin\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 children a bit of a preach after they\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen fightin\xe2\x80\x99 I ses to \xe2\x80\x99em all, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was at school my jography told\r\nas th\xe2\x80\x99 world was shaped like a orange an\xe2\x80\x99 I found out before I was ten\r\nthat th\xe2\x80\x99 whole orange doesn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to nobody. No one owns more than\r\nhis bit of a quarter an\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99s times it seems like there\xe2\x80\x99s not enow\r\nquarters to go round. But don\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x94none o\xe2\x80\x99 you\xe2\x80\x94think as you own th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwhole orange or you\xe2\x80\x99ll find out you\xe2\x80\x99re mistaken, an\xe2\x80\x99 you won\xe2\x80\x99t find it\r\nout without hard knocks.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x98What children learns from children,\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x98is that there\xe2\x80\x99s no sense in grabbin\xe2\x80\x99 at th\xe2\x80\x99 whole orange\xe2\x80\x94peel\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 all. If you do you\xe2\x80\x99ll likely not get even th\xe2\x80\x99 pips, an\xe2\x80\x99 them\xe2\x80\x99s too\r\nbitter to eat.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a shrewd woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven, putting on his coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she\xe2\x80\x99s got a way of saying things,\xe2\x80\x9d ended Mrs. Medlock, much\r\npleased. \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes I\xe2\x80\x99ve said to her, \xe2\x80\x98Eh! Susan, if you was a\r\ndifferent woman an\xe2\x80\x99 didn\xe2\x80\x99t talk such broad Yorkshire I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen the\r\ntimes when I should have said you was clever.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThat night Colin slept without once awakening and when he opened his\r\neyes in the morning he lay still and smiled without knowing it\xe2\x80\x94smiled\r\nbecause he felt so curiously comfortable. It was actually nice to be\r\nawake, and he turned over and stretched his limbs luxuriously. He felt\r\nas if tight strings which had held him had loosened themselves and let\r\nhim go. He did not know that Dr. Craven would have said that his nerves\r\nhad relaxed and rested themselves. Instead of lying and staring at the\r\nwall and wishing he had not awakened, his mind was full of the plans he\r\nand Mary had made yesterday, of pictures of the garden and of Dickon\r\nand his wild creatures. It was so nice to have things to think about.\r\nAnd he had not been awake more than ten minutes when he heard feet\r\nrunning along the corridor and Mary was at the door. The next minute\r\nshe was in the room and had run across to his bed, bringing with her a\r\nwaft of fresh air full of the scent of the morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been out! You\xe2\x80\x99ve been out! There\xe2\x80\x99s that nice smell of leaves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe cried.\r\n\r\nShe had been running and her hair was loose and blown and she was\r\nbright with the air and pink-cheeked, though he could not see it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, a little breathless with her speed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nnever saw anything so beautiful! It has _come!_ I thought it had come\r\nthat other morning, but it was only coming. It is here now! It has\r\ncome, the Spring! Dickon says so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas it?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin, and though he really knew nothing about it he\r\nfelt his heart beat. He actually sat up in bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOpen the window!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, laughing half with joyful excitement and\r\nhalf at his own fancy. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we may hear golden trumpets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd though he laughed, Mary was at the window in a moment and in a\r\nmoment more it was opened wide and freshness and softness and scents\r\nand birds\xe2\x80\x99 songs were pouring through.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s fresh air,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cLie on your back and draw in long\r\nbreaths of it. That\xe2\x80\x99s what Dickon does when he\xe2\x80\x99s lying on the moor. He\r\nsays he feels it in his veins and it makes him strong and he feels as\r\nif he could live forever and ever. Breathe it and breathe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was only repeating what Dickon had told her, but she caught Colin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Forever and ever\xe2\x80\x99! Does it make him feel like that?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and he\r\ndid as she told him, drawing in long deep breaths over and over again\r\nuntil he felt that something quite new and delightful was happening to\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nMary was at his bedside again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThings are crowding up out of the earth,\xe2\x80\x9d she ran on in a hurry. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nthere are flowers uncurling and buds on everything and the green veil\r\nhas covered nearly all the gray and the birds are in such a hurry about\r\ntheir nests for fear they may be too late that some of them are even\r\nfighting for places in the secret garden. And the rose-bushes look as\r\nwick as wick can be, and there are primroses in the lanes and woods,\r\nand the seeds we planted are up, and Dickon has brought the fox and the\r\ncrow and the squirrels and a new-born lamb.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then she paused for breath. The new-born lamb Dickon had found\r\nthree days before lying by its dead mother among the gorse bushes on\r\nthe moor. It was not the first motherless lamb he had found and he knew\r\nwhat to do with it. He had taken it to the cottage wrapped in his\r\njacket and he had let it lie near the fire and had fed it with warm\r\nmilk. It was a soft thing with a darling silly baby face and legs\r\nrather long for its body. Dickon had carried it over the moor in his\r\narms and its feeding bottle was in his pocket with a squirrel, and when\r\nMary had sat under a tree with its limp warmness huddled on her lap she\r\nhad felt as if she were too full of strange joy to speak. A lamb\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlamb! A living lamb who lay on your lap like a baby!\r\n\r\nShe was describing it with great joy and Colin was listening and\r\ndrawing in long breaths of air when the nurse entered. She started a\r\nlittle at the sight of the open window. She had sat stifling in the\r\nroom many a warm day because her patient was sure that open windows\r\ngave people cold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure you are not chilly, Master Colin?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer. \xe2\x80\x9cI am breathing long breaths of fresh air. It\r\nmakes you strong. I am going to get up to the sofa for breakfast. My\r\ncousin will have breakfast with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nurse went away, concealing a smile, to give the order for two\r\nbreakfasts. She found the servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall a more amusing place than the\r\ninvalid\xe2\x80\x99s chamber and just now everybody wanted to hear the news from\r\nupstairs. There was a great deal of joking about the unpopular young\r\nrecluse who, as the cook said, \xe2\x80\x9chad found his master, and good for\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d The servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall had been very tired of the tantrums, and the\r\nbutler, who was a man with a family, had more than once expressed his\r\nopinion that the invalid would be all the better \xe2\x80\x9cfor a good hiding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Colin was on his sofa and the breakfast for two was put upon the\r\ntable he made an announcement to the nurse in his most Rajah-like\r\nmanner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA boy, and a fox, and a crow, and two squirrels, and a new-born lamb,\r\nare coming to see me this morning. I want them brought upstairs as soon\r\nas they come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not to begin playing with the animals\r\nin the servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall and keep them there. I want them here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nurse gave a slight gasp and tried to conceal it with a cough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what you can do,\xe2\x80\x9d added Colin, waving his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\ntell Martha to bring them here. The boy is Martha\xe2\x80\x99s brother. His name\r\nis Dickon and he is an animal charmer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope the animals won\xe2\x80\x99t bite, Master Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you he was a charmer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin austerely. \xe2\x80\x9cCharmers\xe2\x80\x99 animals\r\nnever bite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are snake-charmers in India,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd they can put their\r\nsnakes\xe2\x80\x99 heads in their mouths.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness!\xe2\x80\x9d shuddered the nurse.\r\n\r\nThey ate their breakfast with the morning air pouring in upon them.\r\nColin\xe2\x80\x99s breakfast was a very good one and Mary watched him with serious\r\ninterest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will begin to get fatter just as I did,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI never wanted\r\nmy breakfast when I was in India and now I always want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted mine this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it was the fresh\r\nair. When do you think Dickon will come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was not long in coming. In about ten minutes Mary held up her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear a caw?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin listened and heard it, the oddest sound in the world to hear\r\ninside a house, a hoarse \xe2\x80\x9ccaw-caw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Soot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cListen again. Do you hear a bleat\xe2\x80\x94a tiny\r\none?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin, quite flushing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the new-born lamb,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon\xe2\x80\x99s moorland boots were thick and clumsy and though he tried to\r\nwalk quietly they made a clumping sound as he walked through the long\r\ncorridors. Mary and Colin heard him marching\xe2\x80\x94marching, until he passed\r\nthrough the tapestry door on to the soft carpet of Colin\xe2\x80\x99s own passage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you please, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Martha, opening the door, \xe2\x80\x9cif you\r\nplease, sir, here\xe2\x80\x99s Dickon an\xe2\x80\x99 his creatures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon came in smiling his nicest wide smile. The new-born lamb was in\r\nhis arms and the little red fox trotted by his side. Nut sat on his\r\nleft shoulder and Soot on his right and Shell\xe2\x80\x99s head and paws peeped\r\nout of his coat pocket.\r\n\r\nColin slowly sat up and stared and stared\xe2\x80\x94as he had stared when he\r\nfirst saw Mary; but this was a stare of wonder and delight. The truth\r\nwas that in spite of all he had heard he had not in the least\r\nunderstood what this boy would be like and that his fox and his crow\r\nand his squirrels and his lamb were so near to him and his friendliness\r\nthat they seemed almost to be part of himself. Colin had never talked\r\nto a boy in his life and he was so overwhelmed by his own pleasure and\r\ncuriosity that he did not even think of speaking.\r\n\r\nBut Dickon did not feel the least shy or awkward. He had not felt\r\nembarrassed because the crow had not known his language and had only\r\nstared and had not spoken to him the first time they met. Creatures\r\nwere always like that until they found out about you. He walked over to\r\nColin\xe2\x80\x99s sofa and put the new-born lamb quietly on his lap, and\r\nimmediately the little creature turned to the warm velvet dressing-gown\r\nand began to nuzzle and nuzzle into its folds and butt its tight-curled\r\nhead with soft impatience against his side. Of course no boy could have\r\nhelped speaking then.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it doing?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wants its mother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon, smiling more and more. \xe2\x80\x9cI brought\r\nit to thee a bit hungry because I knowed tha\xe2\x80\x99d like to see it feed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe knelt down by the sofa and took a feeding-bottle from his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on, little \xe2\x80\x99un,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning the small woolly white head\r\nwith a gentle brown hand. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is what tha\xe2\x80\x99s after. Tha\xe2\x80\x99ll get more\r\nout o\xe2\x80\x99 this than tha\xe2\x80\x99 will out o\xe2\x80\x99 silk velvet coats. There now,\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\npushed the rubber tip of the bottle into the nuzzling mouth and the\r\nlamb began to suck it with ravenous ecstasy.\r\n\r\nAfter that there was no wondering what to say. By the time the lamb\r\nfell asleep questions poured forth and Dickon answered them all. He\r\ntold them how he had found the lamb just as the sun was rising three\r\nmornings ago. He had been standing on the moor listening to a skylark\r\nand watching him swing higher and higher into the sky until he was only\r\na speck in the heights of blue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d almost lost him but for his song an\xe2\x80\x99 I was wonderin\xe2\x80\x99 how a chap\r\ncould hear it when it seemed as if he\xe2\x80\x99d get out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 world in a\r\nminute\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 just then I heard somethin\xe2\x80\x99 else far off among th\xe2\x80\x99 gorse\r\nbushes. It was a weak bleatin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 I knowed it was a new lamb as was\r\nhungry an\xe2\x80\x99 I knowed it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be hungry if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t lost its mother\r\nsomehow, so I set off searchin\xe2\x80\x99. Eh! I did have a look for it. I went\r\nin an\xe2\x80\x99 out among th\xe2\x80\x99 gorse bushes an\xe2\x80\x99 round an\xe2\x80\x99 round an\xe2\x80\x99 I always\r\nseemed to take th\xe2\x80\x99 wrong turnin\xe2\x80\x99. Captain curled up near Dickon, who sat on the hearth-rug from\r\npreference.\r\n\r\nThey looked at the pictures in the gardening books and Dickon knew all\r\nthe flowers by their country names and knew exactly which ones were\r\nalready growing in the secret garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldna\xe2\x80\x99 say that there name,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pointing to one under which\r\nwas written \xe2\x80\x9cAquilegia,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cbut us calls that a columbine, an\xe2\x80\x99 that there\r\none it\xe2\x80\x99s a snapdragon and they both grow wild in hedges, but these is\r\ngarden ones an\xe2\x80\x99 they\xe2\x80\x99re bigger an\xe2\x80\x99 grander. There\xe2\x80\x99s some big clumps o\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncolumbine in th\xe2\x80\x99 garden. They\xe2\x80\x99ll look like a bed o\xe2\x80\x99 blue an\xe2\x80\x99 white\r\nbutterflies flutterin\xe2\x80\x99 when they\xe2\x80\x99re out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to see them,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to see them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that tha\xe2\x80\x99 mun,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary quite seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 munnot lose\r\nno time about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI SHALL LIVE FOREVER\xe2\x80\x94AND EVER\xe2\x80\x94AND EVER!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nBut they were obliged to wait more than a week because first there came\r\nsome very windy days and then Colin was threatened with a cold, which\r\ntwo things happening one after the other would no doubt have thrown him\r\ninto a rage but that there was so much careful and mysterious planning\r\nto do and almost every day Dickon came in, if only for a few minutes,\r\nto talk about what was happening on the moor and in the lanes and\r\nhedges and on the borders of streams. The things he had to tell about\r\notters\xe2\x80\x99 and badgers\xe2\x80\x99 and water-rats\xe2\x80\x99 houses, not to mention birds\xe2\x80\x99\r\nnests and field-mice and their burrows, were enough to make you almost\r\ntremble with excitement when you heard all the intimate details from an\r\nanimal charmer and realized with what thrilling eagerness and anxiety\r\nthe whole busy underworld was working.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re same as us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon, \xe2\x80\x9conly they have to build their homes\r\nevery year. An\xe2\x80\x99 it keeps \xe2\x80\x99em so busy they fair scuffle to get \xe2\x80\x99em\r\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe most absorbing thing, however, was the preparations to be made\r\nbefore Colin could be transported with sufficient secrecy to the\r\ngarden. No one must see the chair-carriage and Dickon and Mary after\r\nthey turned a certain corner of the shrubbery and entered upon the walk\r\noutside the ivied walls. As each day passed, Colin had become more and\r\nmore fixed in his feeling that the mystery surrounding the garden was\r\none of its greatest charms. Nothing must spoil that. No one must ever\r\nsuspect that they had a secret. People must think that he was simply\r\ngoing out with Mary and Dickon because he liked them and did not object\r\nto their looking at him. They had long and quite delightful talks about\r\ntheir route. They would go up this path and down that one and cross the\r\nother and go round among the fountain flower-beds as if they were\r\nlooking at the \xe2\x80\x9cbedding-out plants\xe2\x80\x9d the head gardener, Mr. Roach, had\r\nbeen having arranged. That would seem such a rational thing to do that\r\nno one would think it at all mysterious. They would turn into the\r\nshrubbery walks and lose themselves until they came to the long walls.\r\nIt was almost as serious and elaborately thought out as the plans of\r\nmarch made by great generals in time of war.\r\n\r\nRumors of the new and curious things which were occurring in the\r\ninvalid\xe2\x80\x99s apartments had of course filtered through the servants\xe2\x80\x99 hall\r\ninto the stable yards and out among the gardeners, but notwithstanding\r\nthis, Mr. Roach was startled one day when he received orders from\r\nMaster Colin\xe2\x80\x99s room to the effect that he must report himself in the\r\napartment no outsider had ever seen, as the invalid himself desired to\r\nspeak to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself as he hurriedly changed his coat,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s to do now? His Royal Highness that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t to be looked at\r\ncalling up a man he\xe2\x80\x99s never set eyes on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Roach was not without curiosity. He had never caught even a glimpse\r\nof the boy and had heard a dozen exaggerated stories about his uncanny\r\nlooks and ways and his insane tempers. The thing he had heard oftenest\r\nwas that he might die at any moment and there had been numerous\r\nfanciful descriptions of a humped back and helpless limbs, given by\r\npeople who had never seen him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThings are changing in this house, Mr. Roach,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Medlock, as\r\nshe led him up the back staircase to the corridor on to which opened\r\nthe hitherto mysterious chamber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s hope they\xe2\x80\x99re changing for the better, Mrs. Medlock,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nanswered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey couldn\xe2\x80\x99t well change for the worse,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued; \xe2\x80\x9cand queer as\r\nit all is there\xe2\x80\x99s them as finds their duties made a lot easier to stand\r\nup under. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you be surprised, Mr. Roach, if you find yourself in\r\nthe middle of a menagerie and Martha Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s Dickon more at home than\r\nyou or me could ever be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere really was a sort of Magic about Dickon, as Mary always privately\r\nbelieved. When Mr. Roach heard his name he smiled quite leniently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d be at home in Buckingham Palace or at the bottom of a coal mine,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet it\xe2\x80\x99s not impudence, either. He\xe2\x80\x99s just fine, is that\r\nlad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was perhaps well he had been prepared or he might have been\r\nstartled. When the bedroom door was opened a large crow, which seemed\r\nquite at home perched on the high back of a carven chair, announced the\r\nentrance of a visitor by saying \xe2\x80\x9cCaw\xe2\x80\x94Caw\xe2\x80\x9d quite loudly. In spite of\r\nMrs. Medlock\xe2\x80\x99s warning, Mr. Roach only just escaped being sufficiently\r\nundignified to jump backward.\r\n\r\nThe young Rajah was neither in bed nor on his sofa. He was sitting in\r\nan armchair and a young lamb was standing by him shaking its tail in\r\nfeeding-lamb fashion as Dickon knelt giving it milk from its bottle. A\r\nsquirrel was perched on Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s bent back attentively nibbling a nut.\r\nThe little girl from India was sitting on a big footstool looking on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is Mr. Roach, Master Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Medlock.\r\n\r\nThe young Rajah turned and looked his servitor over\xe2\x80\x94at least that was\r\nwhat the head gardener felt happened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are Roach, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI sent for you to give you some\r\nvery important orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Roach, wondering if he was to receive\r\ninstructions to fell all the oaks in the park or to transform the\r\norchards into water-gardens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going out in my chair this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cIf the fresh\r\nair agrees with me I may go out every day. When I go, none of the\r\ngardeners are to be anywhere near the Long Walk by the garden walls. No\r\none is to be there. I shall go out about two o\xe2\x80\x99clock and everyone must\r\nkeep away until I send word that they may go back to their work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Roach, much relieved to hear that the\r\noaks might remain and that the orchards were safe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, turning to her, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is that thing you say in\r\nIndia when you have finished talking and want people to go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say, \xe2\x80\x98You have my permission to go,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary.\r\n\r\nThe Rajah waved his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have my permission to go, Roach,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, remember, this is\r\nvery important.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaw\xe2\x80\x94Caw!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the crow hoarsely but not impolitely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, sir. And\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll be likely to find out the size of his own quarter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInside the room Colin was leaning back on his cushions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all safe now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd this afternoon I shall see it\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nafternoon I shall be in it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon went back to the garden with his creatures and Mary stayed with\r\nColin. She did not think he looked tired but he was very quiet before\r\ntheir lunch came and he was quiet while they were eating it. She\r\nwondered why and asked him about it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat big eyes you\xe2\x80\x99ve got, Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you are thinking\r\nthey get as big as saucers. What are you thinking about now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help thinking about what it will look like,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe garden?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe springtime,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking that I\xe2\x80\x99ve really never seen\r\nit before. I scarcely ever went out and when I did go I never looked at\r\nit. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t even think about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw it in India because there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nShut in and morbid as his life had been, Colin had more imagination\r\nthan she had and at least he had spent a good deal of time looking at\r\nwonderful books and pictures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat morning when you ran in and said \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s come! I\xe2\x80\x99ve a picture like\r\nit in one of my books\xe2\x80\x94crowds of lovely people and children with\r\ngarlands and branches with blossoms on them, everyone laughing and\r\ndancing and crowding and playing on pipes.
