I\xe2\x80\x99m sure\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d dance and sing and flute and that would be the wafts of music.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey both laughed but it was not because the idea was laughable but\r\nbecause they both so liked it.\r\n\r\nA little later the nurse made Colin ready. She noticed that instead of\r\nlying like a log while his clothes were put on he sat up and made some\r\nefforts to help himself, and he talked and laughed with Mary all the\r\ntime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is one of his good days, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Dr. Craven, who\r\ndropped in to inspect him. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s in such good spirits that it makes him\r\nstronger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll call in again later in the afternoon, after he has come in,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDr. Craven. \xe2\x80\x9cI must see how the going out agrees with him. I wish,\xe2\x80\x9d in\r\na very low voice, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he would let you go with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather give up the case this moment, sir, than even stay here\r\nwhile it\xe2\x80\x99s suggested,\xe2\x80\x9d answered the nurse. With sudden firmness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t really decided to suggest it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor, with his\r\nslight nervousness. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll try the experiment. Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s a lad I\xe2\x80\x99d trust\r\nwith a new-born child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe strongest footman in the house carried Colin downstairs and put him\r\nin his wheeled chair near which Dickon waited outside. After the\r\nmanservant had arranged his rugs and cushions the Rajah waved his hand\r\nto him and to the nurse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have my permission to go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and they both disappeared\r\nquickly and it must be confessed giggled when they were safely inside\r\nthe house.\r\n\r\nDickon began to push the wheeled chair slowly and steadily. Mistress\r\nMary walked beside it and Colin leaned back and lifted his face to the\r\nsky. The arch of it looked very high and the small snowy clouds seemed\r\nlike white birds floating on outspread wings below its crystal\r\nblueness. The wind swept in soft big breaths down from the moor and was\r\nstrange with a wild clear scented sweetness. Colin kept lifting his\r\nthin chest to draw it in, and his big eyes looked as if it were they\r\nwhich were listening\xe2\x80\x94listening, instead of his ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are so many sounds of singing and humming and calling out,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that scent the puffs of wind bring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s gorse on th\xe2\x80\x99 moor that\xe2\x80\x99s openin\xe2\x80\x99 out,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbees are at it wonderful today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot a human creature was to be caught sight of in the paths they took.\r\nIn fact every gardener or gardener\xe2\x80\x99s lad had been witched away. But\r\nthey wound in and out among the shrubbery and out and round the\r\nfountain beds, following their carefully planned route for the mere\r\nmysterious pleasure of it. But when at last they turned into the Long\r\nWalk by the ivied walls the excited sense of an approaching thrill made\r\nthem, for some curious reason they could not have explained, begin to\r\nspeak in whispers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is it,\xe2\x80\x9d breathed Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is where I used to walk up and down\r\nand wonder and wonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin, and his eyes began to search the ivy with eager\r\ncuriousness. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I can see nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I thought,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\nThen there was a lovely breathless silence and the chair wheeled on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the garden where Ben Weatherstaff works,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\nA few yards more and Mary whispered again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is where the robin flew over the wall,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cOh! I wish he\xe2\x80\x99d come again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary with solemn delight, pointing under a big lilac\r\nbush, \xe2\x80\x9cis where he perched on the little heap of earth and showed me\r\nthe key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Colin sat up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere? Where? There?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and his eyes were as big as the wolf\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin Red Riding-Hood, when Red Riding-Hood felt called upon to remark on\r\nthem. Dickon stood still and the wheeled chair stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, stepping on to the bed close to the ivy, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nwhere I went to talk to him when he chirped at me from the top of the\r\nwall. And this is the ivy the wind blew back,\xe2\x80\x9d and she took hold of the\r\nhanging green curtain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! is it\xe2\x80\x94is it!\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here is the handle, and here is the door. Dickon push him in\xe2\x80\x94push\r\nhim in quickly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Dickon did it with one strong, steady, splendid push.\r\n\r\nBut Colin had actually dropped back against his cushions, even though\r\nhe gasped with delight, and he had covered his eyes with his hands and\r\nheld them there shutting out everything until they were inside and the\r\nchair stopped as if by magic and the door was closed. Not till then did\r\nhe take them away and look round and round and round as Dickon and Mary\r\nhad done. And over walls and earth and trees and swinging sprays and\r\ntendrils the fair green veil of tender little leaves had crept, and in\r\nthe grass under the trees and the gray urns in the alcoves and here and\r\nthere everywhere were touches or splashes of gold and purple and white\r\nand the trees were showing pink and snow above his head and there were\r\nfluttering of wings and faint sweet pipes and humming and scents and\r\nscents. And the sun fell warm upon his face like a hand with a lovely\r\ntouch. And in wonder Mary and Dickon stood and stared at him. He looked\r\nso strange and different because a pink glow of color had actually\r\ncrept all over him\xe2\x80\x94ivory face and neck and hands and all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall get well! I shall get well!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out. \xe2\x80\x9cMary! Dickon! I\r\nshall get well! And I shall live forever and ever and ever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\nBEN WEATHERSTAFF\r\n\r\n\r\nOne of the strange things about living in the world is that it is only\r\nnow and then one is quite sure one is going to live forever and ever\r\nand ever. One knows it sometimes when one gets up at the tender solemn\r\ndawn-time and goes out and stands alone and throws one\xe2\x80\x99s head far back\r\nand looks up and up and watches the pale sky slowly changing and\r\nflushing and marvelous unknown things happening until the East almost\r\nmakes one cry out and one\xe2\x80\x99s heart stands still at the strange\r\nunchanging majesty of the rising of the sun\xe2\x80\x94which has been happening\r\nevery morning for thousands and thousands and thousands of years. One\r\nknows it then for a moment or so. And one knows it sometimes when one\r\nstands by oneself in a wood at sunset and the mysterious deep gold\r\nstillness slanting through and under the branches seems to be saying\r\nslowly again and again something one cannot quite hear, however much\r\none tries. Then sometimes the immense quiet of the dark blue at night\r\nwith millions of stars waiting and watching makes one sure; and\r\nsometimes a sound of far-off music makes it true; and sometimes a look\r\nin someone\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\nAnd it was like that with Colin when he first saw and heard and felt\r\nthe Springtime inside the four high walls of a hidden garden. That\r\nafternoon the whole world seemed to devote itself to being perfect and\r\nradiantly beautiful and kind to one boy. Perhaps out of pure heavenly\r\ngoodness the spring came and crowded everything it possibly could into\r\nthat one place. More than once Dickon paused in what he was doing and\r\nstood still with a sort of growing wonder in his eyes, shaking his head\r\nsoftly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! it is graidely,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m twelve goin\xe2\x80\x99 on thirteen an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a lot o\xe2\x80\x99 afternoons in thirteen years, but seems to me like I\r\nnever seed one as graidely as this \xe2\x80\x99ere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, it is a graidely one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, and she sighed for mere joy.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant it\xe2\x80\x99s the graidelest one as ever was in this world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes tha\xe2\x80\x99 think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin with dreamy carefulness, \xe2\x80\x9cas happen it\r\nwas made loike this \xe2\x80\x99ere all o\xe2\x80\x99 purpose for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mary admiringly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 good\r\nYorkshire. Tha\xe2\x80\x99rt shapin\xe2\x80\x99 first-rate\xe2\x80\x94that tha\xe2\x80\x99 art.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd delight reigned.\r\n\r\nThey drew the chair under the plum-tree, which was snow-white with\r\nblossoms and musical with bees. It was like a king\xe2\x80\x99s canopy, a fairy\r\nking\xe2\x80\x99s. There were flowering cherry-trees near and apple-trees whose\r\nbuds were pink and white, and here and there one had burst open wide.\r\nBetween the blossoming branches of the canopy bits of blue sky looked\r\ndown like wonderful eyes.\r\n\r\nMary and Dickon worked a little here and there and Colin watched them.\r\nThey brought him things to look at\xe2\x80\x94buds which were opening, buds which\r\nwere tight closed, bits of twig whose leaves were just showing green,\r\nthe feather of a woodpecker which had dropped on the grass, the empty\r\nshell of some bird early hatched. Dickon pushed the chair slowly round\r\nand round the garden, stopping every other moment to let him look at\r\nwonders springing out of the earth or trailing down from trees. It was\r\nlike being taken in state round the country of a magic king and queen\r\nand shown all the mysterious riches it contained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if we shall see the robin?\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99ll see him often enow after a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen th\xe2\x80\x99\r\neggs hatches out th\xe2\x80\x99 little chap he\xe2\x80\x99ll be kep\xe2\x80\x99 so busy it\xe2\x80\x99ll make his\r\nhead swim. Tha\xe2\x80\x99ll see him flyin\xe2\x80\x99 backward an\xe2\x80\x99 for\xe2\x80\x99ard carryin\xe2\x80\x99 worms\r\nnigh as big as himsel\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 that much noise goin\xe2\x80\x99 on in th\xe2\x80\x99 nest when he\r\ngets there as fair flusters him so as he scarce knows which big mouth\r\nto drop th\xe2\x80\x99 first piece in. An\xe2\x80\x99 gapin\xe2\x80\x99 beaks an\xe2\x80\x99 squawks on every side.\r\nMother says as when she sees th\xe2\x80\x99 work a robin has to keep them gapin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbeaks filled, she feels like she was a lady with nothin\xe2\x80\x99 to do. She\r\nsays she\xe2\x80\x99s seen th\xe2\x80\x99 little chaps when it seemed like th\xe2\x80\x99 sweat must be\r\ndroppin\xe2\x80\x99 off \xe2\x80\x99em, though folk can\xe2\x80\x99t see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis made them giggle so delightedly that they were obliged to cover\r\ntheir mouths with their hands, remembering that they must not be heard.\r\nColin had been instructed as to the law of whispers and low voices\r\nseveral days before. He liked the mysteriousness of it and did his\r\nbest, but in the midst of excited enjoyment it is rather difficult\r\nnever to laugh above a whisper.\r\n\r\nEvery moment of the afternoon was full of new things and every hour the\r\nsunshine grew more golden. The wheeled chair had been drawn back under\r\nthe canopy and Dickon had sat down on the grass and had just drawn out\r\nhis pipe when Colin saw something he had not had time to notice before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a very old tree over there, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nDickon looked across the grass at the tree and Mary looked and there\r\nwas a brief moment of stillness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dickon, after it, and his low voice had a very gentle\r\nsound.\r\n\r\nMary gazed at the tree and thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe branches are quite gray and there\xe2\x80\x99s not a single leaf anywhere,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nColin went on. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite dead, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cBut them roses as has climbed all over it will\r\nnear hide every bit o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 dead wood when they\xe2\x80\x99re full o\xe2\x80\x99 leaves an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nflowers. It won\xe2\x80\x99t look dead then. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be th\xe2\x80\x99 prettiest of all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary still gazed at the tree and thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks as if a big branch had been broken off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwonder how it was done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s been done many a year,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d with a sudden\r\nrelieved start and laying his hand on Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at that robin! There\r\nhe is! He\xe2\x80\x99s been foragin\xe2\x80\x99 for his mate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin was almost too late but he just caught sight of him, the flash of\r\nred-breasted bird with something in his beak. He darted through the\r\ngreenness and into the close-grown corner and was out of sight. Colin\r\nleaned back on his cushion again, laughing a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s taking her tea to her. Perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s five o\xe2\x80\x99clock. I think I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlike some tea myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so they were safe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was Magic which sent the robin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary secretly to Dickon\r\nafterward. \xe2\x80\x9cI know it was Magic.\xe2\x80\x9d For both she and Dickon had been\r\nafraid Colin might ask something about the tree whose branch had broken\r\noff ten years ago and they had talked it over together and Dickon had\r\nstood and rubbed his head in a troubled way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe mun look as if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t no different from th\xe2\x80\x99 other trees,\xe2\x80\x9d he had\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cWe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t never tell him how it broke, poor lad. If he says\r\nanything about it we mun\xe2\x80\x94we mun try to look cheerful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that we mun,\xe2\x80\x9d had answered Mary.\r\n\r\nBut she had not felt as if she looked cheerful when she gazed at the\r\ntree. She wondered and wondered in those few moments if there was any\r\nreality in that other thing Dickon had said. He had gone on rubbing his\r\nrust-red hair in a puzzled way, but a nice comforted look had begun to\r\ngrow in his blue eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Craven was a very lovely young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d he had gone on rather\r\nhesitatingly. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 mother she thinks maybe she\xe2\x80\x99s about Misselthwaite\r\nmany a time lookin\xe2\x80\x99 after Mester Colin, same as all mothers do when\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re took out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 world. They have to come back, tha\xe2\x80\x99 sees.\r\nHappen she\xe2\x80\x99s been in the garden an\xe2\x80\x99 happen it was her set us to work,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 told us to bring him here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had thought he meant something about Magic. She was a great\r\nbeliever in Magic. Secretly she quite believed that Dickon worked\r\nMagic, of course good Magic, on everything near him and that was why\r\npeople liked him so much and wild creatures knew he was their friend.\r\nShe wondered, indeed, if it were not possible that his gift had brought\r\nthe robin just at the right moment when Colin asked that dangerous\r\nquestion. She felt that his Magic was working all the afternoon and\r\nmaking Colin look like an entirely different boy. It did not seem\r\npossible that he could be the crazy creature who had screamed and\r\nbeaten and bitten his pillow. Even his ivory whiteness seemed to\r\nchange. The faint glow of color which had shown on his face and neck\r\nand hands when he first got inside the garden really never quite died\r\naway. He looked as if he were made of flesh instead of ivory or wax.\r\n\r\nThey saw the robin carry food to his mate two or three times, and it\r\nwas so suggestive of afternoon tea that Colin felt they must have some.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and make one of the men servants bring some in a basket to the\r\nrhododendron walk,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then you and Dickon can bring it\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was an agreeable idea, easily carried out, and when the white cloth\r\nwas spread upon the grass, with hot tea and buttered toast and\r\ncrumpets, a delightfully hungry meal was eaten, and several birds on\r\ndomestic errands paused to inquire what was going on and were led into\r\ninvestigating crumbs with great activity. Nut and Shell whisked up\r\ntrees with pieces of cake and Soot took the entire half of a buttered\r\ncrumpet into a corner and pecked at and examined and turned it over and\r\nmade hoarse remarks about it until he decided to swallow it all\r\njoyfully in one gulp.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was dragging towards its mellow hour. The sun was\r\ndeepening the gold of its lances, the bees were going home and the\r\nbirds were flying past less often. Dickon and Mary were sitting on the\r\ngrass, the tea-basket was repacked ready to be taken back to the house,\r\nand Colin was lying against his cushions with his heavy locks pushed\r\nback from his forehead and his face looking quite a natural color.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want this afternoon to go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I shall come back\r\ntomorrow, and the day after, and the day after, and the day after.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll get plenty of fresh air, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to get nothing else,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve seen the spring now\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99m going to see the summer. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to see everything grow here.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m going to grow here myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat tha\xe2\x80\x99 will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cUs\xe2\x80\x99ll have thee walkin\xe2\x80\x99 about here an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndiggin\xe2\x80\x99 same as other folk afore long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin flushed tremendously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalk!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cDig! Shall I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon\xe2\x80\x99s glance at him was delicately cautious. Neither he nor Mary had\r\never asked if anything was the matter with his legs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor sure tha\xe2\x80\x99 will,\xe2\x80\x9d he said stoutly. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x94tha\xe2\x80\x99s got legs o\xe2\x80\x99 thine\r\nown, same as other folks!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary was rather frightened until she heard Colin\xe2\x80\x99s answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing really ails them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they are so thin and weak.\r\nThey shake so that I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid to try to stand on them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth Mary and Dickon drew a relieved breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen tha\xe2\x80\x99 stops bein\xe2\x80\x99 afraid tha\xe2\x80\x99lt stand on \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon said with\r\nrenewed cheer. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99lt stop bein\xe2\x80\x99 afraid in a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall?\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, and he lay still as if he were wondering about\r\nthings.\r\n\r\nThey were really very quiet for a little while. The sun was dropping\r\nlower. It was that hour when everything stills itself, and they really\r\nhad had a busy and exciting afternoon. Colin looked as if he were\r\nresting luxuriously. Even the creatures had ceased moving about and had\r\ndrawn together and were resting near them. Soot had perched on a low\r\nbranch and drawn up one leg and dropped the gray film drowsily over his\r\neyes. Mary privately thought he looked as if he might snore in a\r\nminute.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of this stillness it was rather startling when Colin half\r\nlifted his head and exclaimed in a loud suddenly alarmed whisper:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon and Mary scrambled to their feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan!\xe2\x80\x9d they both cried in low quick voices.\r\n\r\nColin pointed to the high wall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered excitedly. \xe2\x80\x9cJust look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary and Dickon wheeled about and looked. There was Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nindignant face glaring at them over the wall from the top of a ladder!\r\nHe actually shook his fist at Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a bachelder, an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 was a wench o\xe2\x80\x99 mine,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ngive thee a hidin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe mounted another step threateningly as if it were his energetic\r\nintention to jump down and deal with her; but as she came toward him he\r\nevidently thought better of it and stood on the top step of his ladder\r\nshaking his fist down at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thowt much o\xe2\x80\x99 thee!\xe2\x80\x9d he harangued. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldna\xe2\x80\x99 abide thee th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfirst time I set eyes on thee. A scrawny buttermilk-faced young besom,\r\nallus askin\xe2\x80\x99 questions an\xe2\x80\x99 pokin\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 nose where it wasna, wanted. I\r\nnever knowed how tha\xe2\x80\x99 got so thick wi\xe2\x80\x99 me. If it hadna\xe2\x80\x99 been for th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nrobin\xe2\x80\x94 Drat him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBen Weatherstaff,\xe2\x80\x9d called out Mary, finding her breath. She stood\r\nbelow him and called up to him with a sort of gasp. \xe2\x80\x9cBen Weatherstaff,\r\nit was the robin who showed me the way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen it did seem as if Ben really would scramble down on her side of\r\nthe wall, he was so outraged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 young bad \xe2\x80\x99un!\xe2\x80\x9d he called down at her. \xe2\x80\x9cLayin\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 badness on a\r\nrobin\xe2\x80\x94not but what he\xe2\x80\x99s impidint enow for anythin\xe2\x80\x99. Him showin\xe2\x80\x99 thee\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 way! Him! Eh! tha\xe2\x80\x99 young nowt\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she could see his next words burst\r\nout because he was overpowered by curiosity\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9chowever i\xe2\x80\x99 this world did\r\ntha\xe2\x80\x99 get in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the robin who showed me the way,\xe2\x80\x9d she protested obstinately.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe didn\xe2\x80\x99t know he was doing it but he did. And I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you from\r\nhere while you\xe2\x80\x99re shaking your fist at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped shaking his fist very suddenly at that very moment and his\r\njaw actually dropped as he stared over her head at something he saw\r\ncoming over the grass toward him.\r\n\r\nAt the first sound of his torrent of words Colin had been so surprised\r\nthat he had only sat up and listened as if he were spellbound. But in\r\nthe midst of it he had recovered himself and beckoned imperiously to\r\nDickon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWheel me over there!\xe2\x80\x9d he commanded. \xe2\x80\x9cWheel me quite close and stop\r\nright in front of him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd this, if you please, this is what Ben Weatherstaff beheld and which\r\nmade his jaw drop. A wheeled chair with luxurious cushions and robes\r\nwhich came toward him looking rather like some sort of State Coach\r\nbecause a young Rajah leaned back in it with royal command in his great\r\nblack-rimmed eyes and a thin white hand extended haughtily toward him.\r\nAnd it stopped right under Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s nose. It was really no\r\nwonder his mouth dropped open.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know who I am?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the Rajah.\r\n\r\nHow Ben Weatherstaff stared! His red old eyes fixed themselves on what\r\nwas before him as if he were seeing a ghost. He gazed and gazed and\r\ngulped a lump down his throat and did not say a word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know who I am?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Colin still more imperiously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff put his gnarled hand up and passed it over his eyes\r\nand over his forehead and then he did answer in a queer shaky voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho tha\xe2\x80\x99 art?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAye, that I do\xe2\x80\x94wi\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 mother\xe2\x80\x99s eyes starin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nat me out o\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 face. Lord knows how tha\xe2\x80\x99 come here. But tha\xe2\x80\x99rt th\xe2\x80\x99\r\npoor cripple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin forgot that he had ever had a back. His face flushed scarlet and\r\nhe sat bolt upright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not a cripple!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried out furiously. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mary, almost shouting up the wall in her fierce\r\nindignation. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not got a lump as big as a pin! I looked and there\r\nwas none there\xe2\x80\x94not one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff passed his hand over his forehead again and gazed as\r\nif he could never gaze enough. His hand shook and his mouth shook and\r\nhis voice shook. He was an ignorant old man and a tactless old man and\r\nhe could only remember the things he had heard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94tha\xe2\x80\x99 hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got a crooked back?\xe2\x80\x9d he said hoarsely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94tha\xe2\x80\x99 hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got crooked legs?\xe2\x80\x9d quavered Ben more hoarsely yet.\r\n\r\nIt was too much. The strength which Colin usually threw into his\r\ntantrums rushed through him now in a new way. Never yet had he been\r\naccused of crooked legs\xe2\x80\x94even in whispers\xe2\x80\x94and the perfectly simple\r\nbelief in their existence which was revealed by Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvoice was more than Rajah flesh and blood could endure. His anger and\r\ninsulted pride made him forget everything but this one moment and\r\nfilled him with a power he had never known before, an almost unnatural\r\nstrength.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted to Dickon, and he actually began to tear the\r\ncoverings off his lower limbs and disentangle himself. \xe2\x80\x9cCome here! Come\r\nhere! This minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon was by his side in a second. Mary caught her breath in a short\r\ngasp and felt herself turn pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can do it! He can do it! He can do it! He can!\xe2\x80\x9d she gabbled over to\r\nherself under her breath as fast as ever she could.\r\n\r\nThere was a brief fierce scramble, the rugs were tossed on the ground,\r\nDickon held Colin\xe2\x80\x99s arm, the thin legs were out, the thin feet were on\r\nthe grass. Colin was standing upright\xe2\x80\x94upright\xe2\x80\x94as straight as an arrow\r\nand looking strangely tall\xe2\x80\x94his head thrown back and his strange eyes\r\nflashing lightning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at me!\xe2\x80\x9d he flung up at Ben Weatherstaff. \xe2\x80\x9cJust look at me\xe2\x80\x94you!\r\nJust look at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s as straight as I am!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s as straight as any lad\r\ni\xe2\x80\x99 Yorkshire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat Ben Weatherstaff did Mary thought queer beyond measure. He choked\r\nand gulped and suddenly tears ran down his weather-wrinkled cheeks as\r\nhe struck his old hands together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he burst forth, \xe2\x80\x9cth\xe2\x80\x99 lies folk tells! Tha\xe2\x80\x99rt as thin as a lath\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 as white as a wraith, but there\xe2\x80\x99s not a knob on thee. Tha\xe2\x80\x99lt make a\r\nmon yet. God bless thee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon held Colin\xe2\x80\x99s arm strongly but the boy had not begun to falter.\r\nHe stood straighter and straighter and looked Ben Weatherstaff in the\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m your master,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen my father is away. And you are to\r\nobey me. This is my garden. Don\xe2\x80\x99t dare to say a word about it! You get\r\ndown from that ladder and go out to the Long Walk and Miss Mary will\r\nmeet you and bring you here. I want to talk to you. We did not want\r\nyou, but now you will have to be in the secret. Be quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s crabbed old face was still wet with that one queer\r\nrush of tears. It seemed as if he could not take his eyes from thin\r\nstraight Colin standing on his feet with his head thrown back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! lad,\xe2\x80\x9d he almost whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! my lad!\xe2\x80\x9d And then remembering\r\nhimself he suddenly touched his hat gardener fashion and said, \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nsir! Yes, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d and obediently disappeared as he descended the ladder.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\nWHEN THE SUN WENT DOWN\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen his head was out of sight Colin turned to Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and meet him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; and Mary flew across the grass to the door\r\nunder the ivy.\r\n\r\nDickon was watching him with sharp eyes. There were scarlet spots on\r\nhis cheeks and he looked amazing, but he showed no signs of falling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can stand,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and his head was still held up and he said it\r\nquite grandly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told thee tha\xe2\x80\x99 could as soon as tha\xe2\x80\x99 stopped bein\xe2\x80\x99 afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d answered\r\nDickon. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99s stopped.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ve stopped,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\nThen suddenly he remembered something Mary had said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you making Magic?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked sharply.\r\n\r\nDickon\xe2\x80\x99s curly mouth spread in a cheerful grin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99s doin\xe2\x80\x99 Magic thysel\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s same Magic as made these\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ere work out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 earth,\xe2\x80\x9d and he touched with his thick boot a clump\r\nof crocuses in the grass.\r\n\r\nColin looked down at them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cthere couldna\xe2\x80\x99 be bigger Magic than that\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94there couldna\xe2\x80\x99 be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew himself up straighter than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to walk to that tree,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, pointing to one a few feet\r\naway from him. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to be standing when Weatherstaff comes here.\r\nI can rest against the tree if I like. When I want to sit down I will\r\nsit down, but not before. Bring a rug from the chair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked to the tree and though Dickon held his arm he was wonderfully\r\nsteady. When he stood against the tree trunk it was not too plain that\r\nhe supported himself against it, and he still held himself so straight\r\nthat he looked tall.\r\n\r\nWhen Ben Weatherstaff came through the door in the wall he saw him\r\nstanding there and he heard Mary muttering something under her breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat art sayin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked rather testily because he did not want his\r\nattention distracted from the long thin straight boy figure and proud\r\nface.\r\n\r\nBut she did not tell him. What she was saying was this:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can do it! You can do it! I told you you could! You can do it! You\r\ncan do it! You _can!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was saying it to Colin because she wanted to make Magic and keep\r\nhim on his feet looking like that. She could not bear that he should\r\ngive in before Ben Weatherstaff. He did not give in. She was uplifted\r\nby a sudden feeling that he looked quite beautiful in spite of his\r\nthinness. He fixed his eyes on Ben Weatherstaff in his funny imperious\r\nway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at me!\xe2\x80\x9d he commanded. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at me all over! Am I a hunchback?\r\nHave I got crooked legs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff had not quite got over his emotion, but he had\r\nrecovered a little and answered almost in his usual way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot tha\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cNowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 sort. What\xe2\x80\x99s tha\xe2\x80\x99 been doin\xe2\x80\x99 with\r\nthysel\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94hidin\xe2\x80\x99 out o\xe2\x80\x99 sight an\xe2\x80\x99 lettin\xe2\x80\x99 folk think tha\xe2\x80\x99 was cripple an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhalf-witted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf-witted!\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cWho thought that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLots o\xe2\x80\x99 fools,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben. \xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 world\xe2\x80\x99s full o\xe2\x80\x99 jackasses brayin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthey never bray nowt but lies. What did tha\xe2\x80\x99 shut thysel\xe2\x80\x99 up for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone thought I was going to die,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he said it with such decision Ben Weatherstaff looked him over, up\r\nand down, down and up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 die!\xe2\x80\x9d he said with dry exultation. \xe2\x80\x9cNowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 sort! Tha\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\ntoo much pluck in thee. When I seed thee put tha\xe2\x80\x99 legs on th\xe2\x80\x99 ground in\r\nsuch a hurry I knowed tha\xe2\x80\x99 was all right. Sit thee down on th\xe2\x80\x99 rug a\r\nbit young Mester an\xe2\x80\x99 give me thy orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a queer mixture of crabbed tenderness and shrewd\r\nunderstanding in his manner. Mary had poured out speech as rapidly as\r\nshe could as they had come down the Long Walk. The chief thing to be\r\nremembered, she had told him, was that Colin was getting well\xe2\x80\x94getting\r\nwell. The garden was doing it. No one must let him remember about\r\nhaving humps and dying.\r\n\r\nThe Rajah condescended to seat himself on a rug under the tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat work do you do in the gardens, Weatherstaff?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnythin\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m told to do,\xe2\x80\x9d answered old Ben. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m kep\xe2\x80\x99 on by\r\nfavor\xe2\x80\x94because she liked me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe?\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 mother,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ben Weatherstaff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother?\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, and he looked about him quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis was her\r\ngarden, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, it was that!\xe2\x80\x9d and Ben Weatherstaff looked about him too. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\nwere main fond of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my garden now. I am fond of it. I shall come here every day,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nannounced Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is to be a secret. My orders are that no one\r\nis to know that we come here. Dickon and my cousin have worked and made\r\nit come alive. I shall send for you sometimes to help\xe2\x80\x94but you must come\r\nwhen no one can see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s face twisted itself in a dry old smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come here before when no one saw me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 last time I was here,\xe2\x80\x9d rubbing his chin and looking round, \xe2\x80\x9cwas\r\nabout two year\xe2\x80\x99 ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut no one has been in it for ten years!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no door!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m no one,\xe2\x80\x9d said old Ben dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 I didn\xe2\x80\x99t come through th\xe2\x80\x99 door.\r\nI come over th\xe2\x80\x99 wall. Th\xe2\x80\x99 rheumatics held me back th\xe2\x80\x99 last two year\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 come an\xe2\x80\x99 did a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 prunin\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nout how it had been done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was so fond of it\xe2\x80\x94she was!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nwas such a pretty young thing. She says to me once, \xe2\x80\x98Ben,\xe2\x80\x99 says she\r\nlaughin\xe2\x80\x99, \xe2\x80\x98if ever I\xe2\x80\x99m ill or if I go away you must take care of my\r\nroses.\xe2\x80\x99 When she did go away th\xe2\x80\x99 orders was no one was ever to come\r\nnigh. But I come,\xe2\x80\x9d with grumpy obstinacy. \xe2\x80\x9cOver th\xe2\x80\x99 wall I come\xe2\x80\x94until\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 rheumatics stopped me\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 I did a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 work once a year. She\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ngave her order first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been as wick as it is if tha\xe2\x80\x99 hadn\xe2\x80\x99t done it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDickon. \xe2\x80\x9cI did wonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you did it, Weatherstaff,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll know how to\r\nkeep the secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, I\xe2\x80\x99ll know, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ben. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99ll be easier for a man wi\xe2\x80\x99\r\nrheumatics to come in at th\xe2\x80\x99 door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the grass near the tree Mary had dropped her trowel. Colin stretched\r\nout his hand and took it up. An odd expression came into his face and\r\nhe began to scratch at the earth. His thin hand was weak enough but\r\npresently as they watched him\xe2\x80\x94Mary with quite breathless interest\xe2\x80\x94he\r\ndrove the end of the trowel into the soil and turned some over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can do it! You can do it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary to herself. \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you, you\r\ncan!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon\xe2\x80\x99s round eyes were full of eager curiousness but he said not a\r\nword. Ben Weatherstaff looked on with interested face.\r\n\r\nColin persevered. After he had turned a few trowelfuls of soil he spoke\r\nexultantly to Dickon in his best Yorkshire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 said as tha\xe2\x80\x99d have me walkin\xe2\x80\x99 about here same as other folk\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntha\xe2\x80\x99 said tha\xe2\x80\x99d have me diggin\xe2\x80\x99. I thowt tha\xe2\x80\x99 was just leein\xe2\x80\x99 to please\r\nme. This is only th\xe2\x80\x99 first day an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ve walked\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 here I am diggin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s mouth fell open again when he heard him, but he\r\nended by chuckling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat sounds as if tha\xe2\x80\x99d got wits enow. Tha\xe2\x80\x99rt a\r\nYorkshire lad for sure. An\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99rt diggin\xe2\x80\x99, too. How\xe2\x80\x99d tha\xe2\x80\x99 like to\r\nplant a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 somethin\xe2\x80\x99? I can get thee a rose in a pot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and get it!\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, digging excitedly. \xe2\x80\x9cQuick! Quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was done quickly enough indeed. Ben Weatherstaff went his way\r\nforgetting rheumatics. Dickon took his spade and dug the hole deeper\r\nand wider than a new digger with thin white hands could make it. Mary\r\nslipped out to run and bring back a watering-can. When Dickon had\r\ndeepened the hole Colin went on turning the soft earth over and over.\r\nHe looked up at the sky, flushed and glowing with the strangely new\r\nexercise, slight as it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to do it before the sun goes quite\xe2\x80\x94quite down,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMary thought that perhaps the sun held back a few minutes just on\r\npurpose. Ben Weatherstaff brought the rose in its pot from the\r\ngreenhouse. He hobbled over the grass as fast as he could. He had begun\r\nto be excited, too. He knelt down by the hole and broke the pot from\r\nthe mould.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, lad,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, handing the plant to Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cSet it in the earth\r\nthysel\xe2\x80\x99 same as th\xe2\x80\x99 king does when he goes to a new place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe thin white hands shook a little and Colin\xe2\x80\x99s flush grew deeper as he\r\nset the rose in the mould and held it while old Ben made firm the\r\nearth. It was filled in and pressed down and made steady. Mary was\r\nleaning forward on her hands and knees. Soot had flown down and marched\r\nforward to see what was being done. Nut and Shell chattered about it\r\nfrom a cherry-tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s planted!\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin at last. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the sun is only slipping over\r\nthe edge. Help me up, Dickon. I want to be standing when it goes.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s part of the Magic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Dickon helped him, and the Magic\xe2\x80\x94or whatever it was\xe2\x80\x94so gave him\r\nstrength that when the sun did slip over the edge and end the strange\r\nlovely afternoon for them there he actually stood on his two\r\nfeet\xe2\x80\x94laughing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\nMAGIC\r\n\r\n\r\nDr. Craven had been waiting some time at the house when they returned\r\nto it. He had indeed begun to wonder if it might not be wise to send\r\nsomeone out to explore the garden paths. When Colin was brought back to\r\nhis room the poor man looked him over seriously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not have stayed so long,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must not overexert\r\nyourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not tired at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt has made me well. Tomorrow I\r\nam going out in the morning as well as in the afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that I can allow it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dr. Craven. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid\r\nit would not be wise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would not be wise to try to stop me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin quite seriously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven Mary had found out that one of Colin\xe2\x80\x99s chief peculiarities was\r\nthat he did not know in the least what a rude little brute he was with\r\nhis way of ordering people about. He had lived on a sort of desert\r\nisland all his life and as he had been the king of it he had made his\r\nown manners and had had no one to compare himself with. Mary had indeed\r\nbeen rather like him herself and since she had been at Misselthwaite\r\nhad gradually discovered that her own manners had not been of the kind\r\nwhich is usual or popular. Having made this discovery she naturally\r\nthought it of enough interest to communicate to Colin. So she sat and\r\nlooked at him curiously for a few minutes after Dr. Craven had gone.\r\nShe wanted to make him ask her why she was doing it and of course she\r\ndid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you looking at me for?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m thinking that I am rather sorry for Dr. Craven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin calmly, but not without an air of some\r\nsatisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t get Misselthwaite at all now I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to\r\ndie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry for him because of that, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I was\r\nthinking just then that it must have been very horrid to have had to be\r\npolite for ten years to a boy who was always rude. I would never have\r\ndone it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I rude?\xe2\x80\x9d Colin inquired undisturbedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you had been his own boy and he had been a slapping sort of man,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Mary, \xe2\x80\x9che would have slapped you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he daren\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he daren\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mistress Mary, thinking the thing out quite\r\nwithout prejudice. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody ever dared to do anything you didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike\xe2\x80\x94because you were going to die and things like that. You were such\r\na poor thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Colin stubbornly, \xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to be a poor thing.\r\nI won\xe2\x80\x99t let people think I\xe2\x80\x99m one. I stood on my feet this afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is always having your own way that has made you so queer,\xe2\x80\x9d Mary\r\nwent on, thinking aloud.\r\n\r\nColin turned his head, frowning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I queer?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cvery. But you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be cross,\xe2\x80\x9d she added\r\nimpartially, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause so am I queer\xe2\x80\x94and so is Ben Weatherstaff. But I\r\nam not as queer as I was before I began to like people and before I\r\nfound the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be queer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to be,\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\nfrowned again with determination.\r\n\r\nHe was a very proud boy. He lay thinking for a while and then Mary saw\r\nhis beautiful smile begin and gradually change his whole face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall stop being queer,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cif I go every day to the garden.\r\nThere is Magic in there\xe2\x80\x94good Magic, you know, Mary. I am sure there\r\nis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t real Magic,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe can pretend it is.\r\n_Something_ is there\xe2\x80\x94_something!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Magic,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cbut not black. It\xe2\x80\x99s as white as snow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey always called it Magic and indeed it seemed like it in the months\r\nthat followed\xe2\x80\x94the wonderful months\xe2\x80\x94the radiant months\xe2\x80\x94the amazing ones.\r\nOh! the things which happened in that garden! If you have never had a\r\ngarden you cannot understand, and if you have had a garden you will\r\nknow that it would take a whole book to describe all that came to pass\r\nthere. At first it seemed that green things would never cease pushing\r\ntheir way through the earth, in the grass, in the beds, even in the\r\ncrevices of the walls. Then the green things began to show buds and the\r\nbuds began to unfurl and show color, every shade of blue, every shade\r\nof purple, every tint and hue of crimson. In its happy days flowers had\r\nbeen tucked away into every inch and hole and corner. Ben Weatherstaff\r\nhad seen it done and had himself scraped out mortar from between the\r\nbricks of the wall and made pockets of earth for lovely clinging things\r\nto grow on. Iris and white lilies rose out of the grass in sheaves, and\r\nthe green alcoves filled themselves with amazing armies of the blue and\r\nwhite flower lances of tall delphiniums or columbines or campanulas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was main fond o\xe2\x80\x99 them\xe2\x80\x94she was,\xe2\x80\x9d Ben Weatherstaff said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe liked\r\nthem things as was allus pointin\xe2\x80\x99 up to th\xe2\x80\x99 blue sky, she used to tell.\r\nNot as she was one o\xe2\x80\x99 them as looked down on th\xe2\x80\x99 earth\xe2\x80\x94not her. She\r\njust loved it but she said as th\xe2\x80\x99 blue sky allus looked so joyful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe seeds Dickon and Mary had planted grew as if fairies had tended\r\nthem. Satiny poppies of all tints danced in the breeze by the score,\r\ngaily defying flowers which had lived in the garden for years and which\r\nit might be confessed seemed rather to wonder how such new people had\r\ngot there. And the roses\xe2\x80\x94the roses! Rising out of the grass, tangled\r\nround the sun-dial, wreathing the tree trunks and hanging from their\r\nbranches, climbing up the walls and spreading over them with long\r\ngarlands falling in cascades\xe2\x80\x94they came alive day by day, hour by hour.\r\nFair fresh leaves, and buds\xe2\x80\x94and buds\xe2\x80\x94tiny at first but swelling and\r\nworking Magic until they burst and uncurled into cups of scent\r\ndelicately spilling themselves over their brims and filling the garden\r\nair.\r\n\r\nColin saw it all, watching each change as it took place. Every morning\r\nhe was brought out and every hour of each day when it didn\xe2\x80\x99t rain he\r\nspent in the garden. Even gray days pleased him. He would lie on the\r\ngrass \xe2\x80\x9cwatching things growing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. If you watched long enough,\r\nhe declared, you could see buds unsheath themselves. Also you could\r\nmake the acquaintance of strange busy insect things running about on\r\nvarious unknown but evidently serious errands, sometimes carrying tiny\r\nscraps of straw or feather or food, or climbing blades of grass as if\r\nthey were trees from whose tops one could look out to explore the\r\ncountry. A mole throwing up its mound at the end of its burrow and\r\nmaking its way out at last with the long-nailed paws which looked so\r\nlike elfish hands, had absorbed him one whole morning. Ants\xe2\x80\x99 ways,\r\nbeetles\xe2\x80\x99 ways, bees\xe2\x80\x99 ways, frogs\xe2\x80\x99 ways, birds\xe2\x80\x99 ways, plants\xe2\x80\x99 ways, gave\r\nhim a new world to explore and when Dickon revealed them all and added\r\nfoxes\xe2\x80\x99 ways, otters\xe2\x80\x99 ways, ferrets\xe2\x80\x99 ways, squirrels\xe2\x80\x99 ways, and trout\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand water-rats\xe2\x80\x99 and badgers\xe2\x80\x99 ways, there was no end to the things to\r\ntalk about and think over.\r\n\r\nAnd this was not the half of the Magic. The fact that he had really\r\nonce stood on his feet had set Colin thinking tremendously and when\r\nMary told him of the spell she had worked he was excited and approved\r\nof it greatly. He talked of it constantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course there must be lots of Magic in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d he said wisely\r\none day, \xe2\x80\x9cbut people don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it is like or how to make it.\r\nPerhaps the beginning is just to say nice things are going to happen\r\nuntil you make them happen. I am going to try and experiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next morning when they went to the secret garden he sent at once\r\nfor Ben Weatherstaff. Ben came as quickly as he could and found the\r\nRajah standing on his feet under a tree and looking very grand but also\r\nvery beautifully smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Ben Weatherstaff,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want you and Dickon and\r\nMiss Mary to stand in a row and listen to me because I am going to tell\r\nyou something very important.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ben Weatherstaff, touching his forehead. (One\r\nof the long concealed charms of Ben Weatherstaff was that in his\r\nboyhood he had once run away to sea and had made voyages. So he could\r\nreply like a sailor.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to try a scientific experiment,\xe2\x80\x9d explained the Rajah. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen\r\nI grow up I am going to make great scientific discoveries and I am\r\ngoing to begin now with this experiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff promptly, though this was the\r\nfirst time he had heard of great scientific discoveries.\r\n\r\nIt was the first time Mary had heard of them, either, but even at this\r\nstage she had begun to realize that, queer as he was, Colin had read\r\nabout a great many singular things and was somehow a very convincing\r\nsort of boy. When he held up his head and fixed his strange eyes on you\r\nit seemed as if you believed him almost in spite of yourself though he\r\nwas only ten years old\xe2\x80\x94going on eleven. At this moment he was\r\nespecially convincing because he suddenly felt the fascination of\r\nactually making a sort of speech like a grown-up person.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe great scientific discoveries I am going to make,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwill be about Magic. Magic is a great thing and scarcely anyone knows\r\nanything about it except a few people in old books\xe2\x80\x94and Mary a little,\r\nbecause she was born in India where there are fakirs. I believe Dickon\r\nknows some Magic, but perhaps he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know he knows it. He charms\r\nanimals and people. I would never have let him come to see me if he had\r\nnot been an animal charmer\xe2\x80\x94which is a boy charmer, too, because a boy\r\nis an animal. I am sure there is Magic in everything, only we have not\r\nsense enough to get hold of it and make it do things for us\xe2\x80\x94like\r\nelectricity and horses and steam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis sounded so imposing that Ben Weatherstaff became quite excited and\r\nreally could not keep still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said and he began to stand up quite straight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen Mary found this garden it looked quite dead,\xe2\x80\x9d the orator\r\nproceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cThen something began pushing things up out of the soil and\r\nmaking things out of nothing. One day things weren\xe2\x80\x99t there and another\r\nthey were. I had never watched things before and it made me feel very\r\ncurious. Scientific people are always curious and I am going to be\r\nscientific. I keep saying to myself, \xe2\x80\x98What is it? What is it?\xe2\x80\x99 It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsomething. It can\xe2\x80\x99t be nothing! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know its name so I call it\r\nMagic. I have never seen the sun rise but Mary and Dickon have and from\r\nwhat they tell me I am sure that is Magic too. Something pushes it up\r\nand draws it. Sometimes since I\xe2\x80\x99ve been in the garden I\xe2\x80\x99ve looked up\r\nthrough the trees at the sky and I have had a strange feeling of being\r\nhappy as if something were pushing and drawing in my chest and making\r\nme breathe fast. Magic is always pushing and drawing and making things\r\nout of nothing. Everything is made out of Magic, leaves and trees,\r\nflowers and birds, badgers and foxes and squirrels and people. So it\r\nmust be all around us. In this garden\xe2\x80\x94in all the places. The Magic in\r\nthis garden has made me stand up and know I am going to live to be a\r\nman. I am going to make the scientific experiment of trying to get some\r\nand put it in myself and make it push and draw me and make me strong. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know how to do it but I think that if you keep thinking about it\r\nand calling it perhaps it will come. Perhaps that is the first baby way\r\nto get it. When I was going to try to stand that first time Mary kept\r\nsaying to herself as fast as she could, \xe2\x80\x98You can do it! You can do it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand I did. I had to try myself at the same time, of course, but her\r\nMagic helped me\xe2\x80\x94and so did Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s. Every morning and evening and as\r\noften in the daytime as I can remember I am going to say, \xe2\x80\x98Magic is in\r\nme! Magic is making me well! I am going to be as strong as Dickon, as\r\nstrong as Dickon!\xe2\x80\x99 And you must all do it, too. That is my experiment\r\nWill you help, Ben Weatherstaff?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff. \xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you keep doing it every day as regularly as soldiers go through\r\ndrill we shall see what will happen and find out if the experiment\r\nsucceeds. You learn things by saying them over and over and thinking\r\nabout them until they stay in your mind forever and I think it will be\r\nthe same with Magic. If you keep calling it to come to you and help you\r\nit will get to be part of you and it will stay and do things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI once heard an officer in India tell my mother that there were fakirs\r\nwho said words over and over thousands of times,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard Jem Fettleworth\xe2\x80\x99s wife say th\xe2\x80\x99 same thing over thousands o\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94callin\xe2\x80\x99 Jem a drunken brute,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff dryly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSummat allus come o\xe2\x80\x99 that, sure enough. He gave her a good hidin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwent to th\xe2\x80\x99 Blue Lion an\xe2\x80\x99 got as drunk as a lord.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin drew his brows together and thought a few minutes. Then he\r\ncheered up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou see something did come of it. She used the wrong\r\nMagic until she made him beat her. If she\xe2\x80\x99d used the right Magic and\r\nhad said something nice perhaps he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have got as drunk as a lord\r\nand perhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps he might have bought her a new bonnet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff chuckled and there was shrewd admiration in his little\r\nold eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99rt a clever lad as well as a straight-legged one, Mester Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cNext time I see Bess Fettleworth I\xe2\x80\x99ll give her a bit of a\r\nhint o\xe2\x80\x99 what Magic will do for her. She\xe2\x80\x99d be rare an\xe2\x80\x99 pleased if th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsinetifik \xe2\x80\x99speriment worked\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 so \xe2\x80\x99ud Jem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon had stood listening to the lecture, his round eyes shining with\r\ncurious delight. Nut and Shell were on his shoulders and he held a\r\nlong-eared white rabbit in his arm and stroked and stroked it softly\r\nwhile it laid its ears along its back and enjoyed itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think the experiment will work?\xe2\x80\x9d Colin asked him, wondering\r\nwhat he was thinking. He so often wondered what Dickon was thinking\r\nwhen he saw him looking at him or at one of his \xe2\x80\x9ccreatures\xe2\x80\x9d with his\r\nhappy wide smile.\r\n\r\nHe smiled now and his smile was wider than usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I do. It\xe2\x80\x99ll work same as th\xe2\x80\x99 seeds do when\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 sun shines on \xe2\x80\x99em. It\xe2\x80\x99ll work for sure. Shall us begin it now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin was delighted and so was Mary. Fired by recollections of fakirs\r\nand devotees in illustrations Colin suggested that they should all sit\r\ncross-legged under the tree which made a canopy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be like sitting in a sort of temple,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m rather\r\ntired and I want to sit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon, \xe2\x80\x9ctha\xe2\x80\x99 mustn\xe2\x80\x99t begin by sayin\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99rt tired. Tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nmight spoil th\xe2\x80\x99 Magic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin turned and looked at him\xe2\x80\x94into his innocent round eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s true,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI must only think of the Magic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt all seemed most majestic and mysterious when they sat down in their\r\ncircle. Ben Weatherstaff felt as if he had somehow been led into\r\nappearing at a prayer-meeting. Ordinarily he was very fixed in being\r\nwhat he called \xe2\x80\x9cagen\xe2\x80\x99 prayer-meetin\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d but this being the Rajah\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naffair he did not resent it and was indeed inclined to be gratified at\r\nbeing called upon to assist. Mistress Mary felt solemnly enraptured.\r\nDickon held his rabbit in his arm, and perhaps he made some charmer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsignal no one heard, for when he sat down, cross-legged like the rest,\r\nthe crow, the fox, the squirrels and the lamb slowly drew near and made\r\npart of the circle, settling each into a place of rest as if of their\r\nown desire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe \xe2\x80\x98creatures\xe2\x80\x99 have come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cThey want to help\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin really looked quite beautiful, Mary thought. He held his head\r\nhigh as if he felt like a sort of priest and his strange eyes had a\r\nwonderful look in them. The light shone on him through the tree canopy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we will begin,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cShall we sway backward and forward,\r\nMary, as if we were dervishes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI canna\xe2\x80\x99 do no swayin\xe2\x80\x99 back\xe2\x80\x99ard and for\xe2\x80\x99ard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got th\xe2\x80\x99 rheumatics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Magic will take them away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin in a High Priest tone, \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nwe won\xe2\x80\x99t sway until it has done it. We will only chant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI canna\xe2\x80\x99 do no chantin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff a trifle testily. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\nturned me out o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 church choir th\xe2\x80\x99 only time I ever tried it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo one smiled. They were all too much in earnest. Colin\xe2\x80\x99s face was not\r\neven crossed by a shadow. He was thinking only of the Magic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will chant,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And he began, looking like a strange boy\r\nspirit. \xe2\x80\x9cThe sun is shining\xe2\x80\x94the sun is shining. That is the Magic. The\r\nflowers are growing\xe2\x80\x94the roots are stirring. That is the Magic. Being\r\nalive is the Magic\xe2\x80\x94being strong is the Magic. The Magic is in me\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nMagic is in me. It is in me\xe2\x80\x94it is in me. It\xe2\x80\x99s in everyone of us. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s back. Magic! Magic! Come and help!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said it a great many times\xe2\x80\x94not a thousand times but quite a goodly\r\nnumber. Mary listened entranced. She felt as if it were at once queer\r\nand beautiful and she wanted him to go on and on. Ben Weatherstaff\r\nbegan to feel soothed into a sort of dream which was quite agreeable.\r\nThe humming of the bees in the blossoms mingled with the chanting voice\r\nand drowsily melted into a doze. Dickon sat cross-legged with his\r\nrabbit asleep on his arm and a hand resting on the lamb\xe2\x80\x99s back. Soot\r\nhad pushed away a squirrel and huddled close to him on his shoulder,\r\nthe gray film dropped over his eyes. At last Colin stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I am going to walk round the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d he announced.\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s head had just dropped forward and he lifted it with\r\na jerk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been asleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 sort,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled Ben. \xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 sermon was good enow\xe2\x80\x94but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nbound to get out afore th\xe2\x80\x99 collection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was not quite awake yet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not in church,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben, straightening himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWho said I were? I heard\r\nevery bit of it. You said th\xe2\x80\x99 Magic was in my back. Th\xe2\x80\x99 doctor calls it\r\nrheumatics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Rajah waved his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the wrong Magic,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will get better. You have my\r\npermission to go to your work. But come back tomorrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like to see thee walk round the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Ben.\r\n\r\nIt was not an unfriendly grunt, but it was a grunt. In fact, being a\r\nstubborn old party and not having entire faith in Magic he had made up\r\nhis mind that if he were sent away he would climb his ladder and look\r\nover the wall so that he might be ready to hobble back if there were\r\nany stumbling.\r\n\r\nThe Rajah did not object to his staying and so the procession was\r\nformed. It really did look like a procession. Colin was at its head\r\nwith Dickon on one side and Mary on the other. Ben Weatherstaff walked\r\nbehind, and the \xe2\x80\x9ccreatures\xe2\x80\x9d trailed after them, the lamb and the fox\r\ncub keeping close to Dickon, the white rabbit hopping along or stopping\r\nto nibble and Soot following with the solemnity of a person who felt\r\nhimself in charge.\r\n\r\nIt was a procession which moved slowly but with dignity. Every few\r\nyards it stopped to rest. Colin leaned on Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s arm and privately\r\nBen Weatherstaff kept a sharp lookout, but now and then Colin took his\r\nhand from its support and walked a few steps alone. His head was held\r\nup all the time and he looked very grand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Magic is in me!\xe2\x80\x9d he kept saying. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Magic is making me strong! I\r\ncan feel it! I can feel it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed very certain that something was upholding and uplifting him.\r\nHe sat on the seats in the alcoves, and once or twice he sat down on\r\nthe grass and several times he paused in the path and leaned on Dickon,\r\nbut he would not give up until he had gone all round the garden. When\r\nhe returned to the canopy tree his cheeks were flushed and he looked\r\ntriumphant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did it! The Magic worked!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is my first scientific\r\ndiscovery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will Dr. Craven say?\xe2\x80\x9d broke out Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t say anything,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin answered, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause he will not be told.\r\nThis is to be the biggest secret of all. No one is to know anything\r\nabout it until I have grown so strong that I can walk and run like any\r\nother boy. I shall come here every day in my chair and I shall be taken\r\nback in it. I won\xe2\x80\x99t have people whispering and asking questions and I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t let my father hear about it until the experiment has quite\r\nsucceeded. Then sometime when he comes back to Misselthwaite I shall\r\njust walk into his study and say \xe2\x80\x98Here I am; I am like any other boy. I\r\nam quite well and I shall live to be a man. It has been done by a\r\nscientific experiment.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will think he is in a dream,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t believe his\r\neyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin flushed triumphantly. He had made himself believe that he was\r\ngoing to get well, which was really more than half the battle, if he\r\nhad been aware of it. And the thought which stimulated him more than\r\nany other was this imagining what his father would look like when he\r\nsaw that he had a son who was as straight and strong as other fathers\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsons. One of his darkest miseries in the unhealthy morbid past days had\r\nbeen his hatred of being a sickly weak-backed boy whose father was\r\nafraid to look at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be obliged to believe them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the things I am going to do, after the Magic works and before I\r\nbegin to make scientific discoveries, is to be an athlete.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have thee takin\xe2\x80\x99 to boxin\xe2\x80\x99 in a week or so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben\r\nWeatherstaff. \xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99lt end wi\xe2\x80\x99 winnin\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 Belt an\xe2\x80\x99 bein\xe2\x80\x99 champion\r\nprize-fighter of all England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin fixed his eyes on him sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeatherstaff,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is disrespectful. You must not take\r\nliberties because you are in the secret. However much the Magic works I\r\nshall not be a prize-fighter. I shall be a Scientific Discoverer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAx pardon\xe2\x80\x94ax pardon, sir\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ben, touching his forehead in\r\nsalute. \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have seed it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a jokin\xe2\x80\x99 matter,\xe2\x80\x9d but his eyes\r\ntwinkled and secretly he was immensely pleased. He really did not mind\r\nbeing snubbed since the snubbing meant that the lad was gaining\r\nstrength and spirit.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLET THEM LAUGH\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe secret garden was not the only one Dickon worked in. Round the\r\ncottage on the moor there was a piece of ground enclosed by a low wall\r\nof rough stones. Early in the morning and late in the fading twilight\r\nand on all the days Colin and Mary did not see him, Dickon worked there\r\nplanting or tending potatoes and cabbages, turnips and carrots and\r\nherbs for his mother. In the company of his \xe2\x80\x9ccreatures\xe2\x80\x9d he did wonders\r\nthere and was never tired of doing them, it seemed. While he dug or\r\nweeded he whistled or sang bits of Yorkshire moor songs or talked to\r\nSoot or Captain or the brothers and sisters he had taught to help him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99d never get on as comfortable as we do,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Sowerby said, \xe2\x80\x9cif it\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t for Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s garden. Anything\xe2\x80\x99ll grow for him. His \xe2\x80\x99taters and\r\ncabbages is twice th\xe2\x80\x99 size of anyone else\xe2\x80\x99s an\xe2\x80\x99 they\xe2\x80\x99ve got a flavor\r\nwith \xe2\x80\x99em as nobody\xe2\x80\x99s has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she found a moment to spare she liked to go out and talk to him.\r\nAfter supper there was still a long clear twilight to work in and that\r\nwas her quiet time. She could sit upon the low rough wall and look on\r\nand hear stories of the day. She loved this time. There were not only\r\nvegetables in this garden. Dickon had bought penny packages of flower\r\nseeds now and then and sown bright sweet-scented things among\r\ngooseberry bushes and even cabbages and he grew borders of mignonette\r\nand pinks and pansies and things whose seeds he could save year after\r\nyear or whose roots would bloom each spring and spread in time into\r\nfine clumps. The low wall was one of the prettiest things in Yorkshire\r\nbecause he had tucked moorland foxglove and ferns and rock-cress and\r\nhedgerow flowers into every crevice until only here and there glimpses\r\nof the stones were to be seen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll a chap\xe2\x80\x99s got to do to make \xe2\x80\x99em thrive, mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he would say, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\nto be friends with \xe2\x80\x99em for sure. They\xe2\x80\x99re just like th\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98creatures.\xe2\x80\x99 If\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re thirsty give \xe2\x80\x99em drink and if they\xe2\x80\x99re hungry give \xe2\x80\x99em a bit o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfood. They want to live same as we do. If they died I should feel as if\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d been a bad lad and somehow treated them heartless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was in these twilight hours that Mrs. Sowerby heard of all that\r\nhappened at Misselthwaite Manor. At first she was only told that\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMester Colin\xe2\x80\x9d had taken a fancy to going out into the grounds with\r\nMiss Mary and that it was doing him good. But it was not long before it\r\nwas agreed between the two children that Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s mother might \xe2\x80\x9ccome\r\ninto the secret.\xe2\x80\x9d Somehow it was not doubted that she was \xe2\x80\x9csafe for\r\nsure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo one beautiful still evening Dickon told the whole story, with all\r\nthe thrilling details of the buried key and the robin and the gray haze\r\nwhich had seemed like deadness and the secret Mistress Mary had planned\r\nnever to reveal. The coming of Dickon and how it had been told to him,\r\nthe doubt of Mester Colin and the final drama of his introduction to\r\nthe hidden domain, combined with the incident of Ben Weatherstaff\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nangry face peering over the wall and Mester Colin\xe2\x80\x99s sudden indignant\r\nstrength, made Mrs. Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s nice-looking face quite change color\r\nseveral times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a good thing that little lass came to th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nManor. It\xe2\x80\x99s been th\xe2\x80\x99 makin\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 her an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 savin, o\xe2\x80\x99 him. Standin\xe2\x80\x99 on\r\nhis feet! An\xe2\x80\x99 us all thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 he was a poor half-witted lad with not a\r\nstraight bone in him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe asked a great many questions and her blue eyes were full of deep\r\nthinking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do they make of it at th\xe2\x80\x99 Manor\xe2\x80\x94him being so well an\xe2\x80\x99 cheerful\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 never complainin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to make of it,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Dickon. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery day as\r\ncomes round his face looks different. It\xe2\x80\x99s fillin\xe2\x80\x99 out and doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look\r\nso sharp an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 waxy color is goin\xe2\x80\x99. If the doctor\r\nknew he\xe2\x80\x99d found out he could stand on his feet he\xe2\x80\x99d likely write and\r\ntell Mester Craven. Mester Colin\xe2\x80\x99s savin\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 secret to tell himself.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to practise his Magic on his legs every day till his father\r\ncomes back an\xe2\x80\x99 then he\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to march into his room an\xe2\x80\x99 show him he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nas straight as other lads. His eyes\r\nwere twinkling with fun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMester Colin is carried down to his chair every time he goes out,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexplained. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 he flies out at John, th\xe2\x80\x99 footman, for not carryin\xe2\x80\x99 him\r\ncareful enough.
