Colin and Mary found it one of their most thrilling sources\r\nof entertainment. The idea of protecting themselves from suspicion had\r\nbeen unconsciously suggested to them first by the puzzled nurse and\r\nthen by Dr. Craven himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour appetite. Is improving very much, Master Colin,\xe2\x80\x9d the nurse had\r\nsaid one day. \xe2\x80\x9cYou used to eat nothing, and so many things disagreed\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing disagrees with me now\xe2\x80\x9d replied Colin, and then seeing the\r\nnurse looking at him curiously he suddenly remembered that perhaps he\r\nought not to appear too well just yet. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least things don\xe2\x80\x99t so often\r\ndisagree with me. It\xe2\x80\x99s the fresh air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse, still looking at him with a mystified\r\nexpression. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I must talk to Dr. Craven about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow she stared at you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary when she went away. \xe2\x80\x9cAs if she\r\nthought there must be something to find out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t have her finding out things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cNo one must begin\r\nto find out yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Dr. Craven came that morning he seemed puzzled, also. He asked a\r\nnumber of questions, to Colin\xe2\x80\x99s great annoyance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou stay out in the garden a great deal,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you\r\ngo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin put on his favorite air of dignified indifference to opinion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not let anyone know where I go,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI go to a place\r\nI like. Everyone has orders to keep out of the way. I won\xe2\x80\x99t be watched\r\nand stared at. You know that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to be out all day but I do not think it has done you harm\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndo not think so. The nurse says that you eat much more than you have\r\never done before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, prompted by a sudden inspiration, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps it is\r\nan unnatural appetite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think so, as your food seems to agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr.\r\nCraven. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are gaining flesh rapidly and your color is better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps\xe2\x80\x94perhaps I am bloated and feverish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, assuming a\r\ndiscouraging air of gloom. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople who are not going to live are\r\noften\xe2\x80\x94different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven shook his head. He was holding Colin\xe2\x80\x99s wrist and he pushed\r\nup his sleeve and felt his arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not feverish,\xe2\x80\x9d he said thoughtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cand such flesh as you\r\nhave gained is healthy. If you can keep this up, my boy, we need not\r\ntalk of dying. Your father will be happy to hear of this remarkable\r\nimprovement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t have him told!\xe2\x80\x9d Colin broke forth fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will only\r\ndisappoint him if I get worse again\xe2\x80\x94and I may get worse this very\r\nnight. I might have a raging fever. I feel as if I might be beginning\r\nto have one now. I won\xe2\x80\x99t have letters written to my father\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t! You are making me angry and you know that is bad for me. I feel\r\nhot already. I hate being written about and being talked over as much\r\nas I hate being stared at!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush-h! my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Dr. Craven soothed him. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing shall be written\r\nwithout your permission. You are too sensitive about things. You must\r\nnot undo the good which has been done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said no more about writing to Mr. Craven and when he saw the nurse\r\nhe privately warned her that such a possibility must not be mentioned\r\nto the patient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe boy is extraordinarily better,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHis advance seems almost\r\nabnormal. But of course he is doing now of his own free will what we\r\ncould not make him do before. Still, he excites himself very easily and\r\nnothing must be said to irritate him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary and Colin were much alarmed and talked together anxiously. From\r\nthis time dated their plan of \xe2\x80\x9cplay actin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may be obliged to have a tantrum,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin regretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to have one and I\xe2\x80\x99m not miserable enough now to work myself into a\r\nbig one. Perhaps I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have one at all. That lump doesn\xe2\x80\x99t come in\r\nmy throat now and I keep thinking of nice things instead of horrible\r\nones. But if they talk about writing to my father I shall have to do\r\nsomething.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made up his mind to eat less, but unfortunately it was not possible\r\nto carry out this brilliant idea when he wakened each morning with an\r\namazing appetite and the table near his sofa was set with a breakfast\r\nof home-made bread and fresh butter, snow-white eggs, raspberry jam and\r\nclotted cream. Mary always breakfasted with him and when they found\r\nthemselves at the table\xe2\x80\x94particularly if there were delicate slices of\r\nsizzling ham sending forth tempting odors from under a hot silver\r\ncover\xe2\x80\x94they would look into each other\xe2\x80\x99s eyes in desperation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think we shall have to eat it all this morning, Mary,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin always\r\nended by saying. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can send away some of the lunch and a great deal\r\nof the dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they never found they could send away anything and the highly\r\npolished condition of the empty plates returned to the pantry awakened\r\nmuch comment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do wish,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin would say also, \xe2\x80\x9cI do wish the slices of ham were\r\nthicker, and one muffin each is not enough for anyone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s enough for a person who is going to die,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mary when\r\nfirst she heard this, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\xe2\x80\x99s not enough for a person who is going to\r\nlive. I sometimes feel as if I could eat three when those nice fresh\r\nheather and gorse smells from the moor come pouring in at the open\r\nwindow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe morning that Dickon\xe2\x80\x94after they had been enjoying themselves in the\r\ngarden for about two hours\xe2\x80\x94went behind a big rosebush and brought forth\r\ntwo tin pails and revealed that one was full of rich new milk with\r\ncream on the top of it, and that the other held cottage-made currant\r\nbuns folded in a clean blue and white napkin, buns so carefully tucked\r\nin that they were still hot, there was a riot of surprised joyfulness.\r\nWhat a wonderful thing for Mrs. Sowerby to think of! What a kind,\r\nclever woman she must be! How good the buns were! And what delicious\r\nfresh milk!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMagic is in her just as it is in Dickon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes her\r\nthink of ways to do things\xe2\x80\x94nice things. She is a Magic person. Tell her\r\nwe are grateful, Dickon\xe2\x80\x94extremely grateful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was given to using rather grown-up phrases at times. He enjoyed\r\nthem. He liked this so much that he improved upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell her she has been most bounteous and our gratitude is extreme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then forgetting his grandeur he fell to and stuffed himself with\r\nbuns and drank milk out of the pail in copious draughts in the manner\r\nof any hungry little boy who had been taking unusual exercise and\r\nbreathing in moorland air and whose breakfast was more than two hours\r\nbehind him.\r\n\r\nThis was the beginning of many agreeable incidents of the same kind.\r\nThey actually awoke to the fact that as Mrs. Sowerby had fourteen\r\npeople to provide food for she might not have enough to satisfy two\r\nextra appetites every day. So they asked her to let them send some of\r\ntheir shillings to buy things.\r\n\r\nDickon made the stimulating discovery that in the wood in the park\r\noutside the garden where Mary had first found him piping to the wild\r\ncreatures there was a deep little hollow where you could build a sort\r\nof tiny oven with stones and roast potatoes and eggs in it. Roasted\r\neggs were a previously unknown luxury and very hot potatoes with salt\r\nand fresh butter in them were fit for a woodland king\xe2\x80\x94besides being\r\ndeliciously satisfying. You could buy both potatoes and eggs and eat as\r\nmany as you liked without feeling as if you were taking food out of the\r\nmouths of fourteen people.\r\n\r\nEvery beautiful morning the Magic was worked by the mystic circle under\r\nthe plum-tree which provided a canopy of thickening green leaves after\r\nits brief blossom-time was ended. After the ceremony Colin always took\r\nhis walking exercise and throughout the day he exercised his newly\r\nfound power at intervals. Each day he grew stronger and could walk more\r\nsteadily and cover more ground. And each day his belief in the Magic\r\ngrew stronger\xe2\x80\x94as well it might. He tried one experiment after another\r\nas he felt himself gaining strength and it was Dickon who showed him\r\nthe best things of all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d he said one morning after an absence, \xe2\x80\x9cI went to Thwaite\r\nfor mother an\xe2\x80\x99 near th\xe2\x80\x99 Blue Cow Inn I seed Bob Haworth. He\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nstrongest chap on th\xe2\x80\x99 moor. He\xe2\x80\x99s the champion wrestler an\xe2\x80\x99 he can jump\r\nhigher than any other chap an\xe2\x80\x99 throw th\xe2\x80\x99 hammer farther. He\xe2\x80\x99s gone all\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 way to Scotland for th\xe2\x80\x99 sports some years. He\xe2\x80\x99s knowed me ever\r\nsince I was a little \xe2\x80\x99un an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99s a friendly sort an\xe2\x80\x99 I axed him some\r\nquestions. Th\xe2\x80\x99 gentry calls him a athlete and I thought o\xe2\x80\x99 thee, Mester\r\nColin, and I says, \xe2\x80\x98How did tha\xe2\x80\x99 make tha\xe2\x80\x99 muscles stick out that way,\r\nBob? Did tha\xe2\x80\x99 do anythin\xe2\x80\x99 extra to make thysel\xe2\x80\x99 so strong?\xe2\x80\x99 An\xe2\x80\x99 he says\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, yes, lad, I did. A strong man in a show that came to Thwaite\r\nonce showed me how to exercise my arms an\xe2\x80\x99 legs an\xe2\x80\x99 every muscle in my\r\nbody. An\xe2\x80\x99 I says, \xe2\x80\x98Could a delicate chap make himself stronger with\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em, Bob?\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 he laughed an\xe2\x80\x99 says, \xe2\x80\x98Art tha\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 delicate chap?\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x98No, but I knows a young gentleman that\xe2\x80\x99s gettin\xe2\x80\x99 well of a long\r\nillness an\xe2\x80\x99 I wish I knowed some o\xe2\x80\x99 them tricks to tell him about.\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t say no names an\xe2\x80\x99 he didn\xe2\x80\x99t ask none. He\xe2\x80\x99s friendly same as I\r\nsaid an\xe2\x80\x99 he stood up an\xe2\x80\x99 showed me good-natured like, an\xe2\x80\x99 I imitated\r\nwhat he did till I knowed it by heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin had been listening excitedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you show me?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, to be sure,\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon answered, getting up. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he says tha\xe2\x80\x99 mun\r\ndo \xe2\x80\x99em gentle at first an\xe2\x80\x99 be careful not to tire thysel\xe2\x80\x99. Rest in\r\nbetween times an\xe2\x80\x99 take deep breaths an\xe2\x80\x99 don\xe2\x80\x99t overdo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be careful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin. \xe2\x80\x9cShow me! Show me! Dickon, you are the\r\nmost Magic boy in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon stood up on the grass and slowly went through a carefully\r\npractical but simple series of muscle exercises. Colin watched them\r\nwith widening eyes. He could do a few while he was sitting down.\r\nPresently he did a few gently while he stood upon his already steadied\r\nfeet. Mary began to do them also. Soot, who was watching the\r\nperformance, became much disturbed and left his branch and hopped about\r\nrestlessly because he could not do them too.\r\n\r\nFrom that time the exercises were part of the day\xe2\x80\x99s duties as much as\r\nthe Magic was. It became possible for both Colin and Mary to do more of\r\nthem each time they tried, and such appetites were the results that but\r\nfor the basket Dickon put down behind the bush each morning when he\r\narrived they would have been lost. But the little oven in the hollow\r\nand Mrs. Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s bounties were so satisfying that Mrs. Medlock and\r\nthe nurse and Dr. Craven became mystified again. You can trifle with\r\nyour breakfast and seem to disdain your dinner if you are full to the\r\nbrim with roasted eggs and potatoes and richly frothed new milk and\r\noatcakes and buns and heather honey and clotted cream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are eating next to nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the nurse. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll die of\r\nstarvation if they can\xe2\x80\x99t be persuaded to take some nourishment. And yet\r\nsee how they look.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mrs. Medlock indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! I\xe2\x80\x99m moithered to death\r\nwith them. They\xe2\x80\x99re a pair of young Satans. Bursting their jackets one\r\nday and the next turning up their noses at the best meals Cook can\r\ntempt them with. Not a mouthful of that lovely young fowl and bread\r\nsauce did they set a fork into yesterday\xe2\x80\x94and the poor woman fair\r\n_invented_ a pudding for them\xe2\x80\x94and back it\xe2\x80\x99s sent. She almost cried.\r\nShe\xe2\x80\x99s afraid she\xe2\x80\x99ll be blamed if they starve themselves into their\r\ngraves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Craven came and looked at Colin long and carefully, He wore an\r\nextremely worried expression when the nurse talked with him and showed\r\nhim the almost untouched tray of breakfast she had saved for him to\r\nlook at\xe2\x80\x94but it was even more worried when he sat down by Colin\xe2\x80\x99s sofa\r\nand examined him. He had been called to London on business and had not\r\nseen the boy for nearly two weeks. When young things begin to gain\r\nhealth they gain it rapidly. The waxen tinge had left, Colins skin and\r\na warm rose showed through it; his beautiful eyes were clear and the\r\nhollows under them and in his cheeks and temples had filled out. His\r\nonce dark, heavy locks had begun to look as if they sprang healthily\r\nfrom his forehead and were soft and warm with life. His lips were\r\nfuller and of a normal color. In fact as an imitation of a boy who was\r\na confirmed invalid he was a disgraceful sight. Dr. Craven held his\r\nchin in his hand and thought him over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear that you do not eat anything,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat will\r\nnot do. You will lose all you have gained\xe2\x80\x94and you have gained\r\namazingly. You ate so well a short time ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you it was an unnatural appetite,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Colin.\r\n\r\nMary was sitting on her stool nearby and she suddenly made a very queer\r\nsound which she tried so violently to repress that she ended by almost\r\nchoking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven, turning to look at her.\r\n\r\nMary became quite severe in her manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was something between a sneeze and a cough,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied with\r\nreproachful dignity, \xe2\x80\x9cand it got into my throat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d she said afterward to Colin, \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stop myself. It just\r\nburst out because all at once I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help remembering that last big\r\npotato you ate and the way your mouth stretched when you bit through\r\nthat thick lovely crust with jam and clotted cream on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there any way in which those children can get food secretly?\xe2\x80\x9d Dr.\r\nCraven inquired of Mrs. Medlock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no way unless they dig it out of the earth or pick it off the\r\ntrees,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Medlock answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThey stay out in the grounds all day\r\nand see no one but each other. And if they want anything different to\r\neat from what\xe2\x80\x99s sent up to them they need only ask for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven, \xe2\x80\x9cso long as going without food agrees with\r\nthem we need not disturb ourselves. The boy is a new creature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo is the girl,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Medlock. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s begun to be downright\r\npretty since she\xe2\x80\x99s filled out and lost her ugly little sour look. Her\r\nhair\xe2\x80\x99s grown thick and healthy looking and she\xe2\x80\x99s got a bright color.\r\nThe glummest, ill-natured little thing she used to be and now her and\r\nMaster Colin laugh together like a pair of crazy young ones. Perhaps\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re growing fat on that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps they are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Craven. \xe2\x80\x9cLet them laugh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV.\r\nTHE CURTAIN\r\n\r\n\r\nAnd the secret garden bloomed and bloomed and every morning revealed\r\nnew miracles. In the robin\xe2\x80\x99s nest there were Eggs and the robin\xe2\x80\x99s mate\r\nsat upon them keeping them warm with her feathery little breast and\r\ncareful wings. At first she was very nervous and the robin himself was\r\nindignantly watchful. Even Dickon did not go near the close-grown\r\ncorner in those days, but waited until by the quiet working of some\r\nmysterious spell he seemed to have conveyed to the soul of the little\r\npair that in the garden there was nothing which was not quite like\r\nthemselves\xe2\x80\x94nothing which did not understand the wonderfulness of what\r\nwas happening to them\xe2\x80\x94the immense, tender, terrible, heart-breaking\r\nbeauty and solemnity of Eggs. If there had been one person in that\r\ngarden who had not known through all his or her innermost being that if\r\nan Egg were taken away or hurt the whole world would whirl round and\r\ncrash through space and come to an end\xe2\x80\x94if there had been even one who\r\ndid not feel it and act accordingly there could have been no happiness\r\neven in that golden springtime air. But they all knew it and felt it\r\nand the robin and his mate knew they knew it.\r\n\r\nAt first the robin watched Mary and Colin with sharp anxiety. For some\r\nmysterious reason he knew he need not watch Dickon. The first moment he\r\nset his dew-bright black eye on Dickon he knew he was not a stranger\r\nbut a sort of robin without beak or feathers. He could speak robin\r\n(which is a quite distinct language not to be mistaken for any other).\r\nTo speak robin to a robin is like speaking French to a Frenchman.\r\nDickon always spoke it to the robin himself, so the queer gibberish he\r\nused when he spoke to humans did not matter in the least. The robin\r\nthought he spoke this gibberish to them because they were not\r\nintelligent enough to understand feathered speech. His movements also\r\nwere robin. They never startled one by being sudden enough to seem\r\ndangerous or threatening. Any robin could understand Dickon, so his\r\npresence was not even disturbing.\r\n\r\nBut at the outset it seemed necessary to be on guard against the other\r\ntwo. In the first place the boy creature did not come into the garden\r\non his legs. He was pushed in on a thing with wheels and the skins of\r\nwild animals were thrown over him. That in itself was doubtful. Then\r\nwhen he began to stand up and move about he did it in a queer\r\nunaccustomed way and the others seemed to have to help him. The robin\r\nused to secrete himself in a bush and watch this anxiously, his head\r\ntilted first on one side and then on the other. He thought that the\r\nslow movements might mean that he was preparing to pounce, as cats do.\r\nWhen cats are preparing to pounce they creep over the ground very\r\nslowly. The robin talked this over with his mate a great deal for a few\r\ndays but after that he decided not to speak of the subject because her\r\nterror was so great that he was afraid it might be injurious to the\r\nEggs.\r\n\r\nWhen the boy began to walk by himself and even to move more quickly it\r\nwas an immense relief. But for a long time\xe2\x80\x94or it seemed a long time to\r\nthe robin\xe2\x80\x94he was a source of some anxiety. He did not act as the other\r\nhumans did. He seemed very fond of walking but he had a way of sitting\r\nor lying down for a while and then getting up in a disconcerting manner\r\nto begin again.\r\n\r\nOne day the robin remembered that when he himself had been made to\r\nlearn to fly by his parents he had done much the same sort of thing. He\r\nhad taken short flights of a few yards and then had been obliged to\r\nrest. So it occurred to him that this boy was learning to fly\xe2\x80\x94or rather\r\nto walk. He mentioned this to his mate and when he told her that the\r\nEggs would probably conduct themselves in the same way after they were\r\nfledged she was quite comforted and even became eagerly interested and\r\nderived great pleasure from watching the boy over the edge of her\r\nnest\xe2\x80\x94though she always thought that the Eggs would be much cleverer and\r\nlearn more quickly. But then she said indulgently that humans were\r\nalways more clumsy and slow than Eggs and most of them never seemed\r\nreally to learn to fly at all. You never met them in the air or on\r\ntree-tops.\r\n\r\nAfter a while the boy began to move about as the others did, but all\r\nthree of the children at times did unusual things. They would stand\r\nunder the trees and move their arms and legs and heads about in a way\r\nwhich was neither walking nor running nor sitting down. They went\r\nthrough these movements at intervals every day and the robin was never\r\nable to explain to his mate what they were doing or tying to do. He\r\ncould only say that he was sure that the Eggs would never flap about in\r\nsuch a manner; but as the boy who could speak robin so fluently was\r\ndoing the thing with them, birds could be quite sure that the actions\r\nwere not of a dangerous nature. Of course neither the robin nor his\r\nmate had ever heard of the champion wrestler, Bob Haworth, and his\r\nexercises for making the muscles stand out like lumps. Robins are not\r\nlike human beings; their muscles are always exercised from the first\r\nand so they develop themselves in a natural manner. If you have to fly\r\nabout to find every meal you eat, your muscles do not become atrophied\r\n(atrophied means wasted away through want of use).\r\n\r\nWhen the boy was walking and running about and digging and weeding like\r\nthe others, the nest in the corner was brooded over by a great peace\r\nand content. Fears for the Eggs became things of the past. Knowing that\r\nyour Eggs were as safe as if they were locked in a bank vault and the\r\nfact that you could watch so many curious things going on made setting\r\na most entertaining occupation. On wet days the Eggs\xe2\x80\x99 mother sometimes\r\nfelt even a little dull because the children did not come into the\r\ngarden.\r\n\r\nBut even on wet days it could not be said that Mary and Colin were\r\ndull. One morning when the rain streamed down unceasingly and Colin was\r\nbeginning to feel a little restive, as he was obliged to remain on his\r\nsofa because it was not safe to get up and walk about, Mary had an\r\ninspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that I am a real boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin had said, \xe2\x80\x9cmy legs and arms and all\r\nmy body are so full of Magic that I can\xe2\x80\x99t keep them still. They want to\r\nbe doing things all the time. Do you know that when I waken in the\r\nmorning, Mary, when it\xe2\x80\x99s quite early and the birds are just shouting\r\noutside and everything seems just shouting for joy\xe2\x80\x94even the trees and\r\nthings we can\xe2\x80\x99t really hear\xe2\x80\x94I feel as if I must jump out of bed and\r\nshout myself. If I did it, just think what would happen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary giggled inordinately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe nurse would come running and Mrs. Medlock would come running and\r\nthey would be sure you had gone crazy and they\xe2\x80\x99d send for the doctor,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said.\r\n\r\nColin giggled himself. He could see how they would all look\xe2\x80\x94how\r\nhorrified by his outbreak and how amazed to see him standing upright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish my father would come home,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to tell him\r\nmyself. I\xe2\x80\x99m always thinking about it\xe2\x80\x94but we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go on like this\r\nmuch longer. I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand lying still and pretending, and besides I\r\nlook too different. I wish it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t raining today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was then Mistress Mary had her inspiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cColin,\xe2\x80\x9d she began mysteriously, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you know how many rooms there are\r\nin this house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout a thousand, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s about a hundred no one ever goes into,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd one\r\nrainy day I went and looked into ever so many of them. No one ever\r\nknew, though Mrs. Medlock nearly found me out. I lost my way when I was\r\ncoming back and I stopped at the end of your corridor. That was the\r\nsecond time I heard you crying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin started up on his sofa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA hundred rooms no one goes into,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds almost like a\r\nsecret garden. Suppose we go and look at them. Wheel me in my chair and\r\nnobody would know we went.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I was thinking,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary. \xe2\x80\x9cNo one would dare to follow\r\nus. There are galleries where you could run. We could do our exercises.\r\nThere is a little Indian room where there is a cabinet full of ivory\r\nelephants. There are all sorts of rooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRing the bell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin.\r\n\r\nWhen the nurse came in he gave his orders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want my chair,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Mary and I are going to look at the\r\npart of the house which is not used. John can push me as far as the\r\npicture-gallery because there are some stairs. Then he must go away and\r\nleave us alone until I send for him again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRainy days lost their terrors that morning. When the footman had\r\nwheeled the chair into the picture-gallery and left the two together in\r\nobedience to orders, Colin and Mary looked at each other delighted. As\r\nsoon as Mary had made sure that John was really on his way back to his\r\nown quarters below stairs, Colin got out of his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to run from one end of the gallery to the other,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand then I am going to jump and then we will do Bob Haworth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexercises.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they did all these things and many others. They looked at the\r\nportraits and found the plain little girl dressed in green brocade and\r\nholding the parrot on her finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll these,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, \xe2\x80\x9cmust be my relations. They lived a long time\r\nago. That parrot one, I believe, is one of my great, great, great,\r\ngreat aunts. She looks rather like you, Mary\xe2\x80\x94not as you look now but as\r\nyou looked when you came here. Now you are a great deal fatter and\r\nbetter looking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo are you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, and they both laughed.\r\n\r\nThey went to the Indian room and amused themselves with the ivory\r\nelephants. They found the rose-colored brocade boudoir and the hole in\r\nthe cushion the mouse had left, but the mice had grown up and run away\r\nand the hole was empty. They saw more rooms and made more discoveries\r\nthan Mary had made on her first pilgrimage. They found new corridors\r\nand corners and flights of steps and new old pictures they liked and\r\nweird old things they did not know the use of. It was a curiously\r\nentertaining morning and the feeling of wandering about in the same\r\nhouse with other people but at the same time feeling as if one were\r\nmiles away from them was a fascinating thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad we came,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin said. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knew I lived in such a big\r\nqueer old place. I like it. We will ramble about every rainy day. We\r\nshall always be finding new queer corners and things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat morning they had found among other things such good appetites that\r\nwhen they returned to Colin\xe2\x80\x99s room it was not possible to send the\r\nluncheon away untouched.\r\n\r\nWhen the nurse carried the tray downstairs she slapped it down on the\r\nkitchen dresser so that Mrs. Loomis, the cook, could see the highly\r\npolished dishes and plates.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at that!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a house of mystery, and those two\r\nchildren are the greatest mysteries in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they keep that up every day,\xe2\x80\x9d said the strong young footman John,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99d be small wonder that he weighs twice as much today as he did a\r\nmonth ago. I should have to give up my place in time, for fear of doing\r\nmy muscles an injury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat afternoon Mary noticed that something new had happened in Colin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom. She had noticed it the day before but had said nothing because\r\nshe thought the change might have been made by chance. She said nothing\r\ntoday but she sat and looked fixedly at the picture over the mantel.\r\nShe could look at it because the curtain had been drawn aside. That was\r\nthe change she noticed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you want me to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, after she had stared\r\na few minutes. \xe2\x80\x9cI always know when you want me to tell you something.\r\nYou are wondering why the curtain is drawn back. I am going to keep it\r\nlike that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make me angry any more to see her laughing. I\r\nwakened when it was bright moonlight two nights ago and felt as if the\r\nMagic was filling the room and making everything so splendid that I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t lie still. I got up and looked out of the window. The room was\r\nquite light and there was a patch of moonlight on the curtain and\r\nsomehow that made me go and pull the cord. She looked right down at me\r\nas if she were laughing because she was glad I was standing there. It\r\nmade me like to look at her. I want to see her laughing like that all\r\nthe time. I think she must have been a sort of Magic person perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are so like her now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cthat sometimes I think perhaps\r\nyou are her ghost made into a boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat idea seemed to impress Colin. He thought it over and then answered\r\nher slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were her ghost\xe2\x80\x94my father would be fond of me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want him to be fond of you?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Mary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI used to hate it because he was not fond of me. If he grew fond of me\r\nI think I should tell him about the Magic. It might make him more\r\ncheerful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIT\xe2\x80\x99S MOTHER!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nTheir belief in the Magic was an abiding thing. After the morning\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nincantations Colin sometimes gave them Magic lectures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like to do it,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause when I grow up and make great\r\nscientific discoveries I shall be obliged to lecture about them and so\r\nthis is practise. I can only give short lectures now because I am very\r\nyoung, and besides Ben Weatherstaff would feel as if he were in church\r\nand he would go to sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 best thing about lecturin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben, \xe2\x80\x9cis that a chap can get up\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 say aught he pleases an\xe2\x80\x99 no other chap can answer him back. I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be agen\xe2\x80\x99 lecturin\xe2\x80\x99 a bit mysel\xe2\x80\x99 sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut when Colin held forth under his tree old Ben fixed devouring eyes\r\non him and kept them there. He looked him over with critical affection.\r\nIt was not so much the lecture which interested him as the legs which\r\nlooked straighter and stronger each day, the boyish head which held\r\nitself up so well, the once sharp chin and hollow cheeks which had\r\nfilled and rounded out and the eyes which had begun to hold the light\r\nhe remembered in another pair. Sometimes when Colin felt Ben\xe2\x80\x99s earnest\r\ngaze meant that he was much impressed he wondered what he was\r\nreflecting on and once when he had seemed quite entranced he questioned\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you thinking about, Ben Weatherstaff?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinkin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d answered Ben, \xe2\x80\x9cas I\xe2\x80\x99d warrant tha\xe2\x80\x99s gone up three or\r\nfour pound this week. I was lookin\xe2\x80\x99 at tha\xe2\x80\x99 calves an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 shoulders.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d like to get thee on a pair o\xe2\x80\x99 scales.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the Magic and\xe2\x80\x94and Mrs. Sowerby\xe2\x80\x99s buns and milk and things,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nColin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see the scientific experiment has succeeded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat morning Dickon was too late to hear the lecture. When he came he\r\nwas ruddy with running and his funny face looked more twinkling than\r\nusual. As they had a good deal of weeding to do after the rains they\r\nfell to work. They always had plenty to do after a warm deep sinking\r\nrain. The moisture which was good for the flowers was also good for the\r\nweeds which thrust up tiny blades of grass and points of leaves which\r\nmust be pulled up before their roots took too firm hold. Colin was as\r\ngood at weeding as anyone in these days and he could lecture while he\r\nwas doing it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Magic works best when you work, yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d he said this morning.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can feel it in your bones and muscles. I am going to read books\r\nabout bones and muscles, but I am going to write a book about Magic. I\r\nam making it up now. I keep finding out things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was not very long after he had said this that he laid down his\r\ntrowel and stood up on his feet. He had been silent for several minutes\r\nand they had seen that he was thinking out lectures, as he often did.\r\nWhen he dropped his trowel and stood upright it seemed to Mary and\r\nDickon as if a sudden strong thought had made him do it. He stretched\r\nhimself out to his tallest height and he threw out his arms exultantly.\r\nColor glowed in his face and his strange eyes widened with joyfulness.\r\nAll at once he had realized something to the full.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMary! Dickon!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cJust look at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stopped their weeding and looked at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember that first morning you brought me in here?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ndemanded.\r\n\r\nDickon was looking at him very hard. Being an animal charmer he could\r\nsee more things than most people could and many of them were things he\r\nnever talked about. He saw some of them now in this boy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that we do,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\r\nMary looked hard too, but she said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust this minute,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, \xe2\x80\x9call at once I remembered it\r\nmyself\xe2\x80\x94when I looked at my hand digging with the trowel\xe2\x80\x94and I had to\r\nstand up on my feet to see if it was real. And it is real! I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n_well_\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m _well!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that th\xe2\x80\x99 art!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dickon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m well! I\xe2\x80\x99m well!\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin again, and his face went quite red all\r\nover.\r\n\r\nHe had known it before in a way, he had hoped it and felt it and\r\nthought about it, but just at that minute something had rushed all\r\nthrough him\xe2\x80\x94a sort of rapturous belief and realization and it had been\r\nso strong that he could not help calling out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall live forever and ever and ever!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried grandly. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nfind out thousands and thousands of things. I shall find out about\r\npeople and creatures and everything that grows\xe2\x80\x94like Dickon\xe2\x80\x94and I shall\r\nnever stop making Magic. I\xe2\x80\x99m well! I\xe2\x80\x99m well! I feel\xe2\x80\x94I feel as if I want\r\nto shout out something\xe2\x80\x94something thankful, joyful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBen Weatherstaff, who had been working near a rose-bush, glanced round\r\nat him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 might sing th\xe2\x80\x99 Doxology,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested in his dryest grunt. He\r\nhad no opinion of the Doxology and he did not make the suggestion with\r\nany particular reverence.\r\n\r\nBut Colin was of an exploring mind and he knew nothing about the\r\nDoxology.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDickon can sing it for thee, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ben Weatherstaff.\r\n\r\nDickon answered with his all-perceiving animal charmer\xe2\x80\x99s smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey sing it i\xe2\x80\x99 church,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cMother says she believes th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nskylarks sings it when they gets up i\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 mornin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she says that, it must be a nice song,\xe2\x80\x9d Colin answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never\r\nbeen in a church myself. I was always too ill. Sing it, Dickon. I want\r\nto hear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDickon was quite simple and unaffected about it. He understood what\r\nColin felt better than Colin did himself. He understood by a sort of\r\ninstinct so natural that he did not know it was understanding. He\r\npulled off his cap and looked round still smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTha\xe2\x80\x99 must take off tha\xe2\x80\x99 cap,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Colin, \xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 so mun tha\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nBen\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 tha\xe2\x80\x99 mun stand up, tha\xe2\x80\x99 knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin took off his cap and the sun shone on and warmed his thick hair\r\nas he watched Dickon intently. Ben Weatherstaff scrambled up from his\r\nknees and bared his head too with a sort of puzzled half-resentful look\r\non his old face as if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know exactly why he was doing this\r\nremarkable thing.\r\n\r\nDickon stood out among the trees and rose-bushes and began to sing in\r\nquite a simple matter-of-fact way and in a nice strong boy voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPraise God from whom all blessings flow,\r\nPraise Him all creatures here below,\r\nPraise Him above ye Heavenly Host,\r\nPraise Father, Son, and Holy Ghost.\r\n            Amen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he had finished, Ben Weatherstaff was standing quite still with\r\nhis jaws set obstinately but with a disturbed look in his eyes fixed on\r\nColin. Colin\xe2\x80\x99s face was thoughtful and appreciative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a very nice song,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI like it. Perhaps it means just\r\nwhat I mean when I want to shout out that I am thankful to the Magic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe stopped and thought in a puzzled way. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps they are both the\r\nsame thing. How can we know the exact names of everything? Sing it\r\nagain, Dickon. Let us try, Mary. I want to sing it, too. It\xe2\x80\x99s my song.\r\nHow does it begin? \xe2\x80\x98Praise God from whom all blessings flow\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they sang it again, and Mary and Colin lifted their voices as\r\nmusically as they could and Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s swelled quite loud and\r\nbeautiful\xe2\x80\x94and at the second line Ben Weatherstaff raspingly cleared his\r\nthroat and at the third line he joined in with such vigor that it\r\nseemed almost savage and when the \xe2\x80\x9cAmen\xe2\x80\x9d came to an end Mary observed\r\nthat the very same thing had happened to him which had happened when he\r\nfound out that Colin was not a cripple\xe2\x80\x94his chin was twitching and he\r\nwas staring and winking and his leathery old cheeks were wet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never seed no sense in th\xe2\x80\x99 Doxology afore,\xe2\x80\x9d he said hoarsely, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\r\nmay change my mind i\xe2\x80\x99 time. I should say tha\xe2\x80\x99d gone up five pound this\r\nweek Mester Colin\xe2\x80\x94five on \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin was looking across the garden at something attracting his\r\nattention and his expression had become a startled one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is coming in here?\xe2\x80\x9d he said quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door in the ivied wall had been pushed gently open and a woman had\r\nentered. She had come in with the last line of their song and she had\r\nstood still listening and looking at them. With the ivy behind her, the\r\nsunlight drifting through the trees and dappling her long blue cloak,\r\nand her nice fresh face smiling across the greenery she was rather like\r\na softly colored illustration in one of Colin\xe2\x80\x99s books. She had\r\nwonderful affectionate eyes which seemed to take everything in\xe2\x80\x94all of\r\nthem, even Ben Weatherstaff and the \xe2\x80\x9ccreatures\xe2\x80\x9d and every flower that\r\nwas in bloom. Unexpectedly as she had appeared, not one of them felt\r\nthat she was an intruder at all. Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s eyes lighted like lamps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s mother\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s who it is!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried and went across the grass at a\r\nrun.\r\n\r\nColin began to move toward her, too, and Mary went with him. They both\r\nfelt their pulses beat faster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s mother!\xe2\x80\x9d Dickon said again when they met halfway. \xe2\x80\x9cI knowed tha\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwanted to see her an\xe2\x80\x99 I told her where th\xe2\x80\x99 door was hid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColin held out his hand with a sort of flushed royal shyness but his\r\neyes quite devoured her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven when I was ill I wanted to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou and Dickon and\r\nthe secret garden. I\xe2\x80\x99d never wanted to see anyone or anything before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sight of his uplifted face brought about a sudden change in her\r\nown. She flushed and the corners of her mouth shook and a mist seemed\r\nto sweep over her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! dear lad!\xe2\x80\x9d she broke out tremulously. \xe2\x80\x9cEh! dear lad!\xe2\x80\x9d as if she\r\nhad not known she were going to say it. She did not say, \xe2\x80\x9cMester\r\nColin,\xe2\x80\x9d but just \xe2\x80\x9cdear lad\xe2\x80\x9d quite suddenly. She might have said it to\r\nDickon in the same way if she had seen something in his face which\r\ntouched her. Colin liked it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you surprised because I am so well?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\nShe put her hand on his shoulder and smiled the mist out of her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, that I am!\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut tha\xe2\x80\x99rt so like thy mother tha\xe2\x80\x99 made my\r\nheart jump.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin a little awkwardly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat will make my\r\nfather like me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, for sure, dear lad,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered and she gave his shoulder a\r\nsoft quick pat. \xe2\x80\x9cHe mun come home\xe2\x80\x94he mun come home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSusan Sowerby,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ben Weatherstaff, getting close to her. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 lad\xe2\x80\x99s legs, wilt tha\xe2\x80\x99? They was like drumsticks i\xe2\x80\x99 stockin\xe2\x80\x99 two\r\nmonth\xe2\x80\x99 ago\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 I heard folk tell as they was bandy an\xe2\x80\x99 knock-kneed both\r\nat th\xe2\x80\x99 same time. I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant tha\xe2\x80\x99rt like thy mother too. Our Martha\r\ntold me as Mrs. Medlock heard she was a pretty woman. Tha\xe2\x80\x99lt be like a\r\nblush rose when tha\xe2\x80\x99 grows up, my little lass, bless thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not mention that when Martha came home on her \xe2\x80\x9cday out\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\ndescribed the plain sallow child she had said that she had no\r\nconfidence whatever in what Mrs. Medlock had heard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t stand\r\nto reason that a pretty woman could be th\xe2\x80\x99 mother o\xe2\x80\x99 such a fou\xe2\x80\x99 little\r\nlass,\xe2\x80\x9d she had added obstinately.\r\n\r\nMary had not had time to pay much attention to her changing face. She\r\nhad only known that she looked \xe2\x80\x9cdifferent\xe2\x80\x9d and seemed to have a great\r\ndeal more hair and that it was growing very fast. But remembering her\r\npleasure in looking at the Mem Sahib in the past she was glad to hear\r\nthat she might some day look like her.\r\n\r\nSusan Sowerby went round their garden with them and was told the whole\r\nstory of it and shown every bush and tree which had come alive. Colin\r\nwalked on one side of her and Mary on the other. Each of them kept\r\nlooking up at her comfortable rosy face, secretly curious about the\r\ndelightful feeling she gave them\xe2\x80\x94a sort of warm, supported feeling. It\r\nseemed as if she understood them as Dickon understood his \xe2\x80\x9ccreatures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe stooped over the flowers and talked about them as if they were\r\nchildren. Soot followed her and once or twice cawed at her and flew\r\nupon her shoulder as if it were Dickon\xe2\x80\x99s. When they told her about the\r\nrobin and the first flight of the young ones she laughed a motherly\r\nlittle mellow laugh in her throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose learnin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em to fly is like learnin\xe2\x80\x99 children to walk, but\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m feared I should be all in a worrit if mine had wings instead o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlegs,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nIt was because she seemed such a wonderful woman in her nice moorland\r\ncottage way that at last she was told about the Magic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you believe in Magic?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Colin after he had explained about\r\nIndian fakirs. \xe2\x80\x9cI do hope you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I do, lad,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI never knowed it by that name but\r\nwhat does th\xe2\x80\x99 name matter? I warrant they call it a different name i\xe2\x80\x99\r\nFrance an\xe2\x80\x99 a different one i\xe2\x80\x99 Germany. Th\xe2\x80\x99 same thing as set th\xe2\x80\x99 seeds\r\nswellin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 sun shinin\xe2\x80\x99 made thee a well lad an\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 Good\r\nThing. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t like us poor fools as think it matters if us is called\r\nout of our names. Th\xe2\x80\x99 Big Good Thing doesn\xe2\x80\x99t stop to worrit, bless\r\nthee. It goes on makin\xe2\x80\x99 worlds by th\xe2\x80\x99 million\xe2\x80\x94worlds like us. Tha\xe2\x80\x99 wert singin\xe2\x80\x99 to it when I come\r\ninto th\xe2\x80\x99 garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI felt so joyful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Colin, opening his beautiful strange eyes at\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cSuddenly I felt how different I was\xe2\x80\x94how strong my arms and legs\r\nwere, you know\xe2\x80\x94and how I could dig and stand\xe2\x80\x94and I jumped up and wanted\r\nto shout out something to anything that would listen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTh\xe2\x80\x99 Magic listened when tha\xe2\x80\x99 sung th\xe2\x80\x99 Doxology.
